<abstract>### 子章节 3.1：环形几何与磁拓扑参数化
本子章节将以“为何需要用一组专门的坐标与参数来描述环形磁约束装置中的几何与磁场”为切入点，从第2章已经建立的磁场、力学与粒子运动的基本方程出发，先把读者的视角从“局部近似/直线磁场”切换到“环形几何中的全局拓扑约束”，并明确后续平衡求解与线圈接口所依赖的最小几何语言。叙述顺序上先搭建环形装置的几何框架与坐标选择原则，再将磁场分解到环向与极向方向以形成可操作的参数化表达，随后引入“磁面—嵌套磁通量面”的组织方式作为所有剖面量与平衡变量的承载结构，完成从空间几何到拓扑对象的过渡；在此基础上再把场线缠绕的定量表征收束到旋转变换与安全因子，并用其径向变化组织磁剪切的叙事，从而把“几何—拓扑—参数”的链条闭合为可用于计算与设计的接口语言。与后续章节的衔接将以“这些参数如何成为2D轴对称平衡（3.2）与3D平衡（3.3）的共同输入/输出变量”为落点，同时为更下游的稳定性（第5章以q与剪切作为关键控制旋钮）、新经典输运与捕获粒子几何（第6章以磁面与B的环向/极向结构为前提）、波传播与模结构（第7章以并行波数与q剪切关联为入口）预留统一的符号体系与认知接口，确保全章在“位形选择—平衡求解—线圈耦合”主线中具备同一套可复用的几何与拓扑参数化语言。

### 子章节 3.2：托卡马克平衡与形状自由度
本节将承接3.1已建立的环形几何与磁拓扑参数化，把“磁面如何存在”进一步推进到“磁面如何在力学上自洽”，先以托卡马克位形与运行原理作为问题框架，明确为何轴对称与环向电流使平衡成为可计算的约束条件，从而自然引入环向平衡与力平衡这一主线；随后把力平衡从定性约束收敛到可求解的数学对象，组织推导与表述Grad–Shafranov方程的变量选择、边界条件类型与解的自由度来源，使读者形成“剖面函数—磁通函数—形状”的因果链条；在此基础上引入索洛维耶夫解析平衡解作为教学中的可控参照系，用它来承载“如何从方程到形状、从参数到几何特征”的叙事转换，进而把等离子体位形的形状自由度按工程可调的主参数序列展开，依次落到拉长率、三角形变与Shafranov位移，并将这些形状量放回到平衡方程的源项与边界响应中形成闭环；最后以“平衡—线圈接口”的视角收束，为3.4的自由边界平衡与等离子体-线圈耦合预留计算接口，同时在结尾显式提醒这些形状自由度将如何在第5章稳定性边界（如垂直稳定与压力驱动极限）中被重新调用，从而完成本节在全章中作为“从拓扑参数化到可控平衡解”的桥梁角色。

### 子章节 3.3：三维平衡与仿星器优化接口
本节将从上一节3.2的轴对称Grad–Shafranov平衡自然过渡到“失去连续对称性后平衡如何被定义与计算”这一核心张力：先用最少的符号约定把三维MHD平衡在“嵌套磁通面—通量函数—约束条件”框架内重新立起，从而让读者理解为何三维情形不再是二维方程的直接推广，而是一类受几何与拓扑共同约束的求解问题；随后叙事主干转向“求解器—指标—优化”的工程闭环，先把三维平衡求解器作为连接“目标物理量（输运/稳定/电流）”与“可制造线圈/边界形状”的接口来组织材料，再把准对称性与全同轨道性作为优化中最常被显式编码的约束类型引入，使读者形成“通过几何谱控制新经典耗散与动量损失，并将其等效回写到降维模型”的思维路径；在此基础上安排一段专门的“无力平衡与泰勒态”作为对照轴：它既提供三维下另一类更强约束的极限平衡观，也为后续讨论三维拓扑破缺时的能量—螺度权衡提供统一语言；最后以“磁岛—重叠—随机性”收束，将读者带到三维平衡最容易失效的边界情形，并把“从平衡到输运”的接口意识前置：哪些效应应当进入本章的位形选择与线圈接口，哪些应当留给第5章的重联链路与第6章/第10章的随机磁场输运与集成建模去展开。与后续3.4的衔接将明确落在“固定边界三维平衡如何升级为自由边界、并与线圈几何及诊断重建形成可迭代收敛环”，从而为全章建立从位形参数化到可实现线圈的统一认知接口。

### 子章节 3.4：自由边界平衡与等离子体-线圈耦合
本子章节将从第3章前面已建立的固定边界/给定线圈电流下的平衡求解接口出发，先把“自由边界”作为工程闭环问题引入：等离子体边界不再由计算域预先规定，而是由线圈、电流约束与边界条件共同决定，从而自然过渡到“等离子体—线圈耦合”这一系统级视角，并明确本节的目标是把平衡从“几何求解”提升为“电磁回路—约束—诊断—结构响应”的一致性求解。叙事主干将按“建模对象→求解器结构→观测与反演→导体响应→控制/集成接口”的顺序推进：先限定自由边界平衡所需的最小变量集合与边界条件组织方式，进而说明求解器在数值上如何把外部线圈、等离子体电流与约束条件组合成可迭代收敛的耦合系统，并把形状自由度与稳定性敏感方向（为第5章垂直稳定性与控制问题）预埋为后续动力学分析的接口；随后把磁诊断引入为“从可测量量到平衡状态”的桥梁，围绕平衡重建的证据链组织材料，使其既能回连本章的平衡求解框架，又能为第9章诊断与控制的系统化讨论建立统一的参数化与误差传递入口；最后将导电结构中的涡流建模作为从“真空磁场”到“装置真实电磁环境”的补全环节，强调其对自由边界求解、磁诊断解释与控制回路有效性的共同影响，从而在章节收束时把本节产出的关键接口（线圈/结构电路模型、诊断前向模型、自由边界约束与误差表征）显式对接到第5章的稳定性边界与第10章的集成建模链路，保证读者在后续学习中能够用同一套状态变量与耦合关系贯通“平衡—诊断—控制—工程结构响应”的闭环叙事。

</abstract>

<wiki_content3.1>title: [calculation] Toroidal geometry, magnetic shear, and mode structure

Question: Consider a large-aspect-ratio tokamak with major radius $R$ and minor radius $r$, supporting Ion Cyclotron Resonance Heating (ICRH) with a radio-frequency fast magnetosonic wave (FW) of angular frequency $\omega$ launched from the low-field side. Assume axisymmetric toroidal geometry and a safety factor $q(r)$ whose local behavior near a particular radius $r=r_{\ast}$ is linear, $q(r) = q_{\ast} + q^{\prime}(r_{\ast})\,(r-r_{\ast})$, where $q^{\prime}(r_{\ast}) = \left.\frac{dq}{dr}\right|_{r_{\ast}}$. The FW has toroidal mode number $n$ and is dominated by a single poloidal harmonic with poloidal mode number $m_{0}$ so that the parallel wavenumber is $k_{\parallel}(r) = \frac{m_{0} - n\,q(r)}{R}$. Near $r=r_{\ast}$, the FW undergoes mode conversion to the Ion Bernstein Wave (IBW) at the Ion-Ion Hybrid (IIH) resonance, where the two branches cross in wavenumber at $k_{\parallel}(r_{\ast})=k_{\ast}$.

Assume that the local cold-plasma wave operator admits two coupled eigenmode envelopes $A(r)$ (FW) and $B(r)$ (IBW) whose evolution in the inhomogeneous plasma is described by first-order coupled mode equations in the radial coordinate $r$,
$$
\frac{dA}{dr} = i\,\Delta(r)\,A + i\,\kappa\,B, \qquad
\frac{dB}{dr} = i\,\kappa\,A - i\,\Delta(r)\,B,
$$
where the detuning $\Delta(r)$ represents the local phase mismatch between the two branches and $\kappa$ is a real, positive, constant coupling coefficient arising from the off-diagonal elements of the anisotropic dielectric associated with toroidal geometry and finite Larmor radius effects. In the vicinity of $r_{\ast}$, suppose $\Delta(r)$ may be linearized as $\Delta(r) \approx \alpha\,(r-r_{\ast})$, with $\alpha = \left.\frac{d\Delta}{dr}\right|_{r_{\ast}}$ determined by the magnetic shear through the $r$-dependence of $k_{\parallel}(r)$.

Starting from the fundamental cold-plasma wave equation and the slowly varying envelope approximation, derive from first principles the expression for the fraction $S_{\mathrm{FW}}$ of FW power that remains on the FW branch after traversing the localized mode-conversion region. Express $S_{\mathrm{FW}}$ solely in terms of $\kappa$ and $\alpha$. Then, using toroidal geometry, relate $\alpha$ to the safety factor gradient $q^{\prime}(r_{\ast})$, $n$, and $R$. Finally, evaluate $S_{\mathrm{FW}}$ numerically for the following parameters (assume large-aspect-ratio and ignore finite orbit width effects): $R = 3.2\,\text{m}$, $n = 18$, $q^{\prime}(r_{\ast}) = 0.7\,\text{m}^{-1}$, and $\kappa = 0.30\,\text{m}^{-1}$. Round your final result to four significant figures and express it as a dimensionless decimal.

Answer: [{"answer": "$$\\boxed{0.9307}$$", "solution": "The starting point is the frequency-domain Maxwell system for a cold, magnetized plasma,\n$$\n\\nabla \\times \\left( \\nabla \\times \\mathbf{E} \\right) - \\frac{\\omega^{2}}{c^{2}}\\,\\boldsymbol{\\varepsilon}(\\mathbf{x},\\omega) \\cdot \\mathbf{E} = \\mathbf{0},\n$$\nwhere $\\boldsymbol{\\varepsilon}$ is the cold-plasma dielectric tensor. In an inhomogeneous medium with slow spatial variation, a WKB expansion in the radial coordinate $r$ decomposes $\\mathbf{E}$ into local eigenmodes of the dispersion operator, and near an avoided crossing (here the IIH resonance), two branches interact dominantly. Projecting onto the local FW and IBW eigenvectors and retaining only the leading-order coupling yields coupled first-order envelope equations for the complex amplitudes $A(r)$ and $B(r)$. The generic form consistent with energy conservation and Hermitian coupling in the absence of damping is\n$$\n\\frac{dA}{dr} = i\\,\\Delta(r)\\,A + i\\,\\kappa\\,B, \\qquad\n\\frac{dB}{dr} = i\\,\\kappa\\,A - i\\,\\Delta(r)\\,B,\n$$\nwhere $\\Delta(r)$ is the local mismatch (detuning) between the two branches and $\\kappa$ is a real coupling coefficient determined by the off-diagonal elements of the dielectric in the chosen eigenbasis. This representation is standard in coupled-mode theory derived from the cold-plasma wave equation via biorthogonal eigenmode projection.\n\nClose to the crossing at $r=r_{\\ast}$, the detuning can be linearized:\n$$\n\\Delta(r) \\approx \\alpha\\,(r - r_{\\ast}), \\qquad \\alpha = \\left.\\frac{d\\Delta}{dr}\\right|_{r_{\\ast}}.\n$$\nThe system then maps to the canonical Landau-Zener two-level problem with coordinate $r$ playing the role of the evolution parameter. To see this, eliminate $B$ by differentiating the first equation and substituting the second:\n\\begin{align*}\n\\frac{d^{2}A}{dr^{2}} &= i\\,\\frac{d\\Delta}{dr}\\,A + i\\,\\Delta\\,\\frac{dA}{dr} + i\\,\\kappa\\,\\frac{dB}{dr} \\\\\n&= i\\,\\alpha\\,A + i\\,\\Delta \\left( i\\,\\Delta\\,A + i\\,\\kappa\\,B \\right) + i\\,\\kappa \\left( i\\,\\kappa\\,A - i\\,\\Delta\\,B \\right) \\\\\n&= i\\,\\alpha\\,A - \\Delta^{2}\\,A - \\kappa^{2}\\,A,\n\\end{align*}\nusing cancellation of the mixed $B$ terms. The second-order scalar equation is\n$$\n\\frac{d^{2}A}{dr^{2}} + \\left( \\kappa^{2} + \\Delta^{2}(r) - i\\,\\alpha \\right) A = 0.\n$$\nWith $\\Delta(r) = \\alpha (r - r_{\\ast})$, this is the parabolic cylinder equation after a linear change of variables and phase rotation. Asymptotic matching across the linear detuning region yields the Landau-Zener survival factor (the probability that the wave remains on its original branch, here the FW, after passing through the mode-conversion layer):\n$$\nS_{\\mathrm{FW}} = \\exp\\!\\left( -\\pi\\,\\frac{\\kappa^{2}}{|\\alpha|} \\right).\n$$\nThis result is a well-tested formula for linear level crossing in coupled-wave systems, derivable from the above differential equations via contour integration in the complex plane or via parabolic cylinder function connection formulas.\n\nNext, we relate $\\alpha$ to toroidal geometry and magnetic shear. The detuning $\\Delta(r)$ measures the mismatch in parallel wavenumber between the interacting branches. Taking $\\Delta(r) = k_{\\parallel}(r) - k_{\\ast}$ with $k_{\\ast}$ constant (the crossing value) gives\n$$\n\\alpha = \\left. \\frac{d\\Delta}{dr} \\right|_{r_{\\ast}} = \\left. \\frac{dk_{\\parallel}}{dr} \\right|_{r_{\\ast}}.\n$$\nFor a large-aspect-ratio tokamak, the parallel wavenumber associated with a mode of poloidal and toroidal integers $(m_{0},n)$ is\n$$\nk_{\\parallel}(r) = \\frac{m_{0} - n\\,q(r)}{R}.\n$$\nDifferentiating with respect to $r$ at $r_{\\ast}$ yields\n$$\n\\alpha = \\left. \\frac{dk_{\\parallel}}{dr} \\right|_{r_{\\ast}} = -\\,\\frac{n}{R}\\,q^{\\prime}(r_{\\ast}),\n$$\nand thus\n$$\n|\\alpha| = \\frac{n}{R}\\,|q^{\\prime}(r_{\\ast})|.\n$$\nTherefore, the survival fraction is\n$$\nS_{\\mathrm{FW}} = \\exp\\!\\left( -\\pi\\,\\frac{\\kappa^{2}}{(n/R)\\,|q^{\\prime}(r_{\\ast})|} \\right) = \\exp\\!\\left( -\\pi\\,\\frac{\\kappa^{2}\\,R}{n\\,|q^{\\prime}(r_{\\ast})|} \\right).\n$$\n\nWe now evaluate the numerical value using the provided parameters:\n$$\nR = 3.2\\,\\text{m}, \\quad n = 18, \\quad q^{\\prime}(r_{\\ast}) = 0.7\\,\\text{m}^{-1}, \\quad \\kappa = 0.30\\,\\text{m}^{-1}.\n$$\nCompute $|\\alpha|$:\n$$\n|\\alpha| = \\frac{n}{R}\\,|q^{\\prime}(r_{\\ast})| = \\frac{18}{3.2} \\times 0.7 = \\frac{12.6}{3.2} = 3.9375\\,\\text{m}^{-2}.\n$$\nCompute the dimensionless ratio:\n$$\n\\frac{\\kappa^{2}}{|\\alpha|} = \\frac{(0.30)^{2}}{3.9375} = \\frac{0.09}{3.9375} = 0.022857142857\\ldots\n$$\nThen\n$$\nS_{\\mathrm{FW}} = \\exp\\!\\left( -\\pi \\times 0.022857142857\\ldots \\right) = \\exp(-0.07183098862\\ldots).\n$$\nNumerically,\n$$\nS_{\\mathrm{FW}} \\approx 0.930672\\ldots\n$$\nRounded to four significant figures as instructed,\n$$\nS_{\\mathrm{FW}} = 0.9307.\n$$\nThis dimensionless decimal is the requested fraction of FW power that remains on the FW branch after traversing the mode-conversion region, showing the explicit dependence on toroidal geometry ($R$), mode structure ($n$), and magnetic shear ($q^{\\prime}$) through the derived expression."}, {"answer": "$$\n\\boxed{0.9307}\n$$", "solution": "The problem asks for the fraction of Fast Wave (FW) power, $S_{\\mathrm{FW}}$, that remains on the FW branch after traversing a mode-conversion region in a tokamak plasma. The process is modeled by a system of coupled first-order ordinary differential equations for the wave envelopes.\n\nThe provided system of equations is:\n$$\n\\frac{dA}{dr} = i\\,\\Delta(r)\\,A + i\\,\\kappa\\,B\n$$\n$$\n\\frac{dB}{dr} = i\\,\\kappa\\,A - i\\,\\Delta(r)\\,B\n$$\nHere, $A(r)$ is the complex envelope of the FW and $B(r)$ is that of the Ion Bernstein Wave (IBW). The coupling coefficient $\\kappa$ is a real positive constant. The detuning from resonance at $r=r_{\\ast}$ is given by the linear approximation $\\Delta(r) \\approx \\alpha\\,(r-r_{\\ast})$.\n\nThis system is a classic example of a spatial Landau-Zener problem, also known as the Budden problem in plasma physics. We are considering a wave that is purely a Fast Wave ($A=1$, $B=0$) far to one side of the conversion region (e.g., as $r \\to -\\infty$) and we wish to find the power remaining in the FW mode far on the other side (as $r \\to +\\infty$). The power in a wave is proportional to the squared magnitude of its envelope, so the incident power is proportional to $|A(-\\infty)|^2=1$ and the transmitted FW power is proportional to $|A(+\\infty)|^2$. The fraction of remaining FW power is therefore $S_{\\mathrm{FW}} = |A(+\\infty)|^2$.\n\nTo solve the system, one can combine the two first-order equations into a single second-order equation. Differentiating the first equation with respect to $r$ yields:\n$$\n\\frac{d^2A}{dr^2} = i\\,\\frac{d\\Delta}{dr}A + i\\,\\Delta\\frac{dA}{dr} + i\\,\\kappa\\frac{dB}{dr}\n$$\nUsing the approximation $\\Delta(r) = \\alpha(r-r_{\\ast})$, we have $\\frac{d\\Delta}{dr} = \\alpha$. Substituting expressions for $\\frac{dA}{dr}$ and $\\frac{dB}{dr}$ from the original system:\n$$\n\\frac{d^2A}{dr^2} = i\\,\\alpha\\,A + i\\,\\Delta\\left(i\\,\\Delta\\,A + i\\,\\kappa\\,B\\right) + i\\,\\kappa\\left(i\\,\\kappa\\,A - i\\,\\Delta\\,B\\right)\n$$\n$$\n\\frac{d^2A}{dr^2} = i\\,\\alpha\\,A - \\Delta^2\\,A - \\Delta\\,\\kappa\\,B - \\kappa^2\\,A + \\Delta\\,\\kappa\\,B\n$$\nThe terms involving $B$ cancel out, leaving a second-order equation for $A$ alone:\n$$\n\\frac{d^2A}{dr^2} - i\\,\\alpha\\,A + (\\Delta^2 + \\kappa^2)A = 0\n$$\nSubstituting $\\Delta(r) = \\alpha(r-r_{\\ast})$:\n$$\n\\frac{d^2A}{dr^2} + \\left(\\alpha^2(r-r_{\\ast})^2 + \\kappa^2 - i\\,\\alpha\\right)A = 0\n$$\nThis is a form of Weber's equation (or parabolic cylinder equation). The solution for the transmission coefficient (the fraction of power that remains in the initial mode after passing through the interaction region) is a standard result for this equation. The fraction of power converted to the other mode is $P_c = 1 - \\exp(-\\pi \\kappa^2 / |\\alpha|)$, and the fraction of power transmitted without conversion is:\n$$\nS_{\\mathrm{FW}} = \\exp\\left(-\\frac{\\pi\\,\\kappa^2}{|\\alpha|}\\right)\n$$\nThis is the first part of the required derivation.\n\nNext, we must relate the parameter $\\alpha$ to the physical quantities given in the problem statement. We are given that $\\alpha = \\left.\\frac{d\\Delta}{dr}\\right|_{r_{\\ast}}$ is determined by the magnetic shear through the $r$-dependence of the parallel wavenumber, $k_{\\parallel}(r)$. The detuning parameter $\\Delta(r)$ represents the local mismatch in wavenumber between the uncoupled modes, which vanishes at the resonance $r=r_{\\ast}$. The most direct interpretation consistent with the problem's framing is that the detuning $\\Delta(r)$ is proportional to the deviation of $k_{\\parallel}(r)$ from its resonant value, $k_{\\ast} = k_{\\parallel}(r_{\\ast})$. For dimensional consistency ($\\Delta$ must have units of inverse length, same as wavenumber), we can propose the simplest relationship:\n$$\n\\Delta(r) = k_{\\parallel}(r) - k_{\\ast}\n$$\nThe problem provides the expression for the parallel wavenumber:\n$$\nk_{\\parallel}(r) = \\frac{m_{0} - n\\,q(r)}{R}\n$$\nNear $r=r_{\\ast}$, the safety factor $q(r)$ is linearized as:\n$$\nq(r) \\approx q_{\\ast} + q^{\\prime}(r_{\\ast})\\,(r-r_{\\ast})\n$$\nwhere $q_{\\ast} = q(r_{\\ast})$. Substituting this into the expression for $k_{\\parallel}(r)$:\n$$\nk_{\\parallel}(r) \\approx \\frac{m_{0} - n\\,(q_{\\ast} + q^{\\prime}(r_{\\ast})\\,(r-r_{\\ast}))}{R}\n$$\n$$\nk_{\\parallel}(r) \\approx \\frac{m_{0} - n\\,q_{\\ast}}{R} - \\frac{n\\,q^{\\prime}(r_{\\ast})}{R}(r-r_{\\ast})\n$$\nSince $k_{\\ast} = k_{\\parallel}(r_{\\ast}) = \\frac{m_{0} - n\\,q_{\\ast}}{R}$, we have:\n$$\nk_{\\parallel}(r) - k_{\\ast} \\approx -\\frac{n\\,q^{\\prime}(r_{\\ast})}{R}(r-r_{\\ast})\n$$\nEquating this with our definition of $\\Delta(r)$:\n$$\n\\Delta(r) \\approx -\\frac{n\\,q^{\\prime}(r_{\\ast})}{R}(r-r_{\\ast})\n$$\nComparing this with the given form $\\Delta(r) \\approx \\alpha\\,(r-r_{\\ast})$, we can identify the parameter $\\alpha$:\n$$\n\\alpha = -\\frac{n\\,q^{\\prime}(r_{\\ast})}{R}\n$$\nThis establishes the required relationship between $\\alpha$ and the specified geometric and magnetic parameters. The absolute value is $|\\alpha| = \\frac{n\\,|q^{\\prime}(r_{\\ast})|}{R}$, since $n$ is a mode number (taken as positive) and $R$ is a radius (positive).\n\nNow, we substitute this expression for $|\\alpha|$ into the formula for $S_{\\mathrm{FW}}$:\n$$\nS_{\\mathrm{FW}} = \\exp\\left(-\\frac{\\pi\\,\\kappa^2 R}{n\\,|q^{\\prime}(r_{\\ast})|}\\right)\n$$\nFinally, we evaluate this expression using the provided numerical values:\n- Major radius $R = 3.2\\,\\text{m}$\n- Toroidal mode number $n = 18$\n- Safety factor gradient $q^{\\prime}(r_{\\ast}) = 0.7\\,\\text{m}^{-1}$\n- Coupling coefficient $\\kappa = 0.30\\,\\text{m}^{-1}$\n\nThe argument of the exponential is:\n$$\n-\\frac{\\pi\\,\\kappa^2 R}{n\\,|q^{\\prime}(r_{\\ast})|} = -\\frac{\\pi\\,(0.30\\,\\text{m}^{-1})^2 (3.2\\,\\text{m})}{18 \\times |0.7\\,\\text{m}^{-1}|}\n$$\n$$\n= -\\frac{\\pi \\times 0.09\\,\\text{m}^{-2} \\times 3.2\\,\\text{m}}{18 \\times 0.7\\,\\text{m}^{-1}}\n$$\n$$\n= -\\frac{\\pi \\times 0.288\\,\\text{m}^{-1}}{12.6\\,\\text{m}^{-1}} \\approx -0.0718109\n$$\nThe value of $S_{\\mathrm{FW}}$ is then:\n$$\nS_{\\mathrm{FW}} = \\exp(-0.0718109) \\approx 0.930704\n$$\nRounding to four significant figures, the fraction of FW power that remains on the FW branch is $0.9307$."}]


title: [multiple_choice] Define magnetic flux coordinates $(\psi,\theta,\phi)$ and state a large-aspect-ratio approximation for $B(\theta)$, discussing its validity for evaluating Pfirsch–Schlüter and plateau transport.

Question: Consider an axisymmetric tokamak with circular, concentric magnetic flux surfaces of minor radius $r$ and major radius $R_0$, with large aspect ratio $\varepsilon \equiv r/R_0 \ll 1$. Magnetic field lines lie on nested toroidal surfaces labeled by a single-valued flux coordinate, and the magnitude of the magnetic field varies poloidally due to the change in major radius along a flux surface. In magnetic flux coordinates $(\psi,\theta,\phi)$, $\psi$ labels flux surfaces, $\theta$ is a poloidal angle along the cross-section of a flux surface, and $\phi$ is the toroidal angle about the symmetry axis. Using only geometry and basic electromagnetic principles, select the option that most accurately:

- Defines the magnetic flux coordinates $(\psi,\theta,\phi)$ consistent with nested flux surfaces and field-line geometry in an axisymmetric tokamak;
- States a scientifically sound large-aspect-ratio approximation for the poloidal variation $B(\theta)$ of the magnetic-field magnitude to leading order in $\varepsilon$;
- Provides a justified assessment of the validity of that approximation for evaluating neoclassical transport in the Pfirsch–Schlüter regime (high collisionality, $\nu_* \gg 1$) and plateau regime (intermediate collisionality).

A. $\psi$ is the poloidal magnetic flux labeling nested flux surfaces so that $B \cdot \nabla \psi = 0$, $\theta$ is a right-handed poloidal angle increasing once around the minor cross-section, and $\phi$ is the toroidal symmetry angle. On a circular, concentric surface, the major radius is $R(\theta) = R_0 + r \cos\theta$, and the dominant toroidal field scales as $B(\theta) \simeq B_0 \, R_0 / R(\theta) = B_0/(1+\varepsilon\cos\theta)$, with first-order expansion $B(\theta) \simeq B_0\left(1-\varepsilon\cos\theta\right)$. This approximation is valid to leading order in $\varepsilon$ for estimating Pfirsch–Schlüter and plateau neoclassical transport coefficients in large-aspect-ratio, circular tokamaks; it may incur errors if strong shaping or finite aspect ratio introduces higher poloidal harmonics beyond $\mathcal{O}(\varepsilon)$.

B. $\psi$ is the toroidal magnetic flux, $\theta$ is the toroidal angle, and $\phi$ is the poloidal angle. The magnetic-field magnitude is poloidally uniform $B(\theta) \simeq B_0$, which is universally valid for all aspect ratios, so the Pfirsch–Schlüter and plateau transport are unaffected by poloidal variation of $B$.

C. $\psi$ labels flux surfaces such that $B \cdot \nabla \psi \neq 0$, $\theta$ is a poloidal angle, and $\phi$ is the toroidal angle. The large-aspect-ratio approximation is $B(\theta) \simeq B_0(1+\varepsilon\cos\theta)$, and this overestimates the trapped fraction, so it should be avoided for plateau transport but remains exact in the Pfirsch–Schlüter regime.

D. $\psi$ is the poloidal magnetic flux labeling flux surfaces, $\theta$ is the poloidal angle, and $\phi$ is the toroidal angle. The magnetic-field magnitude is approximated by $B(\theta) \simeq B_0\left(1-2\varepsilon\cos\theta\right)$, which is required to capture Pfirsch–Schlüter parallel compressibility and is invalid for plateau transport because trapped particles depend on higher-order harmonics that dominate at $\mathcal{O}(\varepsilon)$.

Answer: [{"answer": "$$\\boxed{A}$$", "solution": "The derivation begins from axisymmetry and basic electromagnetic and geometric principles. In an axisymmetric tokamak, magnetic field lines lie on nested toroidal surfaces, and a flux surface label $\\psi$ can be chosen proportional to the poloidal magnetic flux, ensuring $B \\cdot \\nabla \\psi = 0$ because $B$ is tangent to the surface. The poloidal angle $\\theta$ parameterizes position around the minor cross-section of the flux surface, and the toroidal angle $\\phi$ parameterizes position around the symmetry axis. These definitions are consistent with flux coordinates used in neoclassical theory, where the safety factor $q(\\psi)$ relates field-line advance via $d\\phi/d\\theta = q(\\psi)$ along a field line, though that relation is not required here.\n\nFor large aspect ratio $\\varepsilon \\equiv r/R_0 \\ll 1$ and circular, concentric flux surfaces, the major radius varies as\n$$\nR(\\theta) = R_0 + r \\cos\\theta = R_0\\left(1 + \\varepsilon \\cos\\theta\\right).\n$$\nThe toroidal magnetic field generated by toroidal coils in vacuum scales inversely with major radius due to Ampère’s law and axisymmetry, $B_\\phi(R) \\propto 1/R$. A common representation is\n$$\nB_\\phi(\\theta) = B_0 \\frac{R_0}{R(\\theta)} = \\frac{B_0}{1 + \\varepsilon \\cos\\theta}.\n$$\nBecause the poloidal component is smaller by $\\mathcal{O}(\\varepsilon/q)$ in magnitude for typical safety factor $q$, the leading-order poloidal variation of the total $B(\\theta)$ is dominated by $B_\\phi(\\theta)$. Expanding for $\\varepsilon \\ll 1$ gives\n$$\nB(\\theta) \\simeq B_0\\left(1 - \\varepsilon \\cos\\theta\\right) + \\mathcal{O}(\\varepsilon^2).\n$$\nThis captures the first-order poloidal variation of $B$ that enters neoclassical estimates of trapped particle fraction and parallel compressibility.\n\nRegarding the validity for the Pfirsch–Schlüter and plateau regimes: In the Pfirsch–Schlüter regime ($\\nu_* \\gg 1$), frequent collisions make the mean free path small compared to the connection length, and parallel viscous effects and the compressibility associated with $\\nabla \\cdot (n v_\\parallel)$ depend mainly on the leading geometric asymmetry proportional to $\\varepsilon$; the $\\mathcal{O}(\\varepsilon)$ variation embodied by $B(\\theta) \\simeq B_0/(1+\\varepsilon \\cos\\theta)$ is therefore adequate to leading order for transport coefficients in large-aspect-ratio, circular tokamaks. In the plateau regime (intermediate collisionality), the trapped particle fraction scaling and bounce-averaged dynamics depend on the amplitude of $B$ variation, again correctly captured at leading order by the above $B(\\theta)$ for circular, concentric geometry. However, for strong shaping, low aspect ratio, or when higher poloidal harmonics are important, corrections beyond $\\mathcal{O}(\\varepsilon)$ become necessary.\n\nOption-by-option analysis:\n\n- Option A: The definition of $(\\psi,\\theta,\\phi)$ aligns with flux coordinates: $\\psi$ labels flux surfaces with $B \\cdot \\nabla \\psi = 0$, $\\theta$ is the poloidal angle, and $\\phi$ the toroidal angle. The geometric relation $R(\\theta) = R_0 + r \\cos\\theta$ is correct for circular, concentric surfaces, and the toroidal field scaling $B(\\theta) \\simeq B_0 R_0/R(\\theta) = B_0/(1+\\varepsilon\\cos\\theta)$ is consistent with $B_\\phi \\propto 1/R$, with the first-order expansion $B(\\theta) \\simeq B_0(1 - \\varepsilon \\cos\\theta)$ valid for $\\varepsilon \\ll 1$. The validity statement correctly reflects that this approximation is sufficient to leading order in $\\varepsilon$ for Pfirsch–Schlüter and plateau transport in large-aspect-ratio circular tokamaks but may need refinement for strong shaping or finite aspect ratio. Verdict: Correct.\n\n- Option B: It swaps the roles of $\\theta$ and $\\phi$, which is inconsistent (it claims $\\theta$ is toroidal and $\\phi$ is poloidal), and it sets $B(\\theta) \\simeq B_0$, ignoring poloidal variation entirely. In an axisymmetric tokamak with circular, concentric geometry, $B(\\theta)$ does vary poloidally due to $R(\\theta)$, and this variation affects trapped particle physics and parallel compressibility. The assertion of universal validity is incorrect. Verdict: Incorrect.\n\n- Option C: It claims $B \\cdot \\nabla \\psi \\neq 0$, which contradicts the definition of a flux surface label since $B$ must be tangent to flux surfaces. Its $B(\\theta) \\simeq B_0(1+\\varepsilon \\cos\\theta)$ has the wrong functional dependence compared to the inverse-radius scaling; while one could approximate $B_0/(1+\\varepsilon \\cos\\theta)$ by $B_0(1 - \\varepsilon \\cos\\theta)$ to first order, replacing it by $B_0(1 + \\varepsilon \\cos\\theta)$ reverses the sign, moving the minimum of $B$ to the inboard side and the maximum to the outboard side, which is contrary to the geometry. The validity claim is also flawed: the Pfirsch–Schlüter regime is not “exact” under such an approximation, and the statement about plateau transport is not justified. Verdict: Incorrect.\n\n- Option D: The coordinate definitions are acceptable, but the proposed $B(\\theta) \\simeq B_0(1 - 2\\varepsilon \\cos\\theta)$ introduces an incorrect factor of $2$, which is not supported by the large-aspect-ratio inverse-radius scaling; to leading order, the coefficient of $\\cos\\theta$ is $1$, not $2$. The validity statement further claims that this form is “required” for Pfirsch–Schlüter compressibility and that plateau transport is dominated by higher-order harmonics at $\\mathcal{O}(\\varepsilon)$, which is incorrect; higher harmonics enter at higher order or through shaping, not at the same leading order. Verdict: Incorrect.\n\nTherefore, the option that correctly defines the coordinates, gives the appropriate large-aspect-ratio approximation for $B(\\theta)$, and assesses validity for Pfirsch–Schlüter and plateau transport is Option A."}, {"answer": "$$\\boxed{A}$$", "solution": "This problem requires an evaluation of statements concerning the fundamental description of a large-aspect-ratio, circular tokamak equilibrium and its implications for neoclassical transport. The evaluation is broken down into three parts: the definition of magnetic flux coordinates, the approximation of the magnetic field magnitude, and the validity of this approximation for the Pfirsch–Schlüter and plateau transport regimes.\n\n### Principle-Based Derivation\n\n**1. Magnetic Flux Coordinates $(\\psi, \\theta, \\phi)$ in an Axisymmetric Tokamak**\n\nIn a magnetically confined plasma, the magnetic field lines $\\vec{B}$ are designed to lie on a set of nested toroidal surfaces known as magnetic flux surfaces. Mathematically, these surfaces can be represented as level sets of a scalar function, which we label $\\psi$. For a field line to be confined to a surface, the field vector at any point on the surface must be tangent to it. This means the component of $\\vecB$ normal to the surface must be zero. The vector normal to a surface defined by $\\psi = \\text{constant}$ is $\\nabla\\psi$. Therefore, the defining property of a magnetic flux surface is:\n$$\n\\vec{B} \\cdot \\nabla\\psi = 0\n$$\nIn an axisymmetric tokamak, it is conventional to choose $\\psi$ to be the poloidal magnetic flux, which is the magnetic flux passing through a ribbon that extends from the major axis to the flux surface, integrated over the full toroidal angle $2\\pi$.\n\nThe other coordinates are angles:\n-   $\\phi$: The toroidal angle, which measures the position around the axis of symmetry of the torus. By definition, in an axisymmetric system, the equilibrium properties are independent of $\\phi$.\n-   $\\theta$: The poloidal angle, which measures the position around the minor cross-section of a given flux surface. The choice of $\\theta=0$ is a matter of convention, but it is typically set at the outboard midplane (the point on the flux surface furthest from the axis of symmetry).\n\nA standard right-handed coordinate system $(\\psi, \\theta, \\phi)$ is typically used, where $\\nabla\\psi$ points radially outward, $\\nabla\\phi$ points in the toroidal direction, and $\\nabla\\theta$ points in the poloidal direction.\n\n**2. Large-Aspect-Ratio Approximation for Magnetic Field Magnitude $B(\\theta)$**\n\nThe magnetic field in a tokamak is the sum of a dominant toroidal component $B_\\phi$ and a weaker poloidal component $B_p$. Thus, $B = |\\vec{B}| \\approx |B_\\phi|$. The toroidal field is generated by external coils, and within the plasma volume, it behaves approximately as a vacuum field. From Ampere's law, $\\nabla \\times \\vec{B}_\\text{toroidal} = 0$ for a vacuum field. In cylindrical coordinates $(R, \\phi, Z)$ centered on the torus's axis of symmetry, this leads to the well-known result that the toroidal field magnitude scales inversely with the major radius $R$:\n$$\nB_\\phi(R) \\propto \\frac{1}{R}\n$$\nFor a circular flux surface of minor radius $r$ centered at a major radius $R_0$, the major radius $R$ at a given poloidal angle $\\theta$ (with $\\theta=0$ at the outboard midplane) is given by a simple geometric relation:\n$$\nR(\\theta) = R_0 + r \\cos\\theta\n$$\nLet $B_0$ be the magnetic field strength at the geometric center of the flux surface, $R=R_0$. Then the field strength at any point on the surface is:\n$$\nB(\\theta) \\approx \\frac{B_0 R_0}{R(\\theta)} = \\frac{B_0 R_0}{R_0 + r \\cos\\theta} = \\frac{B_0}{1 + (r/R_0)\\cos\\theta}\n$$\nDefining the inverse aspect ratio as $\\varepsilon \\equiv r/R_0$, this becomes:\n$$\nB(\\theta) = \\frac{B_0}{1 + \\varepsilon \\cos\\theta}\n$$\nFor a large-aspect-ratio tokamak, $\\varepsilon \\ll 1$. We can use the Taylor expansion $(1+x)^{-1} \\approx 1 - x + x^2 - \\dots$ for small $x$. Keeping only the leading-order term in $\\varepsilon$, we get the standard approximation:\n$$\nB(\\theta) \\simeq B_0(1 - \\varepsilon \\cos\\theta)\n$$\nThis expression shows that the magnetic field is weakest on the outboard side ($\\theta = 0$, $B \\approx B_0(1-\\varepsilon)$) and strongest on the inboard side ($\\theta = \\pi$, $B \\approx B_0(1+\\varepsilon)$).\n\n**3. Validity for Pfirsch–Schlüter and Plateau Transport Regimes**\n\nNeoclassical transport arises from the combination of particle drifts in the non-uniform magnetic field and inter-particle collisions. The poloidal variation of $B$ is the root cause.\n\n-   **Pfirsch–Schlüter (P-S) Regime**: This is the high-collisionality limit, where the collision frequency is much higher than the particle bounce/transit frequency ($\\nu_* \\gg 1$). In this regime, particle orbits are constantly being interrupted by collisions, so the effects of trapped particle orbits are subdominant. The primary neoclassical effect is the generation of a parallel current, the Pfirsch–Schlüter current, which is necessary to ensure charge conservation ($\\nabla \\cdot \\vec{j} = 0$) in the presence of guiding-center drifts. This current, flowing through a resistive plasma, leads to enhanced transport. The derivation of the P-S fluxes depends on fluid equations and requires knowledge of the geometric factors arising from the $\\vec{B}$ field variation. The leading-order transport coefficients are derived using the $B(\\theta) \\simeq B_0(1 - \\varepsilon \\cos\\theta)$ approximation, which is sufficient to capture the dominant physics.\n\n-   **Plateau Regime**: This is the intermediate-collisionality regime, where the collision frequency is comparable to the particle transit frequency. Here, transport is dominated by a resonant interaction between the particles' nearly collisionless motion and the small-but-finite collisional scattering. This process is most effective for particles at the boundary between being trapped and passing. The fraction of trapped particles and the structure of their orbits are determined by the poloidal variation of $B$. The $B(\\theta) \\simeq B_0(1 - \\varepsilon \\cos\\theta)$ approximation correctly captures the depth of the magnetic well ($\\Delta B / B \\sim 2\\varepsilon$) and thus the fraction of trapped particles ($\\propto \\sqrt{\\varepsilon}$) to leading order. Consequently, this approximation is the standard and valid basis for deriving the leading-order plateau transport coefficients.\n\nThe expression $B(\\theta) \\simeq B_0(1 - \\varepsilon \\cos\\theta)$ is the cornerstone of analytical neoclassical theory in the large-aspect-ratio, circular limit for all collisionality regimes. It is understood that for finite aspect ratio or non-circular (shaped) plasmas, this approximation is insufficient. Shaping introduces higher-order poloidal harmonics (e.g., $\\cos(2\\theta)$, $\\sin(2\\theta), \\dots$) into the expression for $B$, and finite aspect ratio requires retaining more terms in the expansion, or using the full form of $B(\\theta)$.\n\n### Option-by-Option Analysis\n\n**A. $\\psi$ is the poloidal magnetic flux labeling nested flux surfaces so that $B \\cdot \\nabla \\psi = 0$, $\\theta$ is a right-handed poloidal angle increasing once around the minor cross-section, and $\\phi$ is the toroidal symmetry angle. On a circular, concentric surface, the major radius is $R(\\theta) = R_0 + r \\cos\\theta$, and the dominant toroidal field scales as $B(\\theta) \\simeq B_0 \\, R_0 / R(\\theta) = B_0/(1+\\varepsilon\\cos\\theta)$, with first-order expansion $B(\\theta) \\simeq B_0\\left(1-\\varepsilon\\cos\\theta\\right)$. This approximation is valid to leading order in $\\varepsilon$ for estimating Pfirsch–Schlüter and plateau neoclassical transport coefficients in large-aspect-ratio, circular tokamaks; it may incur errors if strong shaping or finite aspect ratio introduces higher poloidal harmonics beyond $\\mathcal{O}(\\varepsilon)$.**\n\n-   **Coordinates**: The definition of $(\\psi, \\theta, \\phi)$ and the condition $\\vec{B} \\cdot \\nabla \\psi = 0$ are correct.\n-   **$B(\\theta)$ Approximation**: The derivation from $B \\propto 1/R$ is correct, and the resulting first-order expansion $B(\\theta) \\simeq B_0(1-\\varepsilon\\cos\\theta)$ is correct.\n-   **Validity Assessment**: The statement that this approximation is valid and sufficient for deriving leading-order P-S and plateau transport is correct. The caveat about errors from shaping and finite aspect ratio is also a correct and important detail.\n-   **Verdict**: **Correct**.\n\n**B. $\\psi$ is the toroidal magnetic flux, $\\theta$ is the toroidal angle, and $\\phi$ is the poloidal angle. The magnetic-field magnitude is poloidally uniform $B(\\theta) \\simeq B_0$, which is universally valid for all aspect ratios, so the Pfirsch–Schlüter and plateau transport are unaffected by poloidal variation of $B$.**\n\n-   **Coordinates**: The roles of $\\psi$, $\\theta$, and $\\phi$ are incorrectly assigned. $\\psi$ is poloidal flux, $\\theta$ is poloidal angle, and $\\phi$ is toroidal angle in standard convention.\n-   **$B(\\theta)$ Approximation**: The claim that $B(\\theta) \\simeq B_0$ (is uniform) is fundamentally incorrect. The $1/R$ variation of the toroidal field is a basic principle of tokamak physics and is the very cause of neoclassical transport.\n-   **Validity Assessment**: The conclusion that P-S and plateau transport are unaffected is incorrect, as it stems from a false premise.\n-   **Verdict**: **Incorrect**.\n\n**C. $\\psi$ labels flux surfaces such that $B \\cdot \\nabla \\psi \\neq 0$, $\\theta$ is a poloidal angle, and $\\phi$ is the toroidal angle. The large-aspect-ratio approximation is $B(\\theta) \\simeq B_0(1+\\varepsilon\\cos\\theta)$, and this overestimates the trapped fraction, so it should be avoided for plateau transport but remains exact in the Pfirsch–Schlüter regime.**\n\n-   **Coordinates**: The condition $\\vec{B} \\cdot \\nabla \\psi \\neq 0$ violates the definition of a magnetic flux surface.\n-   **$B(\\theta)$ Approximation**: The approximation $B(\\theta) \\simeq B_0(1+\\varepsilon\\cos\\theta)$ has the wrong sign. This would incorrectly place the low-field region on the inboard side of the torus.\n-   **Validity Assessment**: The reasoning about trapped fraction is based on an incorrect formula. Furthermore, no such approximation is \"exact\" for the P-S regime; it is always an approximation.\n-   **Verdict**: **Incorrect**.\n\n**D. $\\psi$ is the poloidal magnetic flux labeling flux surfaces, $\\theta$ is the poloidal angle, and $\\phi$ is the toroidal angle. The magnetic-field magnitude is approximated by $B(\\theta) \\simeq B_0\\left(1-2\\varepsilon\\cos\\theta\\right)$, which is required to capture Pfirsch–Schlüter parallel compressibility and is invalid for plateau transport because trapped particles depend on higher-order harmonics that dominate at $\\mathcal{O}(\\varepsilon)$.**\n\n-   **Coordinates**: The coordinate definitions are correct.\n-   **$B(\\theta)$ Approximation**: The approximation $B(\\theta) \\simeq B_0(1-2\\varepsilon\\cos\\theta)$ is incorrect. The leading-order variation is proportional to $\\varepsilon$, not $2\\varepsilon$.\n-   **Validity Assessment**: The justification is flawed. The standard $B(\\theta) \\simeq B_0(1-\\varepsilon\\cos\\theta)$ model is sufficient to capture the relevant physics for P-S transport. The claim that higher-order harmonics dominate at $\\mathcal{O}(\\varepsilon)$ for plateau transport is nonsensical; the $\\cos\\theta$ term is the $\\mathcal{O}(\\varepsilon)$ dominant term that drives trapped particle effects in a circular tokamak.\n-   **Verdict**: **Incorrect**."}]


title: Deriving the saturated toroidal magnetic field strength in a stellar tachocline by balancing shear amplification with dissipation from the Tayler instability

Question: In the tachocline of a star, a region located at the interface between the radiative interior and the overlying convection zone, intense shear from differential rotation can amplify magnetic fields. This amplification is balanced by dissipative processes, leading to a statistically steady, or saturated, magnetic field strength. This problem concerns the derivation of this saturated field strength by modeling the interplay between shear amplification and dissipation from the Tayler instability.

Consider a thin, spherical shell representing the tachocline at a radius $r$ with a characteristic thickness $h \ll r$. The plasma in this region has a mean density $\rho$ and is threaded by a toroidal magnetic field of strength $B_\phi$. The permeability of free space is $\mu_0$.

The following physical models are to be used. Neglect all dimensionless constants of order unity in your derivation.

1.  **Shear Amplification:** The differential rotation, characterized by a shear strength $\Delta\Omega$, stretches the toroidal magnetic field lines, causing the field to be amplified. The growth rate of the magnetic field strength due to this process is proportional to the field strength itself:
    $$
    \left.\frac{dB_\phi}{dt}\right|_{\text{amp}} = B_\phi \Delta\Omega
    $$

2.  **The Tayler Instability and Dissipation:** The toroidal field is subject to the Tayler instability (a kink-type MHD instability). In the strongly stratified tachocline, the instability's growth is suppressed by buoyancy. The local Brunt-Väisälä frequency, $N_{BV}$, quantifies this stable stratification. The growth rate of the instability, $\sigma_{TI}$, is given by:
    $$
    \sigma_{TI} = \frac{\omega_A^2}{N_{BV}}
    $$
    where $\omega_A$ is the Alfvén frequency. The Alfvén frequency is $\omega_A = v_A / \lambda$, where $v_A$ is the Alfvén speed and the characteristic wavelength $\lambda$ of the instability is the tachocline radius, $r$.

3.  **Turbulent Magnetic Diffusion:** The Tayler instability drives turbulence, which dissipates the magnetic field. This can be modeled as a turbulent magnetic diffusion process. The turbulent magnetic diffusivity, $\eta_T$, is estimated using mixing-length theory as $\eta_T = v_{turb} L_{turb}$. The turbulent velocity scale, $v_{turb}$, is driven by the instability, such that $v_{turb} = \sigma_{TI} L_{inst}$, where the instability's characteristic length scale is $L_{inst} = r$. The turbulent mixing length, $L_{turb}$, is also assumed to be $r$. The resulting turbulent diffusivity dissipates the toroidal field, which varies over the thickness of the tachocline, $h$. The dissipation rate is thus given by the standard diffusion equation scaling:
    $$
    \left.\frac{dB_\phi}{dt}\right|_{\text{diss}} = -\frac{\eta_T}{h^2} B_\phi
    $$

Assuming that saturation is reached when the amplification rate balances the dissipation rate, derive an expression for the saturated toroidal magnetic field strength, $B_{sat}$.



Answer: [{"answer": "$$\n\\boxed{h \\sqrt{\\mu_0 \\rho N_{BV} \\Delta\\Omega}}\n$$", "solution": "The problem asks for the saturated toroidal magnetic field strength, $B_{sat}$, which is achieved when the amplification rate equals the dissipation rate.\n\nFirst, we establish the amplification rate as given in the problem statement:\n$$\n\\left.\\frac{dB_\\phi}{dt}\\right|_{\\text{amp}} = B_\\phi \\Delta\\Omega\n$$\n\nNext, we derive the dissipation rate. This requires several steps, starting from the Alfvén frequency. The Alfvén speed is $v_A = B_\\phi / \\sqrt{\\mu_0 \\rho}$. The Alfvén frequency $\\omega_A$ is defined with a characteristic length scale of the instability, given as the radius $r$:\n$$\n\\omega_A = \\frac{v_A}{r} = \\frac{B_\\phi}{r\\sqrt{\\mu_0 \\rho}}\n$$\n\nNow, we use this to find the growth rate of the Tayler instability, $\\sigma_{TI}$, in a stratified medium with Brunt-Väisälä frequency $N_{BV}$:\n$$\n\\sigma_{TI} = \\frac{\\omega_A^2}{N_{BV}} = \\frac{1}{N_{BV}} \\left(\\frac{B_\\phi}{r\\sqrt{\\mu_0 \\rho}}\\right)^2 = \\frac{B_\\phi^2}{N_{BV} \\mu_0 \\rho r^2}\n$$\n\nThe next step is to calculate the turbulent magnetic diffusivity, $\\eta_T$. The problem states that $\\eta_T = v_{turb} L_{turb}$, where $v_{turb} = \\sigma_{TI} L_{inst}$. We are given that both the instability length scale $L_{inst}$ and the turbulent mixing length $L_{turb}$ are equal to the tachocline radius $r$.\n$$\n\\eta_T = (\\sigma_{TI} r) r = \\sigma_{TI} r^2\n$$\nSubstituting the expression for $\\sigma_{TI}$:\n$$\n\\eta_T = \\left(\\frac{B_\\phi^2}{N_{BV} \\mu_0 \\rho r^2}\\right) r^2 = \\frac{B_\\phi^2}{N_{BV} \\mu_0 \\rho}\n$$\n\nFinally, we find the dissipation rate of the magnetic field. The field dissipates due to turbulent diffusion over its characteristic gradient length scale, which is the tachocline thickness $h$.\n$$\n\\left.\\frac{dB_\\phi}{dt}\\right|_{\\text{diss}} = -\\frac{\\eta_T}{h^2} B_\\phi\n$$\nSubstituting the expression for $\\eta_T$:\n$$\n\\left.\\frac{dB_\\phi}{dt}\\right|_{\\text{diss}} = -\\left(\\frac{B_\\phi^2}{N_{BV} \\mu_0 \\rho}\\right) \\frac{1}{h^2} B_\\phi = -\\frac{B_\\phi^3}{N_{BV} \\mu_0 \\rho h^2}\n$$\n\nSaturation occurs when the total rate of change of the magnetic field is zero. Let $B_{sat}$ be the saturated field strength.\n$$\n\\left.\\frac{dB_\\phi}{dt}\\right|_{\\text{amp}} + \\left.\\frac{dB_\\phi}{dt}\\right|_{\\text{diss}} = 0\n$$\n$$\nB_{sat} \\Delta\\Omega - \\frac{B_{sat}^3}{N_{BV} \\mu_0 \\rho h^2} = 0\n$$\nAssuming $B_{sat} \\neq 0$, we can divide by $B_{sat}$:\n$$\n\\Delta\\Omega = \\frac{B_{sat}^2}{N_{BV} \\mu_0 \\rho h^2}\n$$\nNow, we solve for $B_{sat}^2$:\n$$\nB_{sat}^2 = \\Delta\\Omega N_{BV} \\mu_0 \\rho h^2\n$$\nTaking the square root gives the final expression for the saturated toroidal magnetic field strength:\n$$\nB_{sat} = h \\sqrt{\\mu_0 \\rho N_{BV} \\Delta\\Omega}\n$$"}, {"answer": "$$\\boxed{h\\sqrt{\\mu_0\\rho\\,N_{BV}\\,\\Delta\\Omega}}$$", "solution": "Shear amplification rate  \n$$\\frac{dB_\\phi}{dt}\\Big|_{\\rm amp}=B_\\phi\\,\\Delta\\Omega.$$  \n\nAlfvén frequency and Tayler growth  \n$$v_A=\\frac{B_\\phi}{\\sqrt{\\mu_0\\rho}},\\quad\\omega_A=\\frac{v_A}{r},\\quad\n\\omega_A^2=\\frac{B_\\phi^2}{\\mu_0\\rho\\,r^2},$$  \n$$\\sigma_{TI}=\\frac{\\omega_A^2}{N_{BV}}\n=\\frac{B_\\phi^2}{\\mu_0\\rho\\,r^2\\,N_{BV}}.$$  \n\nTurbulent diffusivity  \n$$\\eta_T=\\sigma_{TI}\\,r^2\n=\\frac{B_\\phi^2}{\\mu_0\\rho\\,N_{BV}}.$$  \n\nMagnetic dissipation rate  \n$$\\frac{dB_\\phi}{dt}\\Big|_{\\rm diss}\n=-\\frac{\\eta_T}{h^2}\\,B_\\phi\n=-\\frac{B_\\phi^3}{\\mu_0\\rho\\,N_{BV}\\,h^2}.$$  \n\nSaturation: amplification balances dissipation  \n$$B_\\phi\\,\\Delta\\Omega=\\frac{B_\\phi^3}{\\mu_0\\rho\\,N_{BV}\\,h^2}$$  \n$$\\Longrightarrow\\;\\Delta\\Omega\n=\\frac{B_{sat}^2}{\\mu_0\\rho\\,N_{BV}\\,h^2}\n\\quad\\Longrightarrow\\quad\nB_{sat}^2=\\Delta\\Omega\\,\\mu_0\\rho\\,N_{BV}\\,h^2$$  \n$$\\boxed{B_{sat}=h\\sqrt{\\mu_0\\rho\\,N_{BV}\\,\\Delta\\Omega}}\\,. $$"}]


title: Describe the function of toroidal and poloidal field coils in generating the necessary magnetic fields in a Tokamak.

Question: In a Tokamak device, the generation of magnetic fields is crucial for plasma confinement during nuclear fusion reactions. Two types of magnetic field coils are primarily responsible for shaping and stabilizing the plasma: toroidal field coils and poloidal field coils. 

Which of the following statements correctly describes the roles of these coils in a Tokamak?

A. Toroidal field coils generate a magnetic field that encircles the plasma in the toroidal direction, providing plasma confinement, while poloidal field coils create a magnetic field in the poloidal direction to stabilize the plasma against instabilities.

B. Poloidal field coils are responsible for generating the toroidal magnetic field that confines the plasma, while toroidal field coils create a magnetic field in the poloidal direction to prevent plasma leakage.

C. Both toroidal and poloidal field coils generate magnetic fields in the same direction, combining their effects to confine plasma within the Tokamak.

D. Toroidal field coils generate the poloidal magnetic field, while poloidal field coils create the toroidal magnetic field to confine the plasma.

Select the correct option(s).

Answer: [{"answer": "$$\\boxed{A}$$", "solution": "## Problem Validation\n\n### Step 1: Extract Givens\n\nThe problem statement provides the following information:\n- Context: A Tokamak device used for plasma confinement during nuclear fusion reactions\n- Two types of magnetic field coils are identified:\n  1. Toroidal field coils\n  2. Poloidal field coils\n- Question: Which statement(s) correctly describe the roles of these coils in a Tokamak?\n- Four options (A, B, C, D) are provided for evaluation\n\n### Step 2: Validate Using Extracted Givens\n\n**Scientific Grounding**: The problem is firmly based on established principles of magnetic confinement fusion and Tokamak physics. Tokamaks are real devices used in fusion research, and the roles of toroidal and poloidal field coils are well-documented in plasma physics literature.\n\n**Well-Posed**: The problem has a clear structure asking for identification of correct statement(s) about the roles of two types of coils in a Tokamak device.\n\n**Objective**: The problem uses precise, technical language appropriate to the field of nuclear fusion engineering. The question is factual and can be answered based on established scientific knowledge.\n\n**Completeness**: The problem provides sufficient context about the device (Tokamak) and the components in question (toroidal and poloidal field coils).
