It is scientifically grounded, well-posed, objective, and complete. I will proceed with the solution.\n\n---\n\n## Solution\n\n### Fundamental Principles of Tokamak Magnetic Confinement\n\nIn a Tokamak device, magnetic confinement of plasma relies on a combination of magnetic fields in two orthogonal directions within the toroidal geometry:\n\n1. **Toroidal Direction**: The direction around the major axis of the torus (the \"long way around\" the donut)\n2. **Poloidal Direction**: The direction around the minor axis of the torus (the \"short way around\" the donut cross-section)\n\nThe key principle is that charged particles in the plasma follow helical paths along magnetic field lines. For effective confinement, the magnetic field must have components in both directions, creating twisted field lines that wrap around the torus.\n\n### Roles of the Coils\n\n**Toroidal Field Coils**: \n- These coils are arranged around the torus like spokes on a wheel\n- They generate a strong magnetic field $\\vec{B}_{\\text{tor}}$ in the **toroidal direction**\n- This field provides the primary confinement force, preventing plasma from expanding radially\n- The toroidal field strength typically ranges from $B_{\\text{tor}} \\sim 1-10$ T\n\n**Poloidal Field Coils**:\n- These coils are positioned around the outside of the toroidal chamber\n- They generate a magnetic field $\\vec{B}_{\\text{pol}}$ in the **poloidal direction**\n- This field serves multiple purposes:\n  - Creates the poloidal component necessary for field line twist\n  - Provides vertical stability to the plasma column\n  - Helps shape the plasma cross-section\n  - Prevents certain magnetohydrodynamic (MHD) instabilities\n\nThe combination of toroidal and poloidal fields creates helical field lines with rotational transform, which is essential for stable plasma confinement.\n\n### Evaluation of Options\n\n**Option A**: \"Toroidal field coils generate a magnetic field that encircles the plasma in the toroidal direction, providing plasma confinement, while poloidal field coils create a magnetic field in the poloidal direction to stabilize the plasma against instabilities.\"\n\nAnalysis: This statement correctly identifies that:\n- Toroidal field coils generate magnetic field in the toroidal direction → **Correct**\n- This toroidal field provides plasma confinement → **Correct**\n- Poloidal field coils create magnetic field in the poloidal direction → **Correct**\n- The poloidal field stabilizes plasma against instabilities → **Correct**\n\n**Verdict: CORRECT**\n\n---\n\n**Option B**: \"Poloidal field coils are responsible for generating the toroidal magnetic field that confines the plasma, while toroidal field coils create a magnetic field in the poloidal direction to prevent plasma leakage.\"\n\nAnalysis: This statement reverses the roles of the two coil systems:\n- Claims poloidal field coils generate toroidal field → **Incorrect** (they generate poloidal field)\n- Claims toroidal field coils generate poloidal field → **Incorrect** (they generate toroidal field)\n\nThis is a complete inversion of the actual physics.\n\n**Verdict: INCORRECT**\n\n---\n\n**Option C**: \"Both toroidal and poloidal field coils generate magnetic fields in the same direction, combining their effects to confine plasma within the Tokamak.\"\n\nAnalysis: This statement claims both coil systems generate fields in the same direction. In reality:\n- Toroidal field coils generate $\\vec{B}_{\\text{tor}}$ in the toroidal direction\n- Poloidal field coils generate $\\vec{B}_{\\text{pol}}$ in the poloidal direction\n- These are **orthogonal directions** in the toroidal geometry, not the same direction\n- The fields combine to create helical field lines, but they are not in the same direction\n\n**Verdict: INCORRECT**\n\n---\n\n**Option D**: \"Toroidal field coils generate the poloidal magnetic field, while poloidal field coils create the toroidal magnetic field to confine the plasma.\"\n\nAnalysis: Like Option B, this statement reverses the roles:\n- Claims toroidal field coils generate poloidal field → **Incorrect**\n- Claims poloidal field coils generate toroidal field → **Incorrect**\n\nThis contradicts the fundamental design and nomenclature of Tokamak coil systems.\n\n**Verdict: INCORRECT**"}, {"answer": "$$\\boxed{A}$$", "solution": "To solve this problem, we must understand the fundamental roles of toroidal field coils and poloidal field coils in a Tokamak device. A Tokamak relies on carefully designed magnetic fields to confine and stabilize the plasma, enabling nuclear fusion reactions.\n\n1. **Toroidal Field Coils**: These coils generate a magnetic field that loops around the toroidal (donut-shaped) axis of the Tokamak. This toroidal magnetic field is essential for confining the plasma and preventing it from escaping perpendicular to the axis of the Tokamak. It is created by running current through the toroidal field coils, producing a field that runs parallel to the major axis of the Tokamak.\n\n2. **Poloidal Field Coils**: These coils generate a magnetic field that encircles the plasma in the poloidal plane (the plane perpendicular to the toroidal axis). This poloidal field is necessary for controlling the position and shape of the plasma, as well as stabilizing it against certain types of instabilities, such as vertical displacement events. The poloidal field is typically generated by external coils and the current flowing through the plasma itself.\n\nLet us now evaluate the given options:\n\n- **Option A**: This statement correctly identifies the roles of the toroidal and poloidal field coils. The toroidal field coils generate the toroidal field for plasma confinement, and the poloidal field coils produce the poloidal field to stabilize the plasma. **Correct**.\n\n- **Option B**: This statement incorrectly swaps the roles of the coils. Poloidal field coils do not generate the toroidal field, and toroidal field coils do not produce the poloidal field. **Incorrect**.\n\n- **Option C**: This statement falsely claims that both types of coils generate magnetic fields in the same direction. In reality, the toroidal and poloidal fields are orthogonal to each other, serving distinct purposes. **Incorrect**.\n\n- **Option D**: This statement reverses the actual functions of the toroidal and poloidal field coils, similar to Option B. **Incorrect**.\n\nThus, the correct answer is Option A."}]


title: [multiple_choice] Define magnetic flux surfaces in an axisymmetric torus and explain why $p$ and other scalar equilibrium quantities must be flux functions $p(\psi)$.

Question: Consider a static, axisymmetric toroidal plasma described by ideal magnetohydrodynamics (MHD) and Maxwell’s equations. Let the symmetry direction be the toroidal angle $\phi$ in cylindrical coordinates $(R,\phi,Z)$, and let $\mathbf{B}(R,Z)$ denote the magnetic field, independent of $\phi$. Magnetic flux surfaces are the two-dimensional manifolds on which magnetic field lines lie; they are commonly labeled by a scalar $\psi(R,Z)$ called the poloidal magnetic flux. Choose the option that most accurately defines magnetic flux surfaces in an axisymmetric torus and correctly explains, from first principles of static equilibrium and axisymmetry, why the pressure $p$ and other single-valued scalar equilibrium quantities must be flux functions $p(\psi)$.

A. Magnetic flux surfaces are nested toroidal surfaces defined by $\psi(R,Z)=\text{const}$ such that $\mathbf{B}\cdot\nabla\psi=0$, i.e., field lines lie within each surface. In static ideal MHD equilibrium and axisymmetry, the force balance implies that $p$ is constant along magnetic field lines, hence constant on each magnetic surface; consequently $p=p(\psi)$. The same argument applies to other single-valued scalar equilibrium quantities without parallel source terms, so they must also be functions of $\psi$.

B. Magnetic flux surfaces are the surfaces on which $|\mathbf{B}|$ is constant. Because $\nabla\times\mathbf{B}=0$ in magnetostatics, the pressure must be spatially uniform, so $p$ is independent of $\psi$.

C. Magnetic flux surfaces are the surfaces of constant toroidal angle $\phi$. Axisymmetry requires that all equilibrium quantities are functions of $\phi$ only; therefore $p=p(\phi)$.

D. Magnetic flux surfaces are defined by $\nabla\cdot\mathbf{B}=0$. Since the magnetic field is divergence-free, it follows that the pressure gradient vanishes everywhere, and thus $p$ is constant throughout the volume.

E. Magnetic flux surfaces are the contours along which cross-field transport is strictly zero. Because transport across $\mathbf{B}$ is suppressed, the pressure must equilibrate rapidly on each surface, implying $p=p(\psi)$ even without invoking force balance or Maxwell’s equations.

Answer: [{"answer": "$$\\boxed{A}$$", "solution": "We start from magnetostatic Maxwell’s equations, $\\nabla\\cdot\\mathbf{B}=0$ and $\\nabla\\times\\mathbf{B}=\\mu_0\\mathbf{j}$, together with the static ideal magnetohydrodynamic (MHD) force balance $\\mathbf{j}\\times\\mathbf{B}=\\nabla p$. Axisymmetry means $\\partial/\\partial\\phi=0$ and all fields are independent of the toroidal angle $\\phi$.\n\nDefinition and existence of magnetic flux surfaces in axisymmetry:\nBecause $\\partial/\\partial\\phi=0$ and $\\nabla\\cdot\\mathbf{B}=0$, an axisymmetric solenoidal field admits a stream-function representation for its poloidal part: there exists a scalar $\\psi(R,Z)$ such that the poloidal magnetic field lies tangent to level sets of $\\psi$, equivalently $\\mathbf{B}\\cdot\\nabla\\psi=0$. The surfaces $\\psi(R,Z)=\\text{const}$ are therefore two-dimensional manifolds on which magnetic field lines lie. These are called magnetic flux surfaces, and $\\psi$ may be identified (up to a constant factor) with the poloidal magnetic flux threading a toroidal ribbon bounded by a given poloidal curve; axisymmetry ensures that this scalar label is well-defined and single-valued.\n\nWhy $p$ must be a flux function:\nThe static ideal MHD force balance is $\\mathbf{j}\\times\\mathbf{B}=\\nabla p$. Taking the scalar product with $\\mathbf{B}$ yields\n$$\n\\mathbf{B}\\cdot\\nabla p=\\mathbf{B}\\cdot(\\mathbf{j}\\times\\mathbf{B})=0,\n$$\nso $p$ is constant along magnetic field lines. In axisymmetry, magnetic field lines lie on nested flux surfaces $\\psi=\\text{const}$; thus $p$ is constant on each surface and can be written as a single-valued function of the flux-surface label,\n$$\np=p(\\psi).\n$$\nThe same line of reasoning applies to any single-valued scalar equilibrium quantity $X$ that enters the stationary balance and has no parallel source or sink in the equilibrium description: if the governing equilibrium relation implies $\\mathbf{B}\\cdot\\nabla X=0$, then $X=X(\\psi)$ is a flux function.\n\nOption-by-option analysis:\n\nA. This option correctly defines magnetic flux surfaces as the nested surfaces $\\psi=\\text{const}$ satisfying $\\mathbf{B}\\cdot\\nabla\\psi=0$, which captures that field lines lie on those surfaces. It then uses the fundamental force balance $\\mathbf{j}\\times\\mathbf{B}=\\nabla p$ and axisymmetry to derive $\\mathbf{B}\\cdot\\nabla p=0$, implying $p$ is constant along field lines and hence a function of the flux-surface label $\\psi$. The extension to other single-valued scalar equilibrium quantities without parallel sources is consistent with the same first-principles argument. Verdict: Correct.\n\nB. This option incorrectly identifies flux surfaces as $|\\mathbf{B}|=\\text{const}$ surfaces. Generally, $|\\mathbf{B}|$ varies on a flux surface in a torus (e.g., due to magnetic well and shaping), so $|\\mathbf{B}|=\\text{const}$ does not define flux surfaces. It also incorrectly states $\\nabla\\times\\mathbf{B}=0$; in magnetostatics with currents, we have $\\nabla\\times\\mathbf{B}=\\mu_0\\mathbf{j}\\neq 0$. Finally, the conclusion that $p$ must be spatially uniform is false; equilibrium supports $\\nabla p\\neq 0$ balanced by $\\mathbf{j}\\times\\mathbf{B}$. Verdict: Incorrect.\n\nC. This option confuses coordinate surfaces with flux surfaces. Surfaces of constant $\\phi$ are poloidal planes, not magnetic flux surfaces, and field lines are not generally confined to a single $\\phi=\\text{const}$ plane. Axisymmetry imposes $\\partial/\\partial\\phi=0$ on equilibrium quantities, meaning they do not depend on $\\phi$, but this does not imply $p=p(\\phi)$. In fact, axisymmetry supports $p=p(\\psi)$ with no $\\phi$ dependence. Verdict: Incorrect.\n\nD. This option misuses $\\nabla\\cdot\\mathbf{B}=0$. While $\\nabla\\cdot\\mathbf{B}=0$ is true, it does not define flux surfaces by itself, nor does it require $\\nabla p=\\mathbf{0}$. Pressure gradients are sustained in equilibrium via $\\mathbf{j}\\times\\mathbf{B}=\\nabla p$. The claim that $p$ is constant throughout the volume is therefore false. Verdict: Incorrect.\n\nE. This option appeals to transport anisotropy rather than equilibrium force balance. While cross-field transport is typically much slower than along-field transport, the statement that $p$ “must” be a flux function in equilibrium follows from $\\mathbf{B}\\cdot\\nabla p=0$ derived from $\\mathbf{j}\\times\\mathbf{B}=\\nabla p$, not from transport arguments. Moreover, transport suppression is not absolute and is time-dependent; the equilibrium condition is the rigorous reason for $p=p(\\psi)$. Verdict: Incorrect."}, {"answer": "$$\\boxed{A}$$", "solution": "The problem statement is subjected to validation.\n\n**Step 1: Extract Givens**\n-   System: Static, axisymmetric toroidal plasma.\n-   Model: Ideal magnetohydrodynamics (MHD) and Maxwell’s equations.\n-   Coordinates: Cylindrical $(R,\\phi,Z)$, with $\\phi$ as the toroidal angle and symmetry direction.\n-   Magnetic Field: $\\mathbf{B} = \\mathbf{B}(R,Z)$, independent of $\\phi$.\n-   Magnetic Flux Surfaces: Two-dimensional ($2$D) manifolds on which magnetic field lines lie, labeled by the poloidal magnetic flux scalar $\\psi(R,Z)$.\n-   Question: Select the option that (1) most accurately defines magnetic flux surfaces in an axisymmetric torus and (2) correctly explains, from first principles of static equilibrium and axisymmetry, why the pressure $p$ and other single-valued scalar equilibrium quantities must be flux functions, i.e., $p(\\psi)$.\n\n**Step 2: Validate Using Extracted Givens**\n-   **Scientific Groundedness:** The problem is firmly rooted in the standard model of ideal MHD, a cornerstone of plasma physics, particularly for describing macroscopic equilibria in fusion devices like tokamaks. The concepts of axisymmetry, magnetic flux surfaces, and the relationship between pressure and magnetic flux are fundamental and scientifically sound.\n-   **Well-Posedness:** The problem asks for the definition and derivation of a standard, well-established result in MHD theory ($p=p(\\psi)$). The premises (static, axisymmetric, ideal MHD) are sufficient to yield a unique and meaningful conclusion.\n-   **Objectivity:** The problem is stated in precise, technical language common to physics and engineering, free of subjectivity or ambiguity.\n-   **Completeness and Consistency:** The problem provides a complete set of physical assumptions (static, axisymmetric, ideal MHD) necessary for the derivation. There are no internal contradictions.\n\n**Step 3: Verdict and Action**\nThe problem statement is scientifically sound, well-posed, and internally consistent. It poses a standard, non-trivial question in plasma physics. Therefore, the problem is **valid**. The solution process will now proceed.\n\n**Derivation from First Principles**\n\nThe foundation of static MHD equilibrium is the force balance equation, which states that the sum of all forces on a plasma fluid element is zero. In ideal MHD, this involves the pressure gradient force and the Lorentz force:\n$$ \\nabla p = \\mathbf{j} \\times \\mathbf{B} $$\nwhere $p$ is the scalar plasma pressure, $\\mathbf{j}$ is the electric current density, and $\\mathbf{B}$ is the magnetic field.\n\nFrom magnetostatics (a consequence of Maxwell's equations in a static scenario), the current density is related to the magnetic field by Ampere's Law:\n$$ \\nabla \\times \\mathbf{B} = \\mu_0 \\mathbf{j} $$\nwhere $\\mu_0$ is the permeability of free space.\n\nThe crucial step is to analyze the relationship between the pressure gradient and the magnetic field. Take the dot product of the force balance equation with the magnetic field $\\mathbf{B}$:\n$$ \\mathbf{B} \\cdot \\nabla p = \\mathbf{B} \\cdot (\\mathbf{j} \\times \\mathbf{B}) $$\nThe term on the right-hand side is a scalar triple product involving two identical vectors ($\\mathbf{B}$). By the properties of the scalar triple product, $\\mathbf{A} \\cdot (\\mathbf{C} \\times \\mathbf{A}) = 0$ for any vectors $\\mathbf{A}$ and $\\mathbf{C}$. Therefore:\n$$ \\mathbf{B} \\cdot (\\mathbf{j} \\times \\mathbf{B}) = 0 $$\nThis leaves us with a fundamental result of static MHD equilibrium:\n$$ \\mathbf{B} \\cdot \\nabla p = 0 $$\nThis equation signifies that the gradient of the pressure, $\\nabla p$, is everywhere orthogonal to the magnetic field vector, $\\mathbf{B}$. Since the gradient vector points in the direction of the steepest ascent of a scalar function, this means that there is no change in pressure along the direction of a magnetic field line. In other words, **pressure $p$ is constant along each magnetic field line.**\n\nMagnetic flux surfaces are, by definition, surfaces that are composed of magnetic field lines. In an axisymmetric system, these surfaces form nested toroids. The mathematical definition of a surface $\\psi(R,Z) = \\text{const}$ being a magnetic flux surface is that the magnetic field vector $\\mathbf{B}$ is everywhere tangent to the surface. This is equivalent to stating that the field is perpendicular to the surface's normal vector, $\\nabla \\psi$. Thus, the definition of a magnetic flux surface is:\n$$ \\mathbf{B} \\cdot \\nabla \\psi = 0 $$\nSince pressure $p$ is constant along each magnetic field line, and since magnetic field lines (ergodically, in general) cover a magnetic flux surface, the pressure must be constant over the entire surface. As these surfaces are uniquely labeled by the value of the poloidal flux function $\\psi$, it follows directly that pressure can only be a function of $\\psi$. That is, $p=p(\\psi)$.\n\nThis reasoning can be extended to any other single-valued scalar quantity $Q$ in equilibrium, provided its governing equation does not introduce source or sink terms or dynamic effects parallel to the magnetic field.\n\n**Option-by-Option Analysis**\n\n**A. Magnetic flux surfaces are nested toroidal surfaces defined by $\\psi(R,Z)=\\text{const}$ such that $\\mathbf{B}\\cdot\\nabla\\psi=0$, i.e., field lines lie within each surface. In static ideal MHD equilibrium and axisymmetry, the force balance implies that $p$ is constant along magnetic field lines, hence constant on each magnetic surface; consequently $p=p(\\psi)$. The same argument applies to other single-valued scalar equilibrium quantities without parallel source terms, so they must also be functions of $\\psi$.**\n-   **Definition of Flux Surface:** The definition provided, $\\psi(R,Z)=\\text{const}$ with the condition $\\mathbf{B}\\cdot\\nabla\\psi=0$, is the correct mathematical and conceptual definition of a magnetic flux surface.\n-   **Derivation of $p=p(\\psi)$:** The explanation correctly identifies that the force balance equation ($\\nabla p = \\mathbf{j} \\times \\mathbf{B}$) leads to $\\mathbf{B}\\cdot\\nabla p=0$, which means $p$ is constant along field lines. The conclusion that $p$ must therefore be constant on flux surfaces, leading to $p=p(\\psi)$, is logically sound and follows the first-principles derivation.\n-   **Generalization:** The extension of the argument to other single-valued scalar quantities is also correct under the specified conditions (no parallel source terms).\n-   **Verdict:** **Correct**. This option is a complete and accurate summary of the relevant theory.\n\n**B. Magnetic flux surfaces are the surfaces on which $|\\mathbf{B}|$ is constant. Because $\\nabla\\times\\mathbf{B}=0$ in magnetostatics, the pressure must be spatially uniform, so $p$ is independent of $\\psi$.**\n-   **Definition of Flux Surface:** This is incorrect. Surfaces of constant magnetic field magnitude, $|\\mathbf{B}| = \\text{const}$, are known as \"mod-B surfaces\" and are, in general, distinct from magnetic flux surfaces. In a tokamak, $|\\mathbf{B}|$ varies as approximately $1/R$ on a flux surface.\n-   **Physics Premise:** The statement $\\nabla\\times\\mathbf{B}=0$ is incorrect. This would imply zero current density ($\\mathbf{j}=0$) and describe a vacuum magnetic field. A plasma capable of confining pressure must carry currents. The correct relation is Ampere's law, $\\nabla\\times\\mathbf{B}=\\mu_0\\mathbf{j}$.\n-   **Conclusion:** The conclusion that $p$ is uniform is based on the false premise of $\\mathbf{j}=0$.\n-   **Verdict:** **Incorrect**.\n\n**C. Magnetic flux surfaces are the surfaces of constant toroidal angle $\\phi$. Axisymmetry requires that all equilibrium quantities are functions of $\\phi$ only; therefore $p=p(\\phi)$.**\n-   **Definition of Flux Surface:** This is incorrect. Surfaces of constant $\\phi$ are meridional planes (half-planes). Magnetic flux surfaces are nested toroidal shells.\n-   **Definition of Axisymmetry:** This statement fundamentally misinterprets axisymmetry. Axisymmetry means that quantities are *independent* of the toroidal angle $\\phi$ (i.e., $\\partial/\\partial\\phi = 0$), not that they are functions of $\\phi$ only.\n-   **Conclusion:** The conclusion is based on two incorrect premises.\n-   **Verdict:** **Incorrect**.\n\n**D. Magnetic flux surfaces are defined by $\\nabla\\cdot\\mathbf{B}=0$. Since the magnetic field is divergence-free, it follows that the pressure gradient vanishes everywhere, and thus $p$ is constant throughout the volume.**\n-   **Definition of Flux Surface:** This is incorrect. $\\nabla\\cdot\\mathbf{B}=0$ (Gauss's law for magnetism) is a universal physical law, true everywhere, not the definition of a specific surface. It expresses the non-existence of magnetic monopoles.\n-   **Logical Leap:** The statement that $\\nabla\\cdot\\mathbf{B}=0$ implies $\\nabla p = 0$ is a non-sequitur. There is no such direct implication. The pressure gradient is determined by the force balance equation, $\\nabla p = \\mathbf{j} \\times \\mathbf{B}$.\n-   **Verdict:** **Incorrect**.\n\n**E. Magnetic flux surfaces are the contours along which cross-field transport is strictly zero. Because transport across $\\mathbf{B}$ is suppressed, the pressure must equilibrate rapidly on each surface, implying $p=p(\\psi)$ even without invoking force balance or Maxwell’s equations.**\n-   **Definition of Flux Surface:** This defines flux surfaces by a consequence (zero cross-field transport in ideal MHD) rather than by their fundamental geometric nature ($\\mathbf{B}\\cdot\\nabla\\psi=0$). In real, non-ideal plasmas, cross-field transport exists, yet the geometric concept of flux surfaces remains crucial. This is an imprecise and consequentialist definition.\n-   **Derivation:** This option presents a transport-based argument: fast parallel transport homogenizes pressure on a flux surface. While this is a valid physical intuition that supports the result, it is not a derivation from the *first principles of static equilibrium* as requested by the problem. The question demands a derivation based on force balance ($\\mathbf{j}\\times\\mathbf{B}=\\nabla p$). This option explicitly states it arrives at the conclusion *without* invoking force balance, thus failing to meet the problem's criteria.\n-   **Verdict:** **Incorrect**.\n\nBased on the analysis, option A is the only one that provides both a correct definition and a correct derivation based on the specified first principles."}]


title: [multiple_choice] Demonstrate that ideal MHD equilibrium implies nested toroidal flux surfaces and state the conditions on $\psi(R,Z)$ under which separatrices and X-points appear.

Question: Consider an axisymmetric toroidal confinement device (a tokamak) described in cylindrical coordinates $(R,\phi,Z)$ with $\partial/\partial \phi = 0$. Assume magnetostatic Maxwell equations hold, namely $\nabla \cdot \mathbf{B} = 0$ and $\nabla \times \mathbf{B} = \mu_0 \mathbf{J}$, and that the plasma is in ideal Magnetohydrodynamics (MHD) equilibrium with scalar pressure, i.e., $\mathbf{J} \times \mathbf{B} = \nabla p$. The poloidal flux function $\psi(R,Z)$ is a scalar field defined on the poloidal plane. Using only the stated governing laws and definitions, reason from first principles about the magnetic geometry.

Which of the following statements correctly capture a logically valid derivation that ideal MHD equilibrium implies nested toroidal magnetic flux surfaces and correctly state the conditions on $\psi(R,Z)$ under which separatrices and X-points appear? Select all that apply.

A. In axisymmetric ideal MHD equilibrium with scalar pressure, there exists a scalar $\psi(R,Z)$ such that $\mathbf{B} \cdot \nabla \psi = 0$, and the level sets $\psi = \text{const}$ are smooth, nested toroidal surfaces away from critical points. An X-point occurs where $\nabla \psi = \mathbf{0}$ and the Hessian of $\psi$ in $(R,Z)$ has one positive and one negative eigenvalue.

B. Nested toroidal flux surfaces require $\nabla \times \mathbf{B} = \mathbf{0}$ and $\nabla p = \mathbf{0}$. A separatrix occurs where $|\nabla \psi|$ is maximal, and X-points correspond to local minima of $\psi$.

C. In ideal MHD equilibrium, $\mathbf{B} \times \nabla p = \mathbf{0}$ implies $p$ is spatially constant, so flux surfaces do not exist. Separatrices and X-points are artifacts of non-ideal (resistive) effects and cannot occur in ideal equilibrium.

D. Under axisymmetry, the existence of nested flux surfaces follows from $\nabla \cdot \mathbf{B} = 0$ alone. X-points occur precisely where $\nabla^2 \psi = 0$.

E. In axisymmetric equilibrium, both $p$ and $R B_\phi$ are functions of $\psi$, and a separatrix is the $\psi$-contour that passes through an X-point characterized by $\nabla \psi = \mathbf{0}$ and a negative determinant of the Hessian of $\psi$ in the $(R,Z)$ plane.

Answer: [{"answer": "$$\\boxed{AE}$$", "solution": "We begin from the stated governing equations: magnetostatics with $\\nabla \\cdot \\mathbf{B} = 0$, $\\nabla \\times \\mathbf{B} = \\mu_0 \\mathbf{J}$, and ideal Magnetohydrodynamics (MHD) equilibrium $\\mathbf{J} \\times \\mathbf{B} = \\nabla p$, with axisymmetry $\\partial/\\partial \\phi = 0$ and scalar pressure $p(R,Z)$.\n\nPrinciple-based derivation of nested flux surfaces:\n1. Take the dot product of the ideal force balance with $\\mathbf{B}$:\n$$\n\\mathbf{B} \\cdot (\\mathbf{J} \\times \\mathbf{B}) = \\mathbf{B} \\cdot \\nabla p \\, .\n$$\nThe left-hand side is identically $0$ because $\\mathbf{B} \\cdot (\\mathbf{J} \\times \\mathbf{B}) = 0$ by scalar triple product antisymmetry. Therefore,\n$$\n\\mathbf{B} \\cdot \\nabla p = 0 \\, .\n$$\nHence, $p$ is constant along magnetic field lines. In axisymmetry, this integrability strongly suggests that there are smooth two-dimensional surfaces on which both magnetic field lines and $p$ lie, provided regularity holds (no singularities). For an axisymmetric divergence-free field, we can exhibit such surfaces explicitly via a scalar stream function.\n\n2. Existence of a poloidal flux function $\\psi(R,Z)$:\nIn cylindrical coordinates $(R,\\phi,Z)$ with $\\partial/\\partial \\phi = 0$, write $\\mathbf{B} = \\mathbf{B}_p + B_\\phi \\, \\hat{\\boldsymbol{\\phi}}$, where $\\mathbf{B}_p = B_R \\hat{\\mathbf{R}} + B_Z \\hat{\\mathbf{Z}}$ is the poloidal component. The divergence-free condition $\\nabla \\cdot \\mathbf{B} = 0$ reduces to\n$$\n\\frac{1}{R}\\frac{\\partial}{\\partial R}\\!\\left(R B_R\\right) + \\frac{\\partial B_Z}{\\partial Z} = 0 \\, .\n$$\nBy standard vector calculus in axisymmetric geometry, there exists a scalar stream function $\\psi(R,Z)$ such that\n$$\nB_R = -\\frac{1}{R} \\frac{\\partial \\psi}{\\partial Z} \\quad \\text{and} \\quad B_Z = \\frac{1}{R} \\frac{\\partial \\psi}{\\partial R} \\, .\n$$\nEquivalently, $\\mathbf{B}_p = \\frac{1}{R} \\nabla \\psi \\times \\hat{\\boldsymbol{\\phi}}$. It follows that\n$$\n\\mathbf{B}_p \\cdot \\nabla \\psi = 0 \\, ,\n$$\nand since $B_\\phi \\hat{\\boldsymbol{\\phi}} \\cdot \\nabla \\psi = 0$ (because $\\nabla \\psi$ lies in the poloidal plane and $\\hat{\\boldsymbol{\\phi}}$ is orthogonal to it),\n$$\n\\mathbf{B} \\cdot \\nabla \\psi = 0 \\, .\n$$\nTherefore, $\\psi = \\text{const}$ surfaces are magnetic surfaces; i.e., magnetic field lines lie within level sets of $\\psi$.\n\n3. Nested toroidal surfaces:\nFor regular points where $\\nabla \\psi \\neq \\mathbf{0}$, the implicit function theorem guarantees that $\\psi = \\text{const}$ defines smooth curves in the $(R,Z)$ plane. Revolving these curves around the symmetry axis (the $\\phi$-direction) produces smooth toroidal surfaces. Because the scalar field $\\psi(R,Z)$ is single-valued and continuous, distinct level sets do not intersect, establishing a nesting of these toroidal surfaces away from critical points. The topology can change only at critical points of $\\psi$ where $\\nabla \\psi = \\mathbf{0}$.\n\n4. Conditions for separatrices and X-points:\nAn X-point in the poloidal plane $(R,Z)$ is a hyperbolic critical point of $\\psi(R,Z)$, characterized by\n$$\n\\nabla \\psi(R_X,Z_X) = \\mathbf{0}\n$$\nand a saddle-type Hessian. Let the Hessian matrix be\n$$\nH(\\psi) = \\begin{pmatrix}\n\\frac{\\partial^2 \\psi}{\\partial R^2} & \\frac{\\partial^2 \\psi}{\\partial R \\partial Z} \\\\\n\\frac{\\partial^2 \\psi}{\\partial Z \\partial R} & \\frac{\\partial^2 \\psi}{\\partial Z^2}\n\\end{pmatrix} \\, ,\n$$\nand denote its determinant by\n$$\nD = \\left(\\frac{\\partial^2 \\psi}{\\partial R^2}\\right)\\!\\left(\\frac{\\partial^2 \\psi}{\\partial Z^2}\\right) - \\left(\\frac{\\partial^2 \\psi}{\\partial R \\partial Z}\\right)^2 \\, .\n$$\nFor a nondegenerate saddle (an X-point), $D < 0$. The level set of $\\psi$ that passes through such an X-point is a separatrix: it separates topologically distinct regions of nested flux surfaces (e.g., distinct bundles of invariant tori or a bounded plasma region from an open field region).\n\n5. Functions of $\\psi$ in axisymmetric equilibrium:\nIn axisymmetric ideal equilibrium, not only is $p$ constant on magnetic surfaces, but the toroidal field function $R B_\\phi$ is also constant on each magnetic surface. One way to see this is to project the equilibrium and Ampère’s law along $\\nabla \\psi$ and use that $\\mathbf{B} \\cdot \\nabla \\psi = 0$ and, in axisymmetry, the current $\\mathbf{J}$ has no component normal to the surfaces: $\\mathbf{J} \\cdot \\nabla \\psi = 0$. Consequently,\n$$\np = p(\\psi) \\quad \\text{and} \\quad R B_\\phi = F(\\psi)\n$$\nfor some scalar function $F$ determined by the equilibrium. This is the standard structure underlying the Grad–Shafranov formulation.\n\nOption-by-option analysis:\n- Option A: This states the existence of $\\psi(R,Z)$ with $\\mathbf{B} \\cdot \\nabla \\psi = 0$, identifies level sets as nested toroidal surfaces away from critical points, and correctly specifies the X-point condition: $\\nabla \\psi = \\mathbf{0}$ with an indefinite Hessian (one positive and one negative eigenvalue), i.e., $D < 0$. This matches the derivation above. Verdict: Correct.\n\n- Option B: It incorrectly asserts that nested toroidal surfaces require vacuum ($\\nabla \\times \\mathbf{B} = \\mathbf{0}$) and uniform pressure ($\\nabla p = \\mathbf{0}$). In fact, nested surfaces arise in non-vacuum ideal equilibria with nonuniform pressure $p = p(\\psi)$. It also misidentifies separatrices with maxima of $|\\nabla \\psi|$ and X-points with local minima. Separatrices are level sets through saddle points; X-points are not minima but hyperbolic critical points with $D < 0$. Verdict: Incorrect.\n\n- Option C: It misstates the equilibrium condition as $\\mathbf{B} \\times \\nabla p = \\mathbf{0}$, which is not implied by ideal MHD force balance $\\mathbf{J} \\times \\mathbf{B} = \\nabla p$. Moreover, $\\mathbf{B} \\cdot \\nabla p = 0$ does not imply spatially constant $p$; it implies $p = p(\\psi)$. Separatrices and X-points are perfectly consistent with ideal equilibrium and are not artifacts of resistivity. Verdict: Incorrect.\n\n- Option D: Divergence-free alone does not guarantee the existence of nested flux surfaces; axisymmetry plus the stream-function representation and equilibrium structure are used to ensure integrability and nesting. The criterion $\\nabla^2 \\psi = 0$ is not the condition for X-points; X-points are characterized by $\\nabla \\psi = \\mathbf{0}$ and $D < 0$. Verdict: Incorrect.\n\n- Option E: This correctly states that in axisymmetric equilibrium both $p$ and $R B_\\phi$ are functions of $\\psi$, consistent with $p = p(\\psi)$ and $R B_\\phi = F(\\psi)$. It correctly defines a separatrix as the $\\psi$-contour passing through an X-point with $\\nabla \\psi = \\mathbf{0}$ and negative Hessian determinant $D < 0$. Verdict: Correct.\n\nTherefore, the correct selections are Options A and E."}, {"answer": "$$\\boxed{AE}$$", "solution": "`: Principle-based derivation and option-by-option analysis.\n        *   `<final_answer>`: `$$\\boxed{...}$$` containing the correct uppercase letter(s) or an empty box if none are correct, or `$$\\boxed{invalid problem}$$` if the problem statement is invalid.\n\n2.  **Problem Validation (Step-by-Step):**\n\n    *   **Step 1: Extract Givens**\n        *   Device: Axisymmetric toroidal confinement device (tokamak).\n        *   Coordinates: Cylindrical $(R, \\phi, Z)$.\n        *   Symmetry: Axisymmetry, $\\partial/\\partial \\phi = 0$.\n        *   Governing Laws (Magnetostatics):\n            *   $\\nabla \\cdot \\mathbf{B} = 0$\n            *   $\\nabla \\times \\mathbf{B} = \\mu_0 \\mathbf{J}$\n        *   Plasma State: Ideal Magnetohydrodynamics (MHD) equilibrium.\n        *   Pressure: Scalar pressure, $p$.\n        *   Equilibrium Condition: $\\mathbf{J} \\times \\mathbf{B} = \\nabla p$.\n        *   Definition: Poloidal flux function $\\psi(R,Z)$ is a scalar field on the poloidal plane.\n        *   Question: Which statements correctly derive that ideal MHD equilibrium implies nested toroidal magnetic flux surfaces and correctly state the conditions for separatrices and X-points based on $\\psi(R,Z)$?\n\n    *   **Step 2: Validate Using Extracted Givens**\n        *   **Scientifically Grounded:** Yes. The problem is framed within the standard model of ideal MHD for tokamaks. The equations ($\\nabla \\cdot \\mathbf{B} = 0$, $\\nabla \\times \\mathbf{B} = \\mu_0 \\mathbf{J}$, $\\mathbf{J} \\times \\mathbf{B} = \\nabla p$) are the fundamental equations of magnetostatic equilibrium.
