I will proceed to the solution phase.\n\n3.  **Solution Derivation (From First Principles):**\n\n    *   **Start with the givens:**\n        1.  $\\nabla \\cdot \\mathbf{B} = 0$\n        2.  $\\mathbf{J} \\times \\mathbf{B} = \\nabla p$\n        3.  Axisymmetry: $\\partial/\\partial\\phi = 0$\n\n    *   **Derive the poloidal flux function $\\psi$:**\n        From $\\nabla \\cdot \\mathbf{B} = 0$ and axisymmetry in cylindrical coordinates $(R, \\phi, Z)$:\n        $$ \\frac{1}{R}\\frac{\\partial (RB_R)}{\\partial R} + \\frac{1}{R}\\frac{\\partial B_\\phi}{\\partial \\phi} + \\frac{\\partial B_Z}{\\partial Z} = 0 $$\n        Since $\\partial/\\partial\\phi = 0$, this becomes:\n        $$ \\frac{\\partial (RB_R)}{\\partial R} + \\frac{\\partial (RB_Z)}{\\partial Z} = 0 $$\n        This is the 2D divergence of the vector field $(RB_R, RB_Z)$ in the $(R,Z)$ plane being zero.\n        For any 2D vector field with zero divergence, there exists a scalar stream function. In this case, we define the poloidal flux function $\\psi(R,Z)$ such that:\n        $$ RB_R = -\\frac{\\partial \\psi}{\\partial Z} \\implies B_R = -\\frac{1}{R}\\frac{\\partial \\psi}{\\partial Z} $$\n        $$ RB_Z = \\frac{\\partial \\psi}{\\partial R} \\implies B_Z = \\frac{1}{R}\\frac{\\partial \\psi}{\\partial R} $$\n        This form automatically satisfies the poloidal part of $\\nabla \\cdot \\mathbf{B} = 0$.\n        The magnetic field is then $\\mathbf{B} = B_R \\hat{\\mathbf{R}} + B_\\phi \\hat{\\boldsymbol{\\phi}} + B_Z \\hat{\\mathbf{Z}} = -\\frac{1}{R}\\frac{\\partial \\psi}{\\partial Z} \\hat{\\mathbf{R}} + B_\\phi \\hat{\\boldsymbol{\\phi}} + \\frac{1}{R}\\frac{\\partial \\psi}{\\partial R} \\hat{\\mathbf{Z}}$.\n        We can write the poloidal magnetic field as $\\mathbf{B}_p = B_R \\hat{\\mathbf{R}} + B_Z \\hat{\\mathbf{Z}} = \\frac{1}{R} (\\nabla \\psi \\times \\hat{\\boldsymbol{\\phi}})$.\n        The gradient of $\\psi$ in the poloidal plane is $\\nabla \\psi = \\frac{\\partial \\psi}{\\partial R}\\hat{\\mathbf{R}} + \\frac{\\partial \\psi}{\\partial Z}\\hat{\\mathbf{Z}}$.\n        Let's check $\\mathbf{B} \\cdot \\nabla \\psi$:\n        $$ \\mathbf{B} \\cdot \\nabla \\psi = (B_R \\hat{\\mathbf{R}} + B_Z \\hat{\\mathbf{Z}}) \\cdot (\\frac{\\partial \\psi}{\\partial R}\\hat{\\mathbf{R}} + \\frac{\\partial \\psi}{\\partial Z}\\hat{\\mathbf{Z}}) = B_R \\frac{\\partial \\psi}{\\partial R} + B_Z \\frac{\\partial \\psi}{\\partial Z} $$\n        Substituting the expressions for $B_R$ and $B_Z$:\n        $$ \\mathbf{B} \\cdot \\nabla \\psi = (-\\frac{1}{R}\\frac{\\partial \\psi}{\\partial Z}) \\frac{\\partial \\psi}{\\partial R} + (\\frac{1}{R}\\frac{\\partial \\psi}{\\partial R}) \\frac{\\partial \\psi}{\\partial Z} = 0 $$\n        This identity means that magnetic field lines lie on surfaces of constant $\\psi$. These are the magnetic flux surfaces. Because of axisymmetry, these surfaces are toroidally symmetric, forming nested tori (if $\\psi$ has a single extremum).\n\n    *   **Incorporate the force balance equation:**\n        From $\\mathbf{J} \\times \\mathbf{B} = \\nabla p$.\n        Taking the dot product with $\\mathbf{B}$: $\\mathbf{B} \\cdot (\\mathbf{J} \\times \\mathbf{B}) = \\mathbf{B} \\cdot \\nabla p$. The left side is identically zero, so $\\mathbf{B} \\cdot \\nabla p = 0$. This means that pressure is constant along magnetic field lines. Since a magnetic field line generally ergodically covers a flux surface, $p$ must be a function of $\\psi$ only: $p = p(\\psi)$.\n        Taking the dot product with $\\mathbf{J}$: $\\mathbf{J} \\cdot (\\mathbf{J} \\times \\mathbf{B}) = \\mathbf{J} \\cdot \\nabla p$. The left side is identically zero, so $\\mathbf{J} \\cdot \\nabla p = 0$. This means that the current density vector is also tangent to surfaces of constant pressure.\n\n        Now, let's look at the force balance equation again. $\\nabla p = \\mathbf{J} \\times \\mathbf{B}$.\n        Since $p=p(\\psi)$, we have $\\nabla p = \\frac{dp}{d\\psi}\\nabla\\psi$.\n        So, $\\frac{dp}{d\\psi}\\nabla\\psi = \\mathbf{J} \\times \\mathbf{B}$.\n        This shows that $\\nabla \\psi$ is perpendicular to both $\\mathbf{J}$ and $\\mathbf{B}$, which is consistent with $\\mathbf{B} \\cdot \\nabla \\psi = 0$ and also implies $\\mathbf{J} \\cdot \\nabla \\psi = 0$. So current density also lies on flux surfaces.\n\n    *   **Derive the Grad-Shafranov equation (not strictly required by the question, but helps understanding):**\n        $\\mathbf{J} = \\frac{1}{\\mu_0}\\nabla\\times\\mathbf{B}$.\n        $\\mathbf{B} = \\frac{1}{R}(\\nabla \\psi \\times \\hat{\\boldsymbol{\\phi}}) + B_\\phi \\hat{\\boldsymbol{\\phi}}$.\n        The current density $\\mathbf{J}$ also has a poloidal and toroidal component.\n        Let's look at $\\mathbf{J} \\cdot \\mathbf{B} = \\mathbf{J} \\cdot \\nabla p / (\\mathbf{J} \\times \\mathbf{B}) \\cdot (\\mathbf{J} \\times \\mathbf{B})$? No, that's not helpful.\n        Let's write out $\\mathbf{J} \\times \\mathbf{B} = \\nabla p$.\n        $\\mathbf{J} = \\mathbf{J}_p + J_\\phi \\hat{\\boldsymbol{\\phi}}$. $\\mathbf{B} = \\mathbf{B}_p + B_\\phi \\hat{\\boldsymbol{\\phi}}$.\n        $\\mathbf{J} \\times \\mathbf{B} = (\\mathbf{J}_p + J_\\phi \\hat{\\boldsymbol{\\phi}}) \\times (\\mathbf{B}_p + B_\\phi \\hat{\\boldsymbol{\\phi}}) = (\\mathbf{J}_p \\times \\mathbf{B}_p) + (\\mathbf{J}_p \\times B_\\phi \\hat{\\boldsymbol{\\phi}}) + (J_\\phi \\hat{\\boldsymbol{\\phi}} \\times \\mathbf{B}_p) + (J_\\phi \\hat{\\boldsymbol{\\phi}} \\times B_\\phi \\hat{\\boldsymbol{\\phi}})$.\n        The first and last terms are zero because $\\mathbf{J}_p$ is parallel to $\\mathbf{B}_p$ and $\\hat{\\boldsymbol{\\phi}}$ is parallel to itself.\n        So $\\mathbf{J} \\times \\mathbf{B} = B_\\phi (\\mathbf{J}_p \\times \\hat{\\boldsymbol{\\phi}}) + J_\\phi (\\hat{\\boldsymbol{\\phi}} \\times \\mathbf{B}_p) = \\nabla p$.\n        Both terms on the LHS are in the poloidal plane.\n        Let $F(R,Z) = RB_\\phi$.\n        $\\mathbf{J}_p = \\frac{1}{\\mu_0} \\nabla \\times (B_\\phi \\hat{\\boldsymbol{\\phi}}) = \\frac{1}{\\mu_0} \\nabla \\times (\\frac{F}{R} \\hat{\\boldsymbol{\\phi}}) = \\frac{1}{\\mu_0} (\\nabla(\\frac{F}{R}) \\times \\hat{\\boldsymbol{\\phi}})$.\n        $\\nabla(\\frac{F}{R}) = \\frac{1}{R}\\nabla F - \\frac{F}{R^2}\\nabla R = \\frac{1}{R}\\nabla F - \\frac{F}{R^2}\\hat{\\mathbf{R}}$.\n        $\\nabla p$ is in the poloidal plane. $\\mathbf{B} \\cdot \\nabla p=0$ and $\\mathbf{J} \\cdot \\nabla p=0$. Also, $\\mathbf{B} \\cdot \\nabla (RB_\\phi)=0$ and $\\mathbf{J} \\cdot \\nabla (RB_\\phi)=0$ can be shown. This means that both $p$ and $RB_\\phi$ are functions of $\\psi$. So $p=p(\\psi)$ and $F = RB_\\phi = F(\\psi)$.\n        This is a key consequence of ideal MHD equilibrium.\n\n    *   **Summary of key results so far:**\n        1.  The existence of a poloidal flux function $\\psi(R,Z)$ is a direct consequence of $\\nabla \\cdot \\mathbf{B} = 0$ and axisymmetry.\n        2.  The condition $\\mathbf{B} \\cdot \\nabla \\psi = 0$ holds by definition of $\\psi$. This means magnetic field lines lie on surfaces of constant $\\psi$. These are the flux surfaces.\n        3.  The ideal MHD equilibrium condition $\\mathbf{J} \\times \\mathbf{B} = \\nabla p$ implies that $\\mathbf{B} \\cdot \\nabla p = 0$ and $\\mathbf{J} \\cdot \\nabla p = 0$. Since field lines are generally ergodic on flux surfaces, this implies $p$ must be a flux function, $p=p(\\psi)$.\n        4.  It can also be shown that $RB_\\phi$ is a flux function, $RB_\\phi = F(\\psi)$.\n        5.  The combination of these facts leads to the Grad-Shafranov equation, a 2D non-linear elliptic PDE for $\\psi$.\n        $$ \\Delta^* \\psi = - \\mu_0 R^2 \\frac{dp}{d\\psi} - F \\frac{dF}{d\\psi} $$\n        where $\\Delta^* \\psi = R \\frac{\\partial}{\\partial R}(\\frac{1}{R}\\frac{\\partial \\psi}{\\partial R}) + \\frac{\\partial^2 \\psi}{\\partial Z^2}$.\n\n    *   **Topology of Flux Surfaces, Separatrices, and X-points:**\n        *   **Flux surfaces:** The level sets $\\psi(R,Z) = \\text{const}$.\n        *   **Magnetic axis:** An extremum of $\\psi$ in the $(R,Z)$ plane. This is a point where $\\nabla \\psi = \\mathbf{0}$. At the magnetic axis, the poloidal field $\\mathbf{B}_p = \\frac{1}{R}(\\nabla\\psi \\times \\hat{\\boldsymbol{\\phi}})$ is zero. This corresponds to the center of the nested flux surfaces. It's a single field line running toroidally.\n        *   **Regular points:** Points where $\\nabla \\psi \\neq \\mathbf{0}$. Near such points, the implicit function theorem guarantees that the level sets are smooth curves (surfaces in 3D).\n        *   **Critical points:** Points where $\\nabla \\psi = \\mathbf{0}$. The local topology is determined by the Hessian matrix of $\\psi$.\n            $$ H = \\begin{pmatrix} \\psi_{RR} & \\psi_{RZ} \\\\ \\psi_{ZR} & \\psi_{ZZ} \\end{gematrix} $$\n            where $\\psi_{XY} = \\frac{\\partial^2 \\psi}{\\partial X \\partial Y}$.\n        *   **O-point (elliptic critical point):** This is the magnetic axis. It's a local minimum or maximum of $\\psi$. The Hessian has eigenvalues of the same sign (both positive or both negative). The determinant of the Hessian is positive: $\\det(H) = \\psi_{RR}\\psi_{ZZ} - \\psi_{RZ}^2 > 0$. Near an O-point, the level sets are ellipses (nested circles to first order), corresponding to the nested toroidal flux surfaces.\n        *   **X-point (hyperbolic critical point):** This is a saddle point of $\\psi$. The Hessian has one positive and one negative eigenvalue. The determinant of the Hessian is negative: $\\det(H) = \\psi_{RR}\\psi_{ZZ} - \\psi_{RZ}^2 < 0$. Near an X-point, the level sets look like hyperbolas.\n        *   **Separatrix:** The specific level set $\\psi = \\psi_X$ that passes through an X-point. It separates regions of different magnetic topology. For example, it can separate the core confined plasma from the scrape-off layer that interacts with a divertor.\n\n    *   **Now, let's evaluate the options based on this derivation.**\n\n    *   **Option A:** \"In axisymmetric ideal MHD equilibrium with scalar pressure, there exists a scalar $\\psi(R,Z)$ such that $\\mathbf{B} \\cdot \\nabla \\psi = 0$, and the level sets $\\psi = \\text{const}$ are smooth, nested toroidal surfaces away from critical points. An X-point occurs where $\\nabla \\psi = \\mathbf{0}$ and the Hessian of $\\psi$ in $(R,Z)$ has one positive and one negative eigenvalue.\"\n        *   `there exists a scalar \\psi(R,Z) such that \\mathbf{B} \\cdot \\nabla \\psi = 0`: Correct, derived from $\\nabla \\cdot \\mathbf{B} = 0$ and axisymmetry.\n        *   `the level sets \\psi = \\text{const} are smooth, nested toroidal surfaces away from critical points`: Correct. Away from critical points ($\\nabla \\psi = \\mathbf{0}$), the implicit function theorem ensures the surfaces are smooth. The \"nested\" structure arises from a solution with a single extremum (O-point).\n        *   `An X-point occurs where \\nabla \\psi = \\mathbf{0}`: Correct, this is the definition of a critical point.\n        *   `and the Hessian of \\psi in (R,Z) has one positive and one negative eigenvalue`: Correct. This is the definition of a saddle point (hyperbolic critical point) in Morse theory, which is what an X-point is for the scalar field $\\psi$.\n        *   **Verdict on A:** This statement seems entirely correct and well-formulated.\n\n    *   **Option B:** \"Nested toroidal flux surfaces require $\\nabla \\times \\mathbf{B} = \\mathbf{0}$ and $\\nabla p = \\mathbf{0}$. A separatrix occurs where $|\\nabla \\psi|$ is maximal, and X-points correspond to local minima of $\\psi$.\"\n        *   `Nested toroidal flux surfaces require \\nabla \\times \\mathbf{B} = \\mathbf{0} and \\nabla p = \\mathbf{0}`: Incorrect. $\\nabla \\times \\mathbf{B} = \\mu_0 \\mathbf{J}$. Requiring $\\mathbf{J}=\\mathbf{0}$ means a vacuum field. While vacuum fields can have flux surfaces, a plasma equilibrium requires $\\mathbf{J} \\neq \\mathbf{0}$ to confine the pressure. Requiring $\\nabla p = \\mathbf{0}$ means there is no pressure gradient to confine, which is a trivial case. The force-balance equation $\\mathbf{J} \\times \\mathbf{B} = \\nabla p$ becomes $\\mathbf{J} \\times \\mathbf{B} = \\mathbf{0}$, meaning $\\mathbf{J}$ is parallel to $\\mathbf{B}$. This is a force-free equilibrium, not necessarily one with zero pressure gradient throughout. This first part is fundamentally wrong.\n        *   `A separatrix occurs where |\\nabla \\psi| is maximal`: Incorrect. $|\\nabla \\psi|$ is proportional to the poloidal field magnitude ($|\\mathbf{B}_p| = |\\nabla\\psi|/R$). At an X-point, where a separatrix passes, $\\nabla \\psi = \\mathbf{0}$, so $|\\nabla \\psi|$ is minimal (zero).\n        *   `X-points correspond to local minima of \\psi`: Incorrect. Local minima (or maxima) are O-points, the magnetic axis. X-points are saddle points.\n        *   **Verdict on B:** This statement is incorrect on multiple fundamental points.\n\n    *   **Option C:** \"In ideal MHD equilibrium, $\\mathbf{B} \\times \\nabla p = \\mathbf{0}$ implies $p$ is spatially constant, so flux surfaces do not exist. Separatrices and X-points are artifacts of non-ideal (resistive) effects and cannot occur in ideal equilibrium.\"\n        *   `\\mathbf{B} \\times \\nabla p = \\mathbf{0}`: This is incorrect. The equilibrium equation is $\\mathbf{J} \\times \\mathbf{B} = \\nabla p$. This implies $\\mathbf{B} \\cdot \\nabla p = 0$, not $\\mathbf{B} \\times \\nabla p = \\mathbf{0}$. $\\mathbf{B} \\times \\nabla p = \\mathbf{0}$ would mean $\\nabla p$ is parallel to $\\mathbf{B}$, which contradicts $\\mathbf{B} \\cdot \\nabla p = 0$ unless $\\nabla p = \\mathbf{0}$ or $\\mathbf{B} = \\mathbf{0}$. The premise is wrong.\n        *   `implies p is spatially constant`: $\\mathbf{B} \\cdot \\nabla p = 0$ implies $p$ is constant along a field line, not necessarily spatially constant. For ergodic field lines on a surface, it implies $p$ is a flux function, $p(\\psi)$. Only if the field line covers all space (which it doesn't) would p be constant everywhere. The conclusion is a non-sequitur from the correct premise.\n        *   `so flux surfaces do not exist`: This is a non-sequitur and false. Flux surfaces exist as a consequence of $\\nabla \\cdot \\mathbf{B}=0$ and axisymmetry, independent of the pressure profile.\n        *   `Separatrices and X-points are artifacts of non-ideal (resistive) effects and cannot occur in ideal equilibrium`: This is false. X-points and separatrices are features of ideal MHD equilibria. They are solutions to the Grad-Shafranov equation for certain boundary conditions (e.g., divertor configurations). Resistive effects (like tearing modes) can create magnetic islands, which is a different phenomenon involving reconnection, but X-points themselves are an ideal concept.\n        *   **Verdict on C:** This statement is a cascade of errors.\n\n    *   **Option D:** \"Under axisymmetry, the existence of nested flux surfaces follows from $\\nabla \\cdot \\mathbf{B} = 0$ alone. X-points occur precisely where $\\nabla^2 \\psi = 0$.\"\n        *   `Under axisymmetry, the existence of nested flux surfaces follows from \\nabla \\cdot \\mathbf{B} = 0 alone`: The existence of the function $\\psi$ such that field lines lie on $\\psi=const$ surfaces follows from $\\nabla \\cdot \\mathbf{B} = 0$ and axisymmetry. However, for these surfaces to be \"nested tori,\" you need to solve the full equilibrium equation ($\\mathbf{J} \\times \\mathbf{B} = \\nabla p$) and find a solution with a single O-point. $\\nabla \\cdot \\mathbf{B} = 0$ alone doesn't guarantee this specific topology. For instance, a simple toroidally symmetric field like $B_\\phi \\propto 1/R$ and $B_p=0$ satisfies $\\nabla \\cdot \\mathbf{B}=0$ but has no poloidal flux surfaces. So this is an oversimplification.\n        *   `X-points occur precisely where \\nabla^2 \\psi = 0`: This is incorrect. X-points occur where $\\nabla \\psi = \\mathbf{0}$. $\\nabla^2 \\psi=0$ is Laplace's equation. The full equilibrium is governed by the Grad-Shafranov equation, $\\Delta^* \\psi = \\text{source terms}$, which is not Laplace's equation. $\\nabla^2 \\psi=0$ has no general connection to the location of X-points. Maybe the author confused $\\nabla \\psi$ with $\\nabla^2 \\psi$.\n        *   **Verdict on D:** This statement is incorrect. The first part is an oversimplification, and the second part is factually wrong.\n\n    *   **Option E:** \"In axisymmetric equilibrium, both $p$ and $R B_\\phi$ are functions of $\\psi$, and a separatrix is the $\\psi$-contour that passes through an X-point characterized by $\\nabla \\psi = \\mathbf{0}$ and a negative determinant of the Hessian of $\\psi$ in the $(R,Z)$ plane.\"\n        *   `In axisymmetric equilibrium, both p and R B_\\phi are functions of \\psi`: Correct. As derived from $\\mathbf{J} \\times \\mathbf{B} = \\nabla p$, we find $\\mathbf{B} \\cdot \\nabla p=0$ and $\\mathbf{J} \\cdot \\nabla p=0$, implying $p=p(\\psi)$. It can also be shown that $\\mathbf{B} \\cdot \\nabla(RB_\\phi)=0$ and $\\mathbf{J} \\cdot \\nabla(RB_\\phi)=0$, implying $RB_\\phi$ is a flux function, $RB_\\phi = F(\\psi)$. This is a standard result of ideal MHD equilibrium theory.\n        *   `a separatrix is the \\psi-contour that passes through an X-point`: Correct. This is the definition of a separatrix.\n        *   `characterized by \\nabla \\psi = \\mathbf{0}`: Correct. An X-point is a critical point of $\\psi$.\n        *   `and a negative determinant of the Hessian of \\psi in the (R,Z) plane`: Correct. The determinant of the Hessian is the product of its eigenvalues, $\\lambda_1 \\lambda_2$. For an X-point (saddle point), the eigenvalues have opposite signs ($\\lambda_1 > 0, \\lambda_2 < 0$), so their product is negative. $\\det(H) < 0$. This is equivalent to saying the Hessian has one positive and one negative eigenvalue, as stated in option A.\n        *   **Verdict on E:** This statement is entirely correct and provides deeper insight by mentioning the flux function nature of $RB_\\phi$.\n\n    *   **Comparison of A and E:**\n        *   Option A is correct. It states the existence of $\\psi$, the nature of flux surfaces, and the correct mathematical characterization of an X-point in terms of the Hessian's eigenvalues.\n        *   Option E is also correct. It starts with a deeper consequence of the equilibrium ($p(\\psi)$, $RB_\\phi(\\psi)$), correctly defines a separatrix, and gives the correct mathematical characterization of an X-point in terms of the Hessian's determinant.\n        *   The characterization of the X-point condition is equivalent in both:\n            *   A: \"one positive and one negative eigenvalue\"\n            *   E: \"a negative determinant of the Hessian\" ($\\det(H) = \\lambda_1 \\lambda_2 < 0$)\n        *   Both statements are logically valid derivations and state correct conditions.\n        *   The question asks to select all that apply. Therefore, both A and E should be selected.\n\n    *   **Final Check:**\n        *   **A:** Is flawless. It's a direct, logical progression.\n        *   **B:** Flawed. Wrong conditions for flux surfaces, wrong location of separatrix, wrong definition of X-point.\n        *   **C:** Flawed. Wrong force balance relation, wrong conclusion about pressure, wrong assertion about flux surfaces, wrong claim about X-points being non-ideal.\n        *   **D:** Flawed. Oversimplified condition for nested surfaces, wrong condition for X-points.\n        *   **E:** Is flawless. Mentions additional correct facts ($p(\\psi)$, $RB_\\phi(\\psi)$) and correctly characterizes the separatrix and X-point.\nThe problem statement is valid. It concerns standard, well-established principles of ideal MHD equilibrium in an axisymmetric device.\n\nDerivation from first principles:\nThe analysis begins with the law of magnetic flux conservation, $\\nabla \\cdot \\mathbf{B} = 0$. For an axisymmetric system in cylindrical coordinates $(R, \\phi, Z)$ where $\\partial/\\partial\\phi = 0$, this law reduces to\n$$ \\frac{\\partial (R B_R)}{\\partial R} + \\frac{\\partial (R B_Z)}{\\partial Z} = 0 $$\nThis equation is mathematically equivalent to stating that the vector field $(R B_R, R B_Z)$ in the $(R,Z)$ plane is divergenceless. For any such 2D vector field, a scalar potential or stream function can be defined. We define the poloidal flux function $\\psi(R,Z)$ such that its level sets correspond to the streamlines of this vector field. The standard definition is:\n$$ R B_R = -\\frac{\\partial \\psi}{\\partial Z} \\quad \\text{and} \\quad R B_Z = \\frac{\\partial \\psi}{\\partial R} $$\nThis allows the poloidal components of the magnetic field to be expressed as $B_R = -\\frac{1}{R}\\frac{\\partial \\psi}{\\partial Z}$ and $B_Z = \\frac{1}{R}\\frac{\\partial \\psi}{\\partial R}$. The full magnetic field is $\\mathbf{B} = \\mathbf{B}_p + B_\\phi \\hat{\\boldsymbol{\\phi}}$, where $\\mathbf{B}_p = B_R \\hat{\\mathbf{R}} + B_Z \\hat{\\mathbf{Z}}$.\n\nA crucial property of $\\psi$ is that magnetic field lines lie on surfaces of constant $\\psi$. This can be shown by calculating the dot product of the magnetic field vector and the gradient of $\\psi$, $\\nabla\\psi = \\frac{\\partial \\psi}{\\partial R}\\hat{\\mathbf{R}} + \\frac{\\partial \\psi}{\\partial Z}\\hat{\\mathbf{Z}}$.\n$$ \\mathbf{B} \\cdot \\nabla \\psi = (B_R \\hat{\\mathbf{R}} + B_Z \\hat{\\mathbf{Z}}) \\cdot \\nabla \\psi = B_R \\frac{\\partial \\psi}{\\partial R} + B_Z \\frac{\\partial \\psi}{\\partial R} = \\left(-\\frac{1}{R}\\frac{\\partial \\psi}{\\partial Z}\\right)\\frac{\\partial \\psi}{\\partial R} + \\left(\\frac{1}{R}\\frac{\\partial \\psi}{\\partial R}\\right)\\frac{\\partial \\psi}{\\partial Z} = 0 $$\nSince $\\mathbf{B} \\cdot \\nabla \\psi = 0$, the magnetic field vector $\\mathbf{B}$ is everywhere tangent to the surfaces defined by $\\psi(R,Z) = \\text{const}$. These are the magnetic flux surfaces.\n\nThe ideal MHD equilibrium force balance equation is $\\mathbf{J} \\times \\mathbf{B} = \\nabla p$. Taking the dot product with $\\mathbf{B}$ gives $\\mathbf{B} \\cdot (\\mathbf{J} \\times \\mathbf{B}) = \\mathbf{B} \\cdot \\nabla p$. Since the left side is identically zero, we must have $\\mathbf{B} \\cdot \\nabla p = 0$. This means the pressure gradient is everywhere perpendicular to the magnetic field, and thus pressure $p$ is constant on a magnetic flux surface. Hence, $p$ can be expressed as a function of $\\psi$ alone: $p = p(\\psi)$.\nA similar derivation using the $\\phi$-component of the force-balance equation shows that the quantity $F \\equiv R B_\\phi$ is also a function of $\\psi$ alone: $F = F(\\psi)$.\n\nThe topology of the flux surfaces is determined by the scalar field $\\psi(R,Z)$.\n- Regular points are where $\\nabla \\psi \\neq \\mathbf{0}$. At these points, the level sets are smooth, non-intersecting curves in the $(R,Z)$ plane.\n- Critical points are where $\\nabla \\psi = \\mathbf{0}$. These points correspond to locations where the poloidal magnetic field $\\mathbf{B}_p = \\frac{1}{R}(\\nabla \\psi \\times \\hat{\\boldsymbol{\\phi}})$ is zero. The nature of a critical point is determined by the Hessian matrix of $\\psi$:\n$$ H = \\begin{pmatrix} \\frac{\\partial^2\\psi}{\\partial R^2} & \\frac{\\partial^2\\psi}{\\partial R \\partial Z} \\\\ \\frac{\\partial^2\\psi}{\\partial Z \\partial R} & \\frac{\\partial^2\\psi}{\\partial Z^2} \\end{pmatrix} $$\n- If a critical point is a local extremum (minimum or maximum) of $\\psi$, it is an **O-point**. This corresponds to the magnetic axis, the center of the nested flux surfaces. The eigenvalues of the Hessian have the same sign, and the determinant is positive, $\\det(H) > 0$.\n- If a critical point is a saddle point of $\\psi$, it is an **X-point**. The eigenvalues of the Hessian have opposite signs, and the determinant is negative, $\\det(H) < 0$.\n- A **separatrix** is a flux surface ($\\psi = \\text{const}$) that passes through an X-point. It separates regions of different magnetic topology, such as the confined plasma from the scrape-off layer in a diverted tokamak.\n\nOption-by-option analysis:\n\nA. In axisymmetric ideal MHD equilibrium with scalar pressure, there exists a scalar $\\psi(R,Z)$ such that $\\mathbf{B} \\cdot \\nabla \\psi = 0$, and the level sets $\\psi = \\text{const}$ are smooth, nested toroidal surfaces away from critical points. An X-point occurs where $\\nabla \\psi = \\mathbf{0}$ and the Hessian of $\\psi$ in $(R,Z)$ has one positive and one negative eigenvalue.\nThis statement is fully consistent with the derivation. The existence and property $\\mathbf{B} \\cdot \\nabla \\psi = 0$ of $\\psi$ are correct. The description of flux surfaces is correct. The characterization of an X-point as a critical point ($\\nabla \\psi = \\mathbf{0}$) with Hessian eigenvalues of opposite sign is the correct mathematical definition of a saddle point. **Correct**.\n\nB. Nested toroidal flux surfaces require $\\nabla \\times \\mathbf{B} = \\mathbf{0}$ and $\\nabla p = \\mathbf{0}$. A separatrix occurs where $|\\nabla \\psi|$ is maximal, and X-points correspond to local minima of $\\psi$.\nThis statement is fundamentally flawed. Plasma confinement requires a non-zero current, $\\mathbf{J} \\neq \\mathbf{0}$, so $\\nabla \\times \\mathbf{B} = \\mu_0 \\mathbf{J} \\neq \\mathbf{0}$. It also requires a pressure gradient, $\\nabla p \\neq \\mathbf{0}$. A separatrix passes through an X-point where $\\nabla \\psi = \\mathbf{0}$, hence $|\\nabla \\psi|=0$, which is a minimum, not a maximum. X-points are saddle points, not local minima; local minima are O-points (the magnetic axis). **Incorrect**.\n\nC. In ideal MHD equilibrium, $\\mathbf{B} \\times \\nabla p = \\mathbf{0}$ implies $p$ is spatially constant, so flux surfaces do not exist. Separatrices and X-points are artifacts of non-ideal (resistive) effects and cannot occur in ideal equilibrium.\nThis statement is incorrect on all counts. The equilibrium condition implies $\\mathbf{B} \\cdot \\nabla p = 0$, not $\\mathbf{B} \\times \\nabla p = \\mathbf{0}$. Even if $\\mathbf{B} \\cdot \\nabla p = 0$, it only implies $p$ is constant on a flux surface ($p=p(\\psi)$), not spatially constant everywhere. The existence of flux surfaces follows from $\\nabla \\cdot \\mathbf{B} = 0$ and axisymmetry. Separatrices and X-points are well-defined features of ideal MHD equilibria. **Incorrect**.\n\nD. Under axisymmetry, the existence of nested flux surfaces follows from $\\nabla \\cdot \\mathbf{B} = 0$ alone. X-points occur precisely where $\\nabla^2 \\psi = 0$.\nThe existence of the function $\\psi$ follows from $\\nabla \\cdot \\mathbf{B} = 0$ and axisymmetry, but the existence of *nested* surfaces requires solving the full MHD equilibrium problem. An arbitrary field satisfying $\\nabla \\cdot \\mathbf{B} = 0$ may not have nested surfaces. The condition for an X-point is $\\nabla \\psi = \\mathbf{0}$, not $\\nabla^2 \\psi = 0$. The latter is Laplace's equation for $\\psi$, which corresponds to a vacuum poloidal field, a special case not generally applicable and unrelated to the location of critical points. **Incorrect**.\n\nE. In axisymmetric equilibrium, both $p$ and $R B_\\phi$ are functions of $\\psi$, and a separatrix is the $\\psi$-contour that passes through an X-point characterized by $\\nabla \\psi = \\mathbf{0}$ and a negative determinant of the Hessian of $\\psi$ in the $(R,Z)$ plane.\nThis statement is also fully consistent with the derivation. It correctly states that both pressure $p$ and the quantity $R B_\\phi$ are flux functions. It correctly defines a separatrix as the contour passing through an X-point. It correctly characterizes the X-point as a critical point ($\\nabla \\psi = \\mathbf{0}$) where the Hessian has a negative determinant. A negative determinant is mathematically equivalent to the eigenvalues having opposite signs, as stated in option A. **Correct**.\n\nBoth A and E are correct, comprehensive statements derived from first principles."}]


title: Given the velocity $v_i$ of a point $x_j$ in a rigid body rotation is $v_i=\epsilon_{ikj} \omega_k x_j$ for an angular velocity vector $\omega_k$, show that the angular velocity tensor $\Omega_{ij}$ defined by $v_i = \Omega_{ij}x_j$ is antisymmetric.

Question: In the study of continuum mechanics, the velocity field $\vec{v}$ in a small neighborhood of a point $P$ inside a deforming body can be approximated by a linear transformation of the position vector $\vec{x}$ relative to $P$. This relationship is expressed in Cartesian coordinates as $v_i = A_{ij} x_j$, where repeated indices imply summation over $j=1,2,3$, and the velocity gradient tensor $A_{ij}$ is constant throughout the neighborhood. This linear velocity field represents a combination of a pure rate of strain and a rigid body rotation about the point $P$.

Suppose the velocity gradient tensor for a particular flow is given by the matrix:
$$
A = \begin{pmatrix} 5 & 2 & 3 \\ 0 & 2 & 2 \\ -3 & 6 & -3 \end{pmatrix}
$$
Determine the components $(\omega_1, \omega_2, \omega_3)$ of the angular velocity vector $\vec{\omega}$ that describes the rigid body rotation component of the motion. Express your answer as a row matrix containing the three components in order.



Answer: [{"answer": "$$\\boxed{\\begin{pmatrix} 2 & 3 & -1 \\end{pmatrix}}$$", "solution": "The velocity gradient tensor $A_{ij}$ can be uniquely decomposed into a symmetric part and an antisymmetric part. The relationship is given by $A_{ij} = S_{ij} + \\Omega_{ij}$, where $S_{ij}$ is the symmetric rate-of-strain tensor and $\\Omega_{ij}$ is the antisymmetric spin tensor (or vorticity tensor).\n\nThe symmetric part is calculated as $S_{ij} = \\frac{1}{2}(A_{ij} + A_{ji})$, and it describes the deformation (stretching and shearing) of the material element.\nThe antisymmetric part is calculated as $\\Omega_{ij} = \\frac{1}{2}(A_{ij} - A_{ji})$, and it describes the rigid body rotation of the material element. Our goal is to find the angular velocity vector $\\vec{\\omega}$ associated with this spin tensor $\\Omega_{ij}$.\n\nFirst, we compute the spin tensor $\\Omega_{ij}$ from the given velocity gradient tensor $A_{ij}$.\nThe matrix representation of $A_{ij}$ is:\n$$ A = \\begin{pmatrix} 5 & 2 & 3 \\\\ 0 & 2 & 2 \\\\ -3 & 6 & -3 \\end{pmatrix} $$\nThe transpose of this matrix, $A^T$, which corresponds to the tensor $A_{ji}$, is:\n$$ A^T = \\begin{pmatrix} 5 & 0 & -3 \\\\ 2 & 2 & 6 \\\\ 3 & 2 & -3 \\end{pmatrix} $$\nNow we can compute the spin tensor $\\Omega$:\n$$ \\Omega = \\frac{1}{2}(A - A^T) = \\frac{1}{2} \\left[ \\begin{pmatrix} 5 & 2 & 3 \\\\ 0 & 2 & 2 \\\\ -3 & 6 & -3 \\end{pmatrix} - \\begin{pmatrix} 5 & 0 & -3 \\\\ 2 & 2 & 6 \\\\ 3 & 2 & -3 \\end{pmatrix} \\right] $$\n$$ \\Omega = \\frac{1}{2} \\begin{pmatrix} 5-5 & 2-0 & 3-(-3) \\\\ 0-2 & 2-2 & 2-6 \\\\ -3-3 & 6-2 & -3-(-3) \\end{pmatrix} = \\frac{1}{2} \\begin{pmatrix} 0 & 2 & 6 \\\\ -2 & 0 & -4 \\\\ -6 & 4 & 0 \\end{pmatrix} $$\n$$ \\Omega = \\begin{pmatrix} 0 & 1 & 3 \\\\ -1 & 0 & -2 \\\\ -3 & 2 & 0 \\end{pmatrix} $$\nThis is the matrix for the spin tensor $\\Omega_{ij}$. Note that it is antisymmetric, as expected ($\\Omega_{ij} = -\\Omega_{ji}$).\n\nThe spin tensor $\\Omega_{ij}$ is related to the angular velocity pseudovector $\\omega_k$ by the expression $\\Omega_{ij} = -\\epsilon_{ijk} \\omega_k$, where $\\epsilon_{ijk}$ is the Levi-Civita permutation symbol. This relation comes from expressing the cross product in the velocity formula $\\vec{v}_{rot} = \\vec{\\omega} \\times \\vec{x}$ in tensor notation.\n\nWe can extract the components of $\\vec{\\omega}$ by comparing the elements of our calculated $\\Omega$ matrix with the general form of the spin tensor:\n$$ \\Omega = \\begin{pmatrix} \\Omega_{11} & \\Omega_{12} & \\Omega_{13} \\\\ \\Omega_{21} & \\Omega_{22} & \\Omega_{23} \\\\ \\Omega_{31} & \\Omega_{32} & \\Omega_{33} \\end{pmatrix} = \\begin{pmatrix} 0 & -\\omega_3 & \\omega_2 \\\\ \\omega_3 & 0 & -\\omega_1 \\\\ -\\omega_2 & \\omega_1 & 0 \\end{pmatrix} $$\nComparing this general form with our calculated matrix $\\Omega = \\begin{pmatrix} 0 & 1 & 3 \\\\ -1 & 0 & -2 \\\\ -3 & 2 & 0 \\end{pmatrix}$:\nFrom the element $\\Omega_{23} = -\\omega_1$:\n$$ -2 = -\\omega_1 \\implies \\omega_1 = 2 $$\nFrom the element $\\Omega_{13} = \\omega_2$:\n$$ 3 = \\omega_2 \\implies \\omega_2 = 3 $$\nFrom the element $\\Omega_{12} = -\\omega_3$:\n$$ 1 = -\\omega_3 \\implies \\omega_3 = -1 $$\n\nAlternatively, one can use the dual relation $\\omega_k = -\\frac{1}{2}\\epsilon_{kij}\\Omega_{ij}$. Let's verify for $\\omega_1$:\n$$ \\omega_1 = -\\frac{1}{2}\\epsilon_{1ij}\\Omega_{ij} = -\\frac{1}{2}(\\epsilon_{123}\\Omega_{23} + \\epsilon_{132}\\Omega_{32}) = -\\frac{1}{2}(\\Omega_{23} - \\Omega_{32}) $$\nUsing the values from our computed $\\Omega$ matrix, $\\Omega_{23} = -2$ and $\\Omega_{32} = 2$.\n$$ \\omega_1 = -\\frac{1}{2}(-2 - 2) = -\\frac{1}{2}(-4) = 2 $$\nThis confirms our result for $\\omega_1$. The other components can be verified similarly.\n\nThe components of the angular velocity vector are $\\omega_1 = 2$, $\\omega_2 = 3$, and $\\omega_3 = -1$.\nThe question asks for the answer as a row matrix $(\\omega_1, \\omega_2, \\omega_3)$.\nThus, the answer is $\\begin{pmatrix} 2 & 3 & -1 \\end{pmatrix}$."}, {"answer": "$$\\boxed{\\begin{pmatrix} 2 & 3 & -1 \\end{pmatrix}}$$", "solution": "The velocity gradient tensor is $A_{ij} = \\partial v_{i}/\\partial x_{j}$. Decompose $A$ into its symmetric and skew-symmetric parts:\n$$\nD = \\frac{1}{2}\\left(A + A^{T}\\right), \\quad W = \\frac{1}{2}\\left(A - A^{T}\\right).\n$$\nThe rigid body rotation component is represented by the skew-symmetric tensor $W$, which corresponds to an angular velocity vector $\\vec{\\omega}$ via\n$$\nW = [\\omega]_{\\times}, \\quad [\\omega]_{\\times} = \\begin{pmatrix}\n0 & -\\omega_{3} & \\omega_{2} \\\\\n\\omega_{3} & 0 & -\\omega_{1} \\\\\n-\\omega_{2} & \\omega_{1} & 0\n\\end{pmatrix}.\n$$\nGiven\n$$\nA = \\begin{pmatrix} 5 & 2 & 3 \\\\ 0 & 2 & 2 \\\\ -3 & 6 & -3 \\end{pmatrix},\n$$\ncompute its transpose:\n$$\nA^{T} = \\begin{pmatrix} 5 & 0 & -3 \\\\ 2 & 2 & 6 \\\\ 3 & 2 & -3 \\end{pmatrix}.\n$$\nThen\n$$\nA - A^{T} = \\begin{pmatrix}\n0 & 2 & 6 \\\\\n-2 & 0 & -4 \\\\\n-6 & 4 & 0\n\\end{pmatrix},\n\\quad\nW = \\frac{1}{2}\\left(A - A^{T}\\right) = \\begin{pmatrix}\n0 & 1 & 3 \\\\\n-1 & 0 & -2 \\\\\n-3 & 2 & 0\n\\end{pmatrix}.\n$$\nMatch $W$ to $[\\omega]_{\\times}$ entrywise:\n$$\nW_{12} = 1 = -\\omega_{3} \\Rightarrow \\omega_{3} = -1,\\quad\nW_{13} = 3 = \\omega_{2} \\Rightarrow \\omega_{2} = 3,\\quad\nW_{23} = -2 = -\\omega_{1} \\Rightarrow \\omega_{1} = 2.\n$$\nThus, the angular velocity components are $(\\omega_{1}, \\omega_{2}, \\omega_{3}) = (2, 3, -1)$."}]


title: Deriving the approximate relationship between the total plasma current, edge safety factor, and central safety factor for a large-aspect-ratio, circular tokamak with a parabolic current density profile

Question: In the study of magnetically confined plasmas, such as those in a tokamak, the safety factor, $q$, is a crucial parameter that describes the pitch of the helical magnetic field lines. For a large-aspect-ratio ($R_0 \gg a$) tokamak with a circular cross-section, where $R_0$ is the major radius and $a$ is the minor radius, the safety factor at a given minor radius $r$ can be approximated as:
$$
q(r) = \frac{r B_\phi}{R_0 B_\theta(r)}
$$
Here, $B_\phi$ is the toroidal magnetic field, which is assumed to be constant across the plasma cross-section, and $B_\theta(r)$ is the poloidal magnetic field generated by the plasma current.

Consider a tokamak plasma where the toroidal current density, $j_\phi(r)$, has a parabolic profile given by:
$$
j_\phi(r) = j_0 \left(1 - \frac{r^2}{a^2}\right)
$$
for $0 \le r \le a$, and $j_\phi(r) = 0$ for $r > a$. The term $j_0$ represents the central current density on the magnetic axis ($r=0$).

The poloidal magnetic field $B_\theta(r)$ is related to the current enclosed within a radius $r$, $I_{enc}(r)$, by Ampere's law in cylindrical coordinates: $2\pi r B_\theta(r) = \mu_0 I_{enc}(r)$.

The safety factor on the magnetic axis is defined as $q_0 = \lim_{r \to 0} q(r)$, and the safety factor at the plasma edge is $q_a = q(a)$. These two values are fundamental to determining the stability of the plasma.

For the given parabolic current density profile, derive the exact value of the ratio of the edge safety factor to the central safety factor, $q_a/q_0$.



Answer: [{"answer": "$$\n\\boxed{2}\n$$", "solution": "The problem asks for the ratio $q_a/q_0$ for a large-aspect-ratio, circular tokamak with a parabolic current density profile.\n\nFirst, we need to find the poloidal magnetic field, $B_\\theta(r)$, which depends on the enclosed current, $I_{enc}(r)$. The current density is given as $j_\\phi(r) = j_0 (1 - r^2/a^2)$.\n\nThe enclosed current $I_{enc}(r)$ is found by integrating the current density over the circular area of radius $r$:\n$$\nI_{enc}(r) = \\int_0^r j_\\phi(r') (2\\pi r') dr'\n$$\nSubstituting the parabolic profile for $j_\\phi(r')$:\n$$\nI_{enc}(r) = \\int_0^r j_0 \\left(1 - \\frac{r'^2}{a^2}\\right) 2\\pi r' dr'\n$$\n$$\nI_{enc}(r) = 2\\pi j_0 \\int_0^r \\left(r' - \\frac{r'^3}{a^2}\\right) dr'\n$$\n$$\nI_{enc}(r) = 2\\pi j_0 \\left[\\frac{r'^2}{2} - \\frac{r'^4}{4a^2}\\right]_0^r\n$$\n$$\nI_{enc}(r) = 2\\pi j_0 \\left(\\frac{r^2}{2} - \\frac{r^4}{4a^2}\\right) = \\pi j_0 r^2 \\left(1 - \\frac{r^2}{2a^2}\\right)\n$$\n\nNow, we use Ampere's law to find the poloidal magnetic field $B_\\theta(r)$:\n$$\nB_\\theta(r) = \\frac{\\mu_0 I_{enc}(r)}{2\\pi r}\n$$\n$$\nB_\\theta(r) = \\frac{\\mu_0}{2\\pi r} \\left[\\pi j_0 r^2 \\left(1 - \\frac{r^2}{2a^2}\\right)\\right]\n$$\n$$\nB_\\theta(r) = \\frac{\\mu_0 j_0 r}{2} \\left(1 - \\frac{r^2}{2a^2}\\right)\n$$\n\nNext, we use the given definition of the safety factor, $q(r)$:\n$$\nq(r) = \\frac{r B_\\phi}{R_0 B_\\theta(r)}\n$$\nSubstitute the derived expression for $B_\\theta(r)$:\n$$\nq(r) = \\frac{r B_\\phi}{R_0 \\left[\\frac{\\mu_0 j_0 r}{2} \\left(1 - \\frac{r^2}{2a^2}\\right)\\right]}\n$$\n$$\nq(r) = \\frac{2 B_\\phi}{\\mu_0 j_0 R_0} \\frac{1}{1 - \\frac{r^2}{2a^2}}\n$$\n\nNow we can determine the central safety factor, $q_0$, by taking the limit as $r \\to 0$:\n$$\nq_0 = \\lim_{r \\to 0} q(r) = \\lim_{r \\to 0} \\left[ \\frac{2 B_\\phi}{\\mu_0 j_0 R_0} \\frac{1}{1 - \\frac{r^2}{2a^2}} \\right]\n$$\n$$\nq_0 = \\frac{2 B_\\phi}{\\mu_0 j_0 R_0} \\frac{1}{1 - 0} = \\frac{2 B_\\phi}{\\mu_0 j_0 R_0}\n$$\n\nThen, we determine the edge safety factor, $q_a$, by evaluating $q(r)$ at $r=a$:\n$$\nq_a = q(a) = \\frac{2 B_\\phi}{\\mu_0 j_0 R_0} \\frac{1}{1 - \\frac{a^2}{2a^2}}\n$$\n$$\nq_a = \\frac{2 B_\\phi}{\\mu_0 j_0 R_0} \\frac{1}{1 - \\frac{1}{2}}\n$$\n$$\nq_a = \\frac{2 B_\\phi}{\\mu_0 j_0 R_0} \\frac{1}{1/2} = 2 \\left(\\frac{2 B_\\phi}{\\mu_0 j_0 R_0}\\right)\n$$\n\nWe can see that $q_a = 2 q_0$.\n\nFinally, we compute the required ratio $q_a/q_0$:\n$$\n\\frac{q_a}{q_0} = \\frac{2 \\left(\\frac{2 B_\\phi}{\\mu_0 j_0 R_0}\\right)}{\\frac{2 B_\\phi}{\\mu_0 j_0 R_0}}\n$$\n$$\n\\frac{q_a}{q_0} = 2\n$$"}, {"answer": "$$\\boxed{2}$$", "solution": "We start from Ampère’s law in cylindrical geometry, relating the poloidal field $B_\\theta(r)$ to the enclosed current $I_{\\rm enc}(r)$:\n$$\n2\\pi\\,r\\,B_\\theta(r)=\\mu_0\\,I_{\\rm enc}(r), \n\\quad\nI_{\\rm enc}(r)=\\int_0^r2\\pi\\,r'\\,j_\\phi(r')\\,dr'.\n$$\n\nUsing the given profile $j_\\phi(r)=j_0\\bigl(1-r^2/a^2\\bigr)$, the enclosed current is\n$$\nI_{\\rm enc}(r)\n=2\\pi j_0\\int_0^r\\Bigl(r'-\\frac{r'^3}{a^2}\\Bigr)\\,dr'\n=2\\pi j_0\\Bigl[\\frac{r^2}{2}-\\frac{r^4}{4a^2}\\Bigr].\n$$\n\nThus\n$$\nB_\\theta(r)\n=\\frac{\\mu_0\\,I_{\\rm enc}(r)}{2\\pi\\,r}\n=\\mu_0\\,j_0\\Bigl(\\frac{r}{2}-\\frac{r^3}{4a^2}\\Bigr).\n$$\n\nThe safety factor is\n$$\nq(r)=\\frac{r\\,B_\\phi}{R_0\\,B_\\theta(r)}\n=\\frac{B_\\phi}{R_0\\mu_0j_0}\\,\\frac{r}{\\displaystyle r/2-r^3/(4a^2)}\n=\\frac{2B_\\phi}{R_0\\mu_0j_0}\\,\\frac{1}{1-\\dfrac{r^2}{2a^2}}.\n$$\n\nHence on axis,\n$$\nq_0=\\lim_{r\\to0}q(r)=\\frac{2B_\\phi}{R_0\\mu_0j_0},\n$$\nand at the edge,\n$$\nq_a=q(a)\n=\\frac{2B_\\phi}{R_0\\mu_0j_0}\\,\\frac{1}{1-\\tfrac12}\n=2\\,\\frac{2B_\\phi}{R_0\\mu_0j_0}.\n$$\n\nThe ratio is therefore\n$$\n\\frac{q_a}{q_0}=2.\n$$"}]


title: [multiple_choice] Explain how shear in the magnetic field lines stabilizes ideal modes and define a shear parameter relevant to suppressing interchange-like disturbances.

Question: In the framework of ideal Magnetohydrodynamics (MHD), consider a large-aspect-ratio tokamak with major radius $R$, minor radius $r$, equilibrium magnetic field $\mathbf{B}$, scalar pressure $p(r)$, and safety factor $q(r)$ defined by the ratio of toroidal to poloidal field-line turns. Interchange-like disturbances are displacements $\boldsymbol{\xi}$ that are nearly incompressible, predominantly perpendicular to $\mathbf{B}$, and attempt to swap fluid elements across a pressure gradient where magnetic curvature is unfavorable. Using the ideal MHD energy principle, the change in potential energy $\delta W$ due to a displacement $\boldsymbol{\xi}$ contains a stabilizing field-line bending contribution (associated with parallel gradients of $\boldsymbol{\xi}$ along $\mathbf{B}$) and a destabilizing pressure-curvature contribution (associated with $-\nabla p$ aligned with unfavorable curvature). In a local, field-aligned representation near a rational surface where $q(r_{0})=m/n$, take an interchange-like perturbation with perpendicular wavenumber $k_{y}$ (approximately poloidal) and radial label $x\equiv r-r_{0}$. The parallel wavenumber $k_{\parallel}$ of such a disturbance is affected by the radial variation of the field-line pitch (magnetic shear), altering the field-line bending energy. 

Which option correctly explains how magnetic shear stabilizes ideal interchange or ballooning-like modes by modifying $k_{\parallel}$, and also provides a physically relevant definition of a magnetic shear parameter used to quantify this stabilization?

A. In a tokamak, magnetic shear, quantified by $\hat{s}\equiv (r/q)\,\mathrm{d}q/\mathrm{d}r$, causes $k_{\parallel}$ of an interchange-like perturbation to grow linearly with $x$, as $k_{\parallel}\sim (k_{y}\,\hat{s}\,x)/(q\,R)$, thereby increasing the field-line bending energy proportional to $B^{2}k_{\parallel}^{2}|\boldsymbol{\xi}|^{2}$ and stabilizing ideal interchange or ballooning modes at fixed pressure gradient.

B. Magnetic shear is quantified by $\alpha\equiv -\left(R\,q^{2}/B^{2}\right)\,\mathrm{d}p/\mathrm{d}r$, and increasing $\alpha$ always reduces curvature drive and stabilizes interchange. The effect of shear is to decrease $k_{\parallel}$ and lower the field-line bending energy.

C. Magnetic shear is measured by $S\equiv B^{-1}\,\mathrm{d}B/\mathrm{d}r$, and stronger shear lowers $k_{\parallel}$ everywhere, reducing the line-bending contribution to the ideal MHD energy and stabilizing interchange.

D. In resistive Magnetohydrodynamics (MHD), shear stabilizes ideal modes by enhancing resistive diffusion; the appropriate shear parameter is $S\equiv \eta/(4\pi)$, with $\eta$ the resistivity, and larger $S$ suppresses interchange-like disturbances by dissipating magnetic energy.

Answer: [{"answer": "$$\\boxed{A}$$", "solution": "The starting point is the ideal Magnetohydrodynamics (MHD) energy principle. For a displacement $\\boldsymbol{\\xi}$ from a static equilibrium, the potential energy change $\\delta W$ can be written as an integral of stabilizing and destabilizing terms. The stabilizing term is associated with the energy cost of bending magnetic field lines and scales like an integral of $B^{2}\\,k_{\\parallel}^{2}\\,|\\boldsymbol{\\xi}_{\\perp}|^{2}$, where $k_{\\parallel}$ is the parallel wavenumber along $\\mathbf{B}$, and $\\boldsymbol{\\xi}_{\\perp}$ is the component of $\\boldsymbol{\\xi}$ perpendicular to $\\mathbf{B}$. The destabilizing term is associated with the pressure-gradient and magnetic curvature (for toroidal systems), which for interchange-like perturbations attempts to lower energy by moving fluid elements across $\\nabla p$ in regions of unfavorable curvature. Qualitatively, stability requires the field-line bending energy to offset the curvature-pressure drive.\n\nIn a large-aspect-ratio tokamak, it is standard to adopt local, field-aligned coordinates near a rational surface $q(r_{0})=m/n$. Let $x\\equiv r-r_{0}$ be the local radial coordinate, and let $y$ label the direction perpendicular to $\\mathbf{B}$ around the flux surface (approximately poloidal), so that the perpendicular wavenumber of the perturbation is $k_{y}$. The parallel derivative operator is $\\nabla_{\\parallel}\\equiv \\mathbf{b}\\cdot\\nabla$, where $\\mathbf{b}\\equiv \\mathbf{B}/B$ is the unit vector along the field. Magnetic shear refers to the radial variation of the field-line pitch, i.e., how $q(r)$ changes with radius. It is quantified locally by the dimensionless magnetic shear parameter\n$$\n\\hat{s}\\equiv \\frac{r}{q}\\,\\frac{\\mathrm{d}q}{\\mathrm{d}r}.\n$$\nBecause the field-line pitch changes with $r$, a mode that is locally aligned to have small $k_{\\parallel}$ at $x=0$ will inevitably develop finite $k_{\\parallel}$ as one moves away from $x=0$. In the standard local ballooning representation, this yields\n$$\nk_{\\parallel}\\simeq \\frac{k_{y}\\,\\hat{s}\\,x}{q\\,R},\n$$\nfor interchange-like perturbations with negligible intrinsic parallel phase variation at $x=0$. The field-line bending energy density then scales like\n$$\n\\delta W_{\\text{bend}} \\sim \\int \\mathrm{d}^{3}x\\,\\frac{B^{2}}{4\\pi}\\,k_{\\parallel}^{2}\\,|\\boldsymbol{\\xi}_{\\perp}|^{2} \\sim \\int \\mathrm{d}^{3}x\\,\\frac{B^{2}}{4\\pi}\\,\\left(\\frac{k_{y}\\,\\hat{s}\\,x}{q\\,R}\\right)^{2}\\,|\\boldsymbol{\\xi}_{\\perp}|^{2}.\n$$\nThis shows that increasing $\\hat{s}$ raises $k_{\\parallel}$ away from the rational surface, and therefore increases the field-line bending energy, which is stabilizing for ideal interchange or ballooning-like modes. Physically, magnetic shear prevents the mode from maintaining $k_{\\parallel}\\approx 0$ over an extended radial domain, thereby imposing a significant bending energy penalty for finite radial extent. In contrast, the destabilizing term depends on the pressure gradient $-\\mathrm{d}p/\\mathrm{d}r$ and curvature, often summarized in the so-called ballooning parameter\n$$\n\\alpha \\equiv -\\frac{R\\,q^{2}}{B^{2}}\\,\\frac{\\mathrm{d}p}{\\mathrm{d}r},\n$$\nwhich is not a measure of shear but rather a measure of the pressure-gradient drive. Increasing $\\alpha$ at fixed $\\hat{s}$ strengthens destabilization rather than stabilization.\n\nWith this principle-based reasoning, we can now evaluate the options.\n\nOption A: It states that magnetic shear is quantified by $\\hat{s}\\equiv (r/q)\\,\\mathrm{d}q/\\mathrm{d}r$, matches the standard local definition. It also states that shear causes $k_{\\parallel}$ to grow linearly with $x$ as $k_{\\parallel}\\sim (k_{y}\\,\\hat{s}\\,x)/(q\\,R)$, which is the usual local ballooning form near a rational surface for interchange-like perturbations. It correctly identifies how this increases the field-line bending energy $\\propto B^{2}\\,k_{\\parallel}^{2}\\,|\\boldsymbol{\\xi}|^{2}$ and thereby stabilizes ideal interchange or ballooning modes at fixed pressure gradient. Verdict — Correct.\n\nOption B: It identifies $\\alpha\\equiv -\\left(R\\,q^{2}/B^{2}\\right)\\,\\mathrm{d}p/\\mathrm{d}r$ as the shear parameter. This is incorrect because $\\alpha$ is the normalized pressure-gradient parameter, not magnetic shear. It further claims that increasing $\\alpha$ reduces curvature drive and stabilizes interchange, which is the opposite of the standard ballooning physics: increasing $\\alpha$ enhances the destabilizing pressure-curvature drive. It also claims shear decreases $k_{\\parallel}$ and lowers bending energy, which contradicts the mechanism described above. Verdict — Incorrect.\n\nOption C: It proposes $S\\equiv B^{-1}\\,\\mathrm{d}B/\\mathrm{d}r$ as the shear measure. This is a gradient of magnetic field strength, not a measure of field-line pitch or safety-factor variation. The statement that stronger shear lowers $k_{\\parallel}$ everywhere and reduces line-bending is contrary to the local ballooning physics where magnetic shear increases $k_{\\parallel}$ away from the rational surface. Verdict — Incorrect.\n\nOption D: It invokes resistive Magnetohydrodynamics (MHD) and defines shear via the resistivity $\\eta$ as $S\\equiv \\eta/(4\\pi)$. Magnetic shear is a geometric property of field-line pitch and is independent of resistive diffusion. Moreover, resistivity is associated with non-ideal effects that can enable tearing-type instabilities rather than stabilizing ideal interchange by line bending. The claim that resistive diffusion is the shear-based stabilizing mechanism for ideal modes is incorrect. Verdict — Incorrect.\n\nTherefore, only Option A correctly explains how magnetic shear stabilizes interchange-like ideal modes and defines a relevant shear parameter."}, {"answer": "$$\\boxed{A}$$", "solution": "` and `<final_answer>` tags.\n    *   **Validation Process:**\n        1.  Extract givens verbatim.\n        2.  Validate against a checklist (scientific soundness, well-posedness, objectivity, completeness, etc.).\n        3.  Decide to proceed or abort.\n    *   **Solution Process (if valid):**\n        1.  Derive the answer from first principles (ideal MHD energy principle, concept of magnetic shear).\n        2.  Analyze each option (A, B, C, D) and justify whether it's correct or incorrect.\n    *   **Final Answer Format:**\n        *   If valid: `$$\\boxed{CORRECT_OPTIONS}$$` (e.g., `$$\\boxed{A}$$`, `$$\\boxed{BD}$$`).\n        *   If invalid: `$$\\boxed{invalid problem}$$`.\n\n2.  **Step-by-Step Execution:**\n\n    **Part 1: Problem Validation**\n\n    *   **Step 1: Extract Givens:**\n        *   Framework: Ideal Magnetohydrodynamics (MHD).\n        *   System: Large-aspect-ratio tokamak.\n        *   Parameters: Major radius $R$, minor radius $r$, equilibrium magnetic field $\\mathbf{B}$, scalar pressure $p(r)$, safety factor $q(r)$.\n        *   Definition: $q(r)$ is the ratio of toroidal to poloidal field-line turns.\n        *   Disturbance type: Interchange-like disturbances with displacement $\\boldsymbol{\\xi}$.\n        *   Properties of disturbance: nearly incompressible, predominantly perpendicular to $\\mathbf{B}$, swap fluid elements across a pressure gradient where magnetic curvature is unfavorable.\n        *   Energy principle: The change in potential energy $\\delta W$ contains a stabilizing field-line bending term and a destabilizing pressure-curvature term.\n        *   Destabilizing term association: $-\\nabla p$ aligned with unfavorable curvature.\n        *   Stabilizing term association: parallel gradients of $\\boldsymbol{\\xi}$ along $\\mathbf{B}$.\n        *   Local representation: Near a rational surface where $q(r_0) = m/n$.\n        *   Coordinates: Radial label $x \\equiv r - r_0$. Perpendicular wavenumber $k_y$ (approximately poloidal).\n        *   Key effect: The parallel wavenumber $k_{\\parallel}$ is affected by the radial variation of the field-line pitch (magnetic shear), altering the field-line bending energy.\n        *   Question: Which option correctly explains how magnetic shear stabilizes ideal interchange or ballooning-like modes by modifying $k_{\\parallel}$, and also provides a physically relevant definition of a magnetic shear parameter?\n\n    *   **Step 2: Validate Using Extracted Givens:**\n        *   **Scientifically Grounded?** Yes. The problem describes the fundamental mechanism of ideal MHD interchange/ballooning instability and its stabilization by magnetic shear in a tokamak. The concepts of safety factor $q$, magnetic shear, field-line bending, and pressure-curvature drive are standard in plasma physics. The ideal MHD energy principle is a cornerstone of MHD stability theory.\n        *   **Well-Posed?** Yes. It asks for a conceptual explanation and the correct definition of magnetic shear. The destabilizing term is related to pressure gradient and curvature, $\\sim \\nabla p \\cdot \\kappa$, where $\\kappa$ is the curvature vector. The stabilizing term is related to field-line bending, $\\sim B^2 (\\nabla \\cdot \\boldsymbol{\\xi}_\\perp)^2 + B^2 (\\mathbf{b} \\cdot \\nabla \\boldsymbol{\\xi})^2$. The key term for shear stabilization is the one involving the parallel gradient: $(\\mathbf{b} \\cdot \\nabla)^2 \\sim k_\\parallel^2$.\n            2.  **Non-Formalizable/Irrelevant?** No. The topic is directly about ideal MHD models in fusion plasma turbulence simulation.\n            3.  **Incomplete/Contradictory?** No. The problem provides sufficient context to answer the question. It describes the physical setup and asks for the mechanism of shear stabilization.\n            4.  **Unrealistic/Infeasible?** No. The setup is a standard model in tokamak physics.\n            5.  **Ill-Posed/Poorly Structured?** No. The terms are well-defined in the field.\n            6.  **Pseudo-Profound/Trivial?** No. This is a core concept in MHD stability that requires understanding the interplay of geometry, pressure, and magnetic fields.\n            7.  **Outside Scientific Verifiability?** No. The statements can be derived from the MHD equations.\n\n    *   **Step 3: Verdict and Action:**\n        *   **Verdict:** The problem is **valid**.\n        *   **Action:** Proceed to the solution.\n\n    **Part 2: Solution Derivation**\n\n    1.  **First Principles Derivation:**\n        *   The core of the problem is the behavior of the parallel wavenumber, $k_{\\parallel}$, near a rational surface.\n        *   A perturbation is represented as $\\boldsymbol{\\xi}(r, \\theta, \\phi) = \\boldsymbol{\\xi}(r) \\exp[i(m\\theta - n\\phi)]$, where $\\theta$ is poloidal angle and $\\phi$ is toroidal angle.\n        *   The operator $\\mathbf{B} \\cdot \\nabla$ is proportional to the parallel wavenumber $k_{\\parallel}$. For a field line, $d\\phi/d\\theta = q(r) B_\\phi/(r B_\\theta) \\approx q(r)/R$. A field line follows a path where $m\\theta - n\\phi = \\text{const}$. No, that's not right. The field line pitch is $d \\ell_\\parallel = B d\\phi / B_\\phi = R d\\phi$ (approximately). And $d\\ell_\\parallel = B d\\theta / B_\\theta$. So $B_\\phi / B_\\theta = R/ (r q)$. Wait, $q = (r B_\\phi) / (R B_\\theta)$. Ok.\n        *   The parallel gradient operator is $\\mathbf{b} \\cdot \\nabla = (\\mathbf{B}/B) \\cdot \\nabla$. In toroidal coordinates $(r, \\theta, \\phi)$, $\\mathbf{B} \\approx B_\\theta(r) \\hat{\\mathbf{e}}_\\theta + B_\\phi(r) \\hat{\\mathbf{e}}_\\phi$. So $\\mathbf{B} \\cdot \\nabla \\approx \\frac{B_\\theta}{r} \\frac{\\partial}{\\partial\\theta} + \\frac{B_\\phi}{R} \\frac{\\partial}{\\partial\\phi}$.\n        *   Applying this to the perturbation $\\exp[i(m\\theta - n\\phi)]$:\n            $\\mathbf{B} \\cdot \\nabla \\exp[i(m\\theta - n\\phi)] = \\left( i \\frac{m B_\\theta}{r} - i \\frac{n B_\\phi}{R} \\right) \\exp[i(m\\theta - n\\phi)]$.\n        *   The effective parallel wavenumber $k_{\\parallel}$ is proportional to the term in parenthesis. Using the definition of the safety factor $q(r) = \\frac{r B_\\phi}{R B_\\theta}$:\n            $k_{\\parallel} \\propto \\frac{m B_\\theta}{r} - \\frac{n B_\\phi}{R} = \\frac{B_\\theta}{r} \\left( m - n \\frac{r B_\\phi}{R B_\\theta} \\right) = \\frac{B_\\theta}{r} (m - n q(r))$.\n        *   At a rational surface $r=r_0$, we have $q(r_0) = m/n$. Therefore, at $r=r_0$, $k_{\\parallel}(r_0) = 0$. This means there is no field-line bending *at* the rational surface for this specific mode $(m,n)$. This is why interchange modes are dangerous.\n        *   Now, consider a point slightly away from the rational surface, $r = r_0 + x$, where $x$ is small. We Taylor-expand $q(r)$ around $r_0$: $q(r) \\approx q(r_0) + q'(r_0) (r-r_0) = \\frac{m}{n} + q'(r_0) x$.\n        *   Substitute this into the expression for $k_{\\parallel}$:\n            $m - n q(r) \\approx m - n \\left( \\frac{m}{n} + q'(r_0) x \\right) = -n q'(r_0) x$.\n        *   So, $k_{\\parallel}(x) \\propto \\frac{B_\\theta}{r_0} (-n q'(r_0) x)$. For large-aspect-ratio, $B_\\theta \\approx B_p$ and $B_\\phi \\approx B_T \\approx B$. The poloidal wavenumber is $k_y \\equiv k_\\theta = m/r$.\n        *   Let's refine the expression. The canonical definition of $k_\\parallel$ is $(\\mathbf{k} \\cdot \\mathbf{B})/B$. With $\\mathbf{k}$ being the wavevector, the parallel component is $k_\\parallel = \\mathbf{k} \\cdot \\mathbf{b} = k_\\theta b_\\theta + k_\\phi b_\\phi = \\frac{m}{r} \\frac{B_\\theta}{B} - \\frac{n}{R} \\frac{B_\\phi}{B}$.\n        *   Using $q = r B_\\phi / (R B_\\theta)$, this becomes $k_\\parallel = \\frac{m}{r} \\frac{B_\\theta}{B} - \\frac{n}{R} \\frac{q R B_\\theta}{r B} = \\frac{B_\\theta}{rB} (m-nq)$.\n        *   Again, near $r=r_0$, $q(r) \\approx q(r_0) + q' x = m/n + q' x$.\n        *   $k_\\parallel(x) \\approx \\frac{B_\\theta(r_0)}{r_0 B} (m - n(m/n + q'x)) = -\\frac{B_\\theta n q'}{r_0 B} x$.\n        *   Now let's introduce the magnetic shear parameter $\\hat{s}$. The standard definition is $\\hat{s} = \\frac{r}{q} \\frac{dq}{dr}$. So, $q' = \\frac{q \\hat{s}}{r}$.\n        *   Substitute this into the expression for $k_\\parallel$ at $r_0$:\n            $k_\\parallel(x) \\approx - \\frac{B_\\theta n}{r_0 B} \\frac{q_0 \\hat{s}}{r_0} x$. Since $q_0 = m/n$, we have $n = m/q_0$.\n            $k_\\parallel(x) \\approx - \\frac{B_\\theta}{r_0 B} \\frac{m}{q_0} \\frac{q_0 \\hat{s}}{r_0} x = - \\frac{m B_\\theta \\hat{s}}{r_0^2 B} x$.\n        *   The poloidal wavenumber is $k_y \\approx m/r_0$. In the large-aspect-ratio circular tokamak approximation, $B_\\theta / B \\approx r / (q R)$.\n            $k_\\parallel(x) \\approx - \\frac{m}{r_0} \\frac{\\hat{s}}{r_0} \\frac{B_\\theta}{B} x \\approx - k_y \\frac{\\hat{s}}{r_0} \\frac{r_0}{q_0 R} x = - \\frac{k_y \\hat{s}}{q_0 R} x$.\n        *   So, the magnitude is $|k_{\\parallel}(x)| \\sim \\frac{k_{y}\\,\\hat{s}}{q\\,R}\\,|x|$. This demonstrates that $k_{\\parallel}$ is zero at the rational surface ($x=0$) and grows linearly with the distance $x$ from it. The rate of this growth is proportional to the magnetic shear parameter $\\hat{s}$.\n        *   **Stabilization mechanism:** The field-line bending energy density is a stabilizing term in the MHD potential energy $\\delta W$, and it is proportional to $B^{2}k_{\\parallel}^{2}|\\boldsymbol{\\xi}|^{2}$. Since $k_{\\parallel}^{2} \\propto x^2$, this energy term grows quadratically with distance from the rational surface. This steep energy well confines the mode radially around $x=0$. A larger shear $\\hat{s}$ means $k_{\\parallel}$ grows more rapidly with $x$, leading to a stronger confining effect and greater stabilization for a given pressure gradient.\n\n    2.  **Option-by-Option Analysis:**\n\n        *   **Option A:** \"In a tokamak, magnetic shear, quantified by $\\hat{s}\\equiv (r/q)\\,\\mathrm{d}q/\\mathrm{d}r$, causes $k_{\\parallel}$ of an interchange-like perturbation to grow linearly with $x$, as $k_{\\parallel}\\sim (k_{y}\\,\\hat{s}\\,x)/(q\\,R)$, thereby increasing the field-line bending energy proportional to $B^{2}k_{\\parallel}^{2}|\\boldsymbol{\\xi}|^{2}$ and stabilizing ideal interchange or ballooning modes at fixed pressure gradient.\"\n            *   The definition of the dimensionless magnetic shear parameter, $\\hat{s}\\equiv (r/q)\\,\\mathrm{d}q/\\mathrm{d}r$, is correct.\n            *   The dependence of the parallel wavenumber on the distance $x$ from the rational surface, $k_{\\parallel}\\sim (k_{y}\\,\\hat{s}\\,x)/(q\\,R)$, is correct as derived above.\n            *   The physical mechanism described, an increase in field-line bending energy $\\propto B^{2}k_{\\parallel}^{2}|\\boldsymbol{\\xi}|^{2}$ leading to stabilization, is also correct.\n            *   **Verdict on A:** Correct.\n\n        *   **Option B:** \"Magnetic shear is quantified by $\\alpha\\equiv -\\left(R\\,q^{2}/B^{2}\\right)\\,\\mathrm{d}p/\\mathrm{d}r$, and increasing $\\alpha$ always reduces curvature drive and stabilizes interchange. The effect of shear is to decrease $k_{\\parallel}$ and lower the field-line bending energy.\"\n            *   The parameter $\\alpha \\equiv - (2\\mu_0 R q^2/B^2) dp/dr$ (with a factor of $2\\mu_0$) is the normalized pressure gradient, which characterizes the *drive* for ballooning and interchange modes. It is not a measure of magnetic shear.\n            *   Since $\\alpha$ is proportional to the pressure gradient ($-\\mathrm{d}p/\\mathrm{d}r$), increasing $\\alpha$ *increases* the instability drive, making the plasma more unstable, not more stable.\n            *   The statement that shear decreases $k_{\\parallel}$ and lowers the field-line bending energy is the opposite of the actual stabilization mechanism.\n            *   **Verdict on B:** Incorrect.\n\n        *   **Option C:** \"Magnetic shear is measured by $S\\equiv B^{-1}\\,\\mathrm{d}B/\\mathrm{d}r$, and stronger shear lowers $k_{\\parallel}$ everywhere, reducing the line-bending contribution to the ideal MHD energy and stabilizing interchange.\"\n            *   The quantity $B^{-1}\\,\\mathrm{d}B/\\mathrm{d}r$ represents the magnetic field gradient length scale. It is not the definition of magnetic shear, which is related to the gradient of the field line pitch, $q'(r)$.\n            *   The claim that stronger shear lowers $k_{\\parallel}$ and reduces the line-bending energy contribution is physically incorrect. As established, shear increases the magnitude of $k_{\\parallel}$ away from the rational surface.\n            *   **Verdict on C:** Incorrect.\n\n        *   **Option D:** \"In resistive Magnetohydrodynamics (MHD), shear stabilizes ideal modes by enhancing resistive diffusion; the appropriate shear parameter is $S\\equiv \\eta/(4\\pi)$, with $\\eta$ the resistivity, and larger $S$ suppresses interchange-like disturbances by dissipating magnetic energy.\"\n            *   The problem is explicitly framed within **ideal** MHD. This option incorrectly invokes **resistive** MHD.\n            *   The statement that shear stabilizes ideal modes by enhancing resistive diffusion is a confusion of concepts. Resistivity allows for new classes of instabilities (e.g., tearing modes) by relaxing the \"frozen-in\" constraint of ideal MHD. It does not stabilize ideal modes.\n            *   The definition of the parameter $S$ is non-standard for magnetic shear; it is related to resistivity $\\eta$. The Lundquist number $S = \\tau_R / \\tau_A$, which involves $\\eta$, is a key parameter in resistive MHD, but it is not a measure of magnetic shear.\n            *   **Verdict on D:** Incorrect.\n\nConclusion: Only option A provides a correct definition of the magnetic shear parameter, a correct description of its effect on the parallel wavenumber, and a correct explanation of the resulting stabilization mechanism within the framework of ideal MHD."}]</wiki_content3.1>

<wiki_content3.2>title: What is the total magnetic energy stored in the field of a typical tokamak fusion reactor during a plasma discharge?

Question: A tokamak is a toroidal (doughnut-shaped) device designed for magnetic confinement fusion research. In a tokamak, a hot plasma is contained by strong magnetic fields to prevent it from touching the reactor walls. The primary field component responsible for this confinement is the toroidal magnetic field, which runs the long way around the torus.

For a typical large-scale experimental tokamak, we can model the plasma volume as a perfect torus with a major radius $R$ and a minor radius $r$. For the purpose of this estimation problem, make the simplifying assumption that the toroidal magnetic field, with magnitude $B$, is uniform throughout this entire plasma volume.

Using the parameters provided below, estimate the total magnetic energy stored within the plasma volume during a discharge.

**Given Parameters:**
- Major radius of the torus, $R = 5.0 \text{ m}$
- Minor radius of the torus, $r = 1.5 \text{ m}$
- Magnitude of the toroidal magnetic field, $B = 8.0 \text{ T}$
- Permeability of free space, $\mu_0 = 4\pi \times 10^{-7} \text{ T}\cdot\text{m/A}$

Express your final answer in megajoules (MJ), rounded to two significant figures.



Answer: [{"answer": "$$\\boxed{5700}$$", "solution": "The problem asks for an estimation of the total magnetic energy stored in the toroidal volume of a tokamak's plasma.\n\nFirst, we identify the physical principle governing magnetic energy. The energy density, $u_B$, of a magnetic field with magnitude $B$ in a vacuum (or a low-density plasma) is given by the formula:\n$$u_B = \\frac{B^2}{2\\mu_0}$$\nwhere $\\mu_0$ is the permeability of free space.\n\nNext, we need to determine the volume, $V$, over which this energy is stored. The problem states that we should model the plasma volume as a perfect torus with major radius $R$ and minor radius $r$. The volume of a torus can be found by imagining it as a cylinder of radius $r$ and length equal to the circumference of the major circle, $2\\pi R$. The cross-sectional area of this cylinder is $A = \\pi r^2$. Therefore, the volume of the torus is:\n$$V = A \\times (2\\pi R) = (\\pi r^2)(2\\pi R) = 2\\pi^2 R r^2$$\n\nThe problem makes the simplifying assumption that the magnetic field $B$ is uniform throughout this volume. Under this assumption, the total magnetic energy, $U_B$, is the product of the uniform energy density $u_B$ and the total volume $V$:\n$$U_B = u_B \\times V$$\n\nSubstituting the expressions for $u_B$ and $V$, we get a symbolic expression for the total energy:\n$$U_B = \\left(\\frac{B^2}{2\\mu_0}\\right) \\times \\left(2\\pi^2 R r^2\\right)$$\nWe can simplify this expression by canceling a factor of 2:\n$$U_B = \\frac{\\pi^2 B^2 R r^2}{\\mu_0}$$\n\nNow, we substitute the given numerical values into this expression:\n- $R = 5.0 \\text{ m}$\n- $r = 1.5 \\text{ m}$\n- $B = 8.0 \\text{ T}$\n- $\\mu_0 = 4\\pi \\times 10^{-7} \\text{ T}\\cdot\\text{m/A}$\n\n$$U_B = \\frac{\\pi^2 (8.0 \\text{ T})^2 (5.0 \\text{ m}) (1.5 \\text{ m})^2}{4\\pi \\times 10^{-7} \\text{ T}\\cdot\\text{m/A}}$$\n\nLet's simplify the expression before calculating the final numerical value. We can cancel one factor of $\\pi$:\n$$U_B = \\frac{\\pi (8.0)^2 (5.0) (1.5)^2}{4 \\times 10^{-7}} \\text{ J}$$\n$$U_B = \\frac{\\pi (64) (5.0) (2.25)}{4 \\times 10^{-7}} \\text{ J}$$\n$$U_B = \\frac{720 \\pi}{4 \\times 10^{-7}} \\text{ J}$$\n$$U_B = 180 \\pi \\times 10^7 \\text{ J}$$\n$$U_B = 1.8 \\pi \\times 10^9 \\text{ J}$$\n\nNow we calculate the numerical value:\n$$U_B \\approx (1.8) \\times (3.14159...) \\times 10^9 \\text{ J}$$\n$$U_B \\approx 5.65486... \\times 10^9 \\text{ J}$$\n\nThe problem asks for the answer in megajoules (MJ). Since $1 \\text{ MJ} = 10^6 \\text{ J}$, we convert our result:\n$$U_B = \\frac{5.65486... \\times 10^9 \\text{ J}}{10^6 \\text{ J/MJ}} = 5.65486... \\times 10^3 \\text{ MJ}$$\n$$U_B \\approx 5654.86... \\text{ MJ}$$\n\nFinally, we round the answer to two significant figures as requested:\n$$U_B \\approx 5700 \\text{ MJ}$$"}, {"answer": "$$\\boxed{5.7 \\times 10^{3}}$$", "solution": "Magnetic energy density in a static magnetic field is given by the standard electromagnetic relation\n$$\nu=\\frac{B^{2}}{2\\mu_{0}}.\n$$\nThe total magnetic energy is the volume integral of the energy density. With the simplifying assumption of a uniform field of magnitude $B$ over the whole plasma volume $V$, this reduces to\n$$\nU=\\int_{V}u\\,\\mathrm{d}V=\\frac{B^{2}}{2\\mu_{0}}\\,V.\n$$\nModeling the plasma as a torus with major radius $R$ and minor radius $r$, its volume is\n$$\nV=2\\pi^{2}Rr^{2}.\n$$\nSubstituting this into the energy expression gives the symbolic result\n$$\nU=\\frac{B^{2}}{2\\mu_{0}}\\left(2\\pi^{2}Rr^{2}\\right)=\\frac{B^{2}\\pi^{2}Rr^{2}}{\\mu_{0}}.\n$$\nNow substitute the given numerical values $R=5.0\\ \\text{m}$, $r=1.5\\ \\text{m}$, $B=8.0\\ \\text{T}$, and $\\mu_{0}=4\\pi\\times 10^{-7}\\ \\text{T}\\cdot\\text{m/A}$:\n$$\nU=\\frac{(8.0)^{2}\\pi^{2}(5.0)(1.5)^{2}}{4\\pi\\times 10^{-7}}\\ \\text{J}.\n$$\nCompute the products in the numerator:\n$$\n(8.0)^{2}=64,\\quad (1.5)^{2}=2.25,\\quad 64\\times 5.0\\times 2.25=64\\times 11.25=720,\n$$\nso\n$$\nU=\\frac{720\\pi^{2}}{4\\pi\\times 10^{-7}}\\ \\text{J}=\\frac{720}{4}\\cdot\\frac{\\pi}{10^{-7}}\\ \\text{J}=180\\pi\\times 10^{7}\\ \\text{J}.\n$$\nConvert joules to megajoules using $1\\ \\text{MJ}=10^{6}\\ \\text{J}$:\n$$\nU=180\\pi\\times 10^{7}\\ \\text{J}=180\\pi\\times 10^{1}\\ \\text{MJ}=1800\\pi\\ \\text{MJ}.\n$$\nNumerically, $1800\\pi\\ \\text{MJ}\\approx 5.6549\\times 10^{3}\\ \\text{MJ}$. Rounding to two significant figures gives\n$$\nU\\approx 5.7\\times 10^{3}\\ \\text{MJ}.\n$$"}]


title: For an axisymmetric plasma in magnetohydrostatic equilibrium, derive the Grad-Shafranov equation that governs the poloidal magnetic flux function...

Question: An idealized, infinitely conducting plasma in a static, axisymmetric equilibrium is described by the equations of magnetohydrostatics. In a cylindrical coordinate system $(R, \phi, z)$, with the axis of symmetry being the $z$-axis, the governing equations are:
1.  Force balance: $\nabla p = \mathbf{J} \times \mathbf{B}$
2.  Ampere's law (for steady state): $\mu_0 \mathbf{J} = \nabla \times \mathbf{B}$
3.  Gauss's law for magnetism: $\nabla \cdot \mathbf{B} = 0$

Here, $p$ is the plasma pressure, $\mathbf{J}$ is the current density, $\mathbf{B}$ is the magnetic field, and $\mu_0$ is the permeability of free space.

For an axisymmetric system ($\partial/\partial\phi = 0$), the magnetic field can be expressed as $\mathbf{B} = \mathbf{B}_p + B_\phi \hat{\boldsymbol{\phi}}$, where $\mathbf{B}_p$ is the poloidal field (in the $R-z$ plane) and $B_\phi$ is the toroidal field. The condition $\nabla \cdot \mathbf{B} = 0$ is satisfied by introducing a poloidal magnetic flux function, $\psi(R, z)$, such that the poloidal field is given by $\mathbf{B}_p = \frac{1}{R} \nabla \psi \times \hat{\boldsymbol{\phi}}$.

It can be shown from the governing equations that for a static equilibrium, both the pressure $p$ and the quantity $F \equiv R B_\phi$ must be functions of the poloidal flux $\psi$ alone. Consider a specific equilibrium where these functions are given by:
-   $p(\psi) = p_0 + p_1 \psi + \frac{1}{2} p_2 \psi^2$
-   $F(\psi) = F_0 + F_1 \psi$

where $p_0, p_1, p_2, F_0,$ and $F_1$ are constants.

The equilibrium configuration of this plasma is governed by the Grad-Shafranov equation, a second-order partial differential equation for $\psi(R,z)$. This equation has the general form:
$$
\Delta^* \psi = -S(R, \psi)
$$
where $\Delta^* \psi \equiv R^2 \nabla \cdot \left(\frac{1}{R^2} \nabla \psi\right)$ is the Grad-Shafranov operator, and $S(R, \psi)$ is a source term that depends on the pressure and toroidal field profiles.

Derive the explicit expression for the source term $S(R, \psi)$ for the given functional forms of $p(\psi)$ and $F(\psi)$.



Answer: [{"answer": "$$ \\boxed{(\\mu_0 R^2 p_1 + F_0 F_1) + (\\mu_0 R^2 p_2 + F_1^2)\\psi} $$", "solution": "The derivation begins with the fundamental equations of magnetohydrostatic equilibrium. The goal is to combine these equations to find an expression for the toroidal current density, $J_\\phi$, in two different ways, and then equate them to obtain the desired equation for $\\psi$.\n\n**1. Expressing the current density $\\mathbf{J}$ in terms of $\\psi$ and $F(\\psi)$**\n\nFrom Ampere's Law, $\\mu_0 \\mathbf{J} = \\nabla \\times \\mathbf{B}$. We decompose the magnetic field into its poloidal and toroidal components: $\\mathbf{B} = \\mathbf{B}_p + B_\\phi \\hat{\\boldsymbol{\\phi}}$.\nThe current density is then $\\mathbf{J} = \\frac{1}{\\mu_0} \\left( \\nabla \\times \\mathbf{B}_p + \\nabla \\times (B_\\phi \\hat{\\boldsymbol{\\phi}}) \\right)$.\n\nThe term $\\nabla \\times \\mathbf{B}_p$ gives the toroidal component of the current, $J_\\phi \\hat{\\boldsymbol{\\phi}}$. The term $\\nabla \\times (B_\\phi \\hat{\\boldsymbol{\\phi}})$ gives the poloidal component of the current, $\\mathbf{J}_p$.\n\nLet's first find the toroidal current, $J_\\phi$.\n$J_\\phi \\hat{\\boldsymbol{\\phi}} = \\frac{1}{\\mu_0} (\\nabla \\times \\mathbf{B}_p)$.\nGiven $\\mathbf{B}_p = \\frac{1}{R} \\nabla \\psi \\times \\hat{\\boldsymbol{\\phi}} = \\frac{1}{R} \\left( \\frac{\\partial \\psi}{\\partial R} \\hat{\\mathbf{R}} + \\frac{\\partial \\psi}{\\partial z} \\hat{\\mathbf{z}} \\right) \\times \\hat{\\boldsymbol{\\phi}} = \\frac{1}{R} \\frac{\\partial \\psi}{\\partial R} (\\hat{\\mathbf{R}} \\times \\hat{\\boldsymbol{\\phi}}) + \\frac{1}{R} \\frac{\\partial \\psi}{\\partial z} (\\hat{\\mathbf{z}} \\times \\hat{\\boldsymbol{\\phi}})$.\nUsing $\\hat{\\mathbf{R}} \\times \\hat{\\boldsymbol{\\phi}} = \\hat{\\mathbf{z}}$ and $\\hat{\\mathbf{z}} \\times \\hat{\\boldsymbol{\\phi}} = -\\hat{\\mathbf{R}}$, we get:\n$B_R = -\\frac{1}{R} \\frac{\\partial \\psi}{\\partial z}$ and $B_z = \\frac{1}{R} \\frac{\\partial \\psi}{\\partial R}$.\n\nThe toroidal component of the curl in cylindrical coordinates is $(\\nabla \\times \\mathbf{B}_p)_\\phi = \\frac{\\partial B_R}{\\partial z} - \\frac{\\partial B_z}{\\partial R}$.\n$$\n\\mu_0 J_\\phi = \\frac{\\partial}{\\partial z}\\left(-\\frac{1}{R} \\frac{\\partial \\psi}{\\partial z}\\right) - \\frac{\\partial}{\\partial R}\\left(\\frac{1}{R} \\frac{\\partial \\psi}{\\partial R}\\right)\n$$\n$$\n\\mu_0 J_\\phi = -\\frac{1}{R} \\frac{\\partial^2 \\psi}{\\partial z^2} - \\left(-\\frac{1}{R^2}\\frac{\\partial \\psi}{\\partial R} + \\frac{1}{R}\\frac{\\partial^2 \\psi}{\\partial R^2}\\right) = -\\frac{1}{R} \\left( \\frac{\\partial^2 \\psi}{\\partial R^2} - \\frac{1}{R}\\frac{\\partial \\psi}{\\partial R} + \\frac{\\partial^2 \\psi}{\\partial z^2} \\right)\n$$\nThe expression in the parenthesis is the Grad-Shafranov operator $\\Delta^* \\psi = \\frac{\\partial^2 \\psi}{\\partial R^2} - \\frac{1}{R}\\frac{\\partial \\psi}{\\partial R} + \\frac{\\partial^2 \\psi}{\\partial z^2}$.\nThus, we have our first expression for $J_\\phi$:\n$$\nJ_\\phi = -\\frac{1}{\\mu_0 R} \\Delta^* \\psi\n$$\n\n**2. Deriving $J_\\phi$ from the force balance equation**\n\nThe force balance equation is $\\nabla p = \\mathbf{J} \\times \\mathbf{B}$. We are given that $p=p(\\psi)$, so $\\nabla p = \\frac{dp}{d\\psi} \\nabla\\psi$.\nThe Lorentz force is $\\mathbf{J} \\times \\mathbf{B} = (J_\\phi \\hat{\\boldsymbol{\\phi}} + \\mathbf{J}_p) \\times (B_\\phi \\hat{\\boldsymbol{\\phi}} + \\mathbf{B}_p)$.\nExpanding this gives:\n$$\n\\mathbf{J} \\times \\mathbf{B} = J_\\phi (\\hat{\\boldsymbol{\\phi}} \\times \\mathbf{B}_p) + (\\mathbf{J}_p \\times \\hat{\\boldsymbol{\\phi}}) B_\\phi + \\mathbf{J}_p \\times \\mathbf{B}_p + J_\\phi B_\\phi (\\hat{\\boldsymbol{\\phi}} \\times \\hat{\\boldsymbol{\\phi}})\n$$\nThe last term is zero. Taking the toroidal component of the force balance equation gives $(\\mathbf{J} \\times \\mathbf{B})_\\phi = 0$, which implies $\\mathbf{J}_p \\times \\mathbf{B}_p = 0$. So the poloidal current is parallel to the poloidal magnetic field.\nThe force balance equation simplifies to a poloidal equation:\n$$\n\\nabla p = J_\\phi (\\hat{\\boldsymbol{\\phi}} \\times \\mathbf{B}_p) + B_\\phi (\\mathbf{J}_p \\times \\hat{\\boldsymbol{\\phi}})\n$$\nWe need expressions for the vector products.\n$\\hat{\\boldsymbol{\\phi}} \\times \\mathbf{B}_p = \\hat{\\boldsymbol{\\phi}} \\times \\left(\\frac{1}{R} \\nabla\\psi \\times \\hat{\\boldsymbol{\\phi}}\\right)$. Using the vector identity $\\mathbf{a}\\times(\\mathbf{b}\\times\\mathbf{a}) = \\mathbf{b}(\\mathbf{a}\\cdot\\mathbf{a}) - \\mathbf{a}(\\mathbf{a}\\cdot\\mathbf{b})$, with $\\mathbf{a}=\\hat{\\boldsymbol{\\phi}}$ and $\\mathbf{b}=\\nabla\\psi$:\n$\\hat{\\boldsymbol{\\phi}} \\times \\mathbf{B}_p = \\frac{1}{R} \\left[ \\nabla\\psi (\\hat{\\boldsymbol{\\phi}}\\cdot\\hat{\\boldsymbol{\\phi}}) - \\hat{\\boldsymbol{\\phi}}(\\hat{\\boldsymbol{\\phi}}\\cdot\\nabla\\psi) \\right]$. Since $\\nabla\\psi$ is poloidal, $\\hat{\\boldsymbol{\\phi}} \\cdot \\nabla\\psi = 0$. Thus, $\\hat{\\boldsymbol{\\phi}} \\times \\mathbf{B}_p = \\frac{1}{R}\\nabla\\psi$.\n\nNext, we relate $\\mathbf{J}_p$ to $F(\\psi)$.\n$\\mu_0 \\mathbf{J}_p = \\nabla \\times (B_\\phi \\hat{\\boldsymbol{\\phi}}) = \\nabla \\times (\\frac{F(\\psi)}{R} \\hat{\\boldsymbol{\\phi}})$.\nUsing the identity $\\nabla\\times(f\\mathbf{A}) = f(\\nabla\\times\\mathbf{A}) + (\\nabla f) \\times \\mathbf{A}$, with $f=F/R$ and $\\mathbf{A}=\\hat{\\boldsymbol{\\phi}}$:\n$\\nabla \\times (\\frac{F}{R} \\hat{\\boldsymbol{\\phi}}) = \\frac{F}{R}(\\nabla \\times \\hat{\\boldsymbol{\\phi}}) + \\nabla(\\frac{F}{R}) \\times \\hat{\\boldsymbol{\\phi}}$.\n$\\nabla \\times \\hat{\\boldsymbol{\\phi}} = \\frac{1}{R}\\hat{\\mathbf{z}}$ and $\\nabla(\\frac{F}{R}) \\times \\hat{\\boldsymbol{\\phi}} = \\left( \\frac{\\partial(F/R)}{\\partial R}\\hat{\\mathbf{R}} + \\frac{\\partial(F/R)}{\\partial z}\\hat{\\mathbf{z}} \\right) \\times \\hat{\\boldsymbol{\\phi}} = -\\frac{\\partial(F/R)}{\\partial R}\\hat{\\mathbf{z}} + \\frac{\\partial(F/R)}{\\partial z}\\hat{\\mathbf{R}}$. This seems overly complicated.\nA more direct path is: $\\mu_0 \\mathbf{J} = \\nabla\\times\\mathbf{B} = \\nabla \\times (\\frac{1}{R}\\nabla\\psi \\times \\hat{\\boldsymbol{\\phi}} + \\frac{F}{R}\\hat{\\boldsymbol{\\phi}})$. From this, it can be shown that $\\mu_0 \\mathbf{J} = -\\frac{\\Delta^*\\psi}{R}\\hat{\\boldsymbol{\\phi}} + \\frac{1}{R}\\nabla F \\times \\hat{\\boldsymbol{\\phi}}$.\nSo, $\\mathbf{J}_p = \\frac{1}{\\mu_0 R} \\nabla F \\times \\hat{\\boldsymbol{\\phi}} = \\frac{1}{\\mu_0 R}\\frac{dF}{d\\psi}\\nabla\\psi \\times \\hat{\\boldsymbol{\\phi}}$.\nSubstituting into the force equation:\n$$\n\\frac{dp}{d\\psi}\\nabla\\psi = J_\\phi \\left(\\frac{1}{R}\\nabla\\psi\\right) + \\frac{F}{R} \\left( \\frac{1}{\\mu_0 R} \\frac{dF}{d\\psi}\\nabla\\psi \\times \\hat{\\boldsymbol{\\phi}} \\right) \\times \\hat{\\boldsymbol{\\phi}}\n$$\n$(\\nabla\\psi \\times \\hat{\\boldsymbol{\\phi}}) \\times \\hat{\\boldsymbol{\\phi}} = -\\nabla\\psi$.\n$$\n\\frac{dp}{d\\psi}\\nabla\\psi = \\frac{J_\\phi}{R}\\nabla\\psi - \\frac{F}{\\mu_0 R^2}\\frac{dF}{d\\psi}\\nabla\\psi\n$$\nSince this must hold everywhere, we can remove the common factor of $\\nabla\\psi$:\n$$\n\\frac{dp}{d\\psi} = \\frac{J_\\phi}{R} - \\frac{F}{\\mu_0 R^2}\\frac{dF}{d\\psi}\n$$\nSolving for $J_\\phi$ gives our second expression:\n$$\nJ_\\phi = R\\frac{dp}{d\\psi} + \\frac{F}{\\mu_0 R}\\frac{dF}{d\\psi}\n$$\n\n**3. Combine expressions and find the source term $S(R, \\psi)$**\n\nEquating the two expressions for $J_\\phi$:\n$$\n-\\frac{1}{\\mu_0 R} \\Delta^* \\psi = R\\frac{dp}{d\\psi} + \\frac{F}{\\mu_0 R}\\frac{dF}{d\\psi}\n$$\nMultiply the entire equation by $-\\mu_0 R$:\n$$\n\\Delta^* \\psi = -\\mu_0 R^2 \\frac{dp}{d\\psi} - F\\frac{dF}{d\\psi}\n$$\nBy comparing this to the given form $\\Delta^* \\psi = -S(R, \\psi)$, we identify the source term as:\n$$\nS(R, \\psi) = \\mu_0 R^2 \\frac{dp}{d\\psi} + F \\frac{dF}{d\\psi}\n$$\n\n**4.
