Assuming that $\\psi$ is a dimensionless coordinate (a common convention in optimization codes) renders the problem fully specified and allows for a rigorous evaluation of the options. Without this standard interpretation, none of the options might be perfectly dimensionally consistent, but the physical errors in options B, C, and D are so significant that they can be dismissed regardless.\n\n### Step 3: Verdict and Action\nThe problem statement is **valid**. It represents a conceptually sound problem in computational fusion science. I will proceed with the solution, adopting the standard interpretation that $\\psi$ is treated as a dimensionless radial coordinate for the purpose of constructing the figure of merit.\n\n## Derivation and Option Analysis\n\nThe stability of a plasma against ideal interchange modes is determined by the sign of the change in potential energy, $\\delta W$, resulting from a displacement. For stability, $\\delta W > 0$. The Mercier criterion is a condition, derived from $\\delta W > 0$, that must be satisfied on every rational flux surface for the plasma to be stable to localized interchange modes. A figure of merit for optimization, $\\mathcal{M}(\\psi)$, should therefore be constructed such that $\\mathcal{M}(\\psi) > 0$ corresponds to stability.\n\nThe core physical effects contributing to interchange stability are:\n\n1.  **Magnetic Shear (Stabilizing):** The variation of the rotational transform with radius, or shear, twists the magnetic field lines. An interchange of two fluid parcels must bend these field lines, which requires energy. This stabilizing effect is typically proportional to the square of the shear, $s^2 = (d\\iota/d\\ln\\psi)^2$, as the sign of the shear is irrelevant for the stability of ideal interchange modes. Therefore, $\\mathcal{M}(\\psi)$ should contain a positive term proportional to $s(\\psi)^2$.\n\n2.  **Magnetic Well (Stabilizing):** A magnetic well, defined by $V''(\\psi) < 0$, means that the volume per unit flux increases as one moves outwards. When a plasma parcel is displaced outwards into a region of weaker magnetic field, it expands, doing work on the magnetic field. In a magnetic well configuration, this process requires more energy than is released by moving down the pressure gradient, leading to stability. The stabilizing effect is proportional to $-V''(\\psi)$. Thus, $\\mathcal{M}(\\psi)$ should contain a positive term that increases as $V''(\\psi)$ becomes more negative.\n\n3.  **Pressure-Curvature Drive (Destabilizing):** In regions of \"unfavorable\" or \"bad\" curvature (where the magnetic field lines are convex as viewed from the plasma, i.e., $\\kappa_n > 0$), an outward displacement moves the plasma to a region where it can expand and release energy. This drive is proportional to the pressure gradient, $|p'(\\psi)| = -p'(\\psi)$, and the magnitude of the unfavorable curvature. Therefore, $\\mathcal{M}(\\psi)$ must contain a negative term proportional to $|p'(\\psi)| \\langle \\max(0, \\kappa_n) \\rangle$.\n\nCombining these elements, a dimensionless figure of merit should have the schematic form:\n$$\n\\mathcal{M}(\\psi) \\sim (\\text{Shear})^2 + (\\text{Magnetic Well}) - (\\text{Pressure Gradient} \\times \\text{Bad Curvature})\n$$\n\n### Dimensional Analysis\nWe assume, as is standard in computational practice, that $\\psi$ is a dimensionless coordinate, e.g., normalized by the total flux.\n-   $\\psi$: dimensionless. $\\Delta\\psi$: dimensionless.\n-   $s(\\psi) = d\\iota/d\\ln\\psi$: dimensionless.\n-   $p(\\psi)$: has units of pressure $[P]$.\n-   $p'(\\psi) = dp/d\\psi$: has units of pressure $[P]$ or energy density $[E/L^3]$.\n-   $\\langle B^2 \\rangle$: has units of energy density $[E/L^3]$.\n-   $V(\\psi)$: has units of volume $[L^3]$.\n-   $V'(\\psi) = dV/d\\psi$: has units of volume $[L^3]$.\n-   $V''(\\psi) = d^2V/d\\psi^2$: has units of volume $[L^3]$.\n-   $\\kappa_n$: has units of inverse length $[L^{-1}]$.\n-   $L_\\perp$: has units of length $[L]$.\n\n### Option-by-Option Analysis\n\n**A. The Mercier criterion is evaluated on each flux surface by balancing pressure-curvature drive against stabilizing magnetic shear and magnetic well; in optimization, one penalizes surfaces where the stability balance is negative and rewards those where it is positive. A consistent dimensionless figure of merit is\n$$\n\\mathcal{M}(\\psi) \\;=\\; \\big\\langle s(\\psi)^2 \\big\\rangle \\;+\\; \\alpha_w \\left(-\\frac{V''(\\psi)}{V'(\\psi)}\\,\\Delta\\psi\\right) \\;-\\; \\alpha_c \\,\\frac{|p'(\\psi)|}{\\langle B^2 \\rangle}\\,\\big\\langle \\max\\!\\big(0,\\kappa_n\\big) \\big\\rangle\\,L_{\\perp},\n$$\nwith positive weights $\\alpha_w$ and $\\alpha_c$, so that magnetic well and shear are stabilizing and bad normal curvature weighted by the pressure gradient is destabilizing; in optimization one maximizes the minimum of $\\mathcal{M}(\\psi)$ over $\\psi$.**\n\n-   **Description:** The description of the balancing act and the optimization strategy (penalizing negative stability, which is equivalent to maximizing the minimum value of the stability metric) is entirely correct and standard practice.\n-   **Physics of Formula:**\n    -   $\\big\\langle s(\\psi)^2 \\big\\rangle$: Correctly models shear stabilization. It is positive and increases with shear.\n    -   $\\alpha_w \\left(-\\frac{V''(\\psi)}{V'(\\psi)}\\,\\Delta\\psi\\right)$: For a magnetic well, $V''(\\psi) < 0$. Since $V'(\\psi) > 0$ (volume increases with radius), this term is positive and correctly represents stabilization from a magnetic well.\n    -   $-\\alpha_c \\,\\frac{|p'(\\psi)|}{\\langle B^2 \\rangle}\\,\\big\\langle \\max\\!\\big(0,\\kappa_n\\big) \\big\\rangle\\,L_{\\perp}$: This term is negative. It is proportional to the pressure gradient $|p'|$ and the \"bad\" curvature $\\max(0,\\kappa_n)$, correctly modeling the destabilizing drive.\n-   **Dimensionality (with dimensionless $\\psi$):**\n    -   $\\big\\langle s(\\psi)^2 \\big\\rangle$: dimensionless.\n    -   $-\\frac{V''(\\psi)}{V'(\\psi)}\\,\\Delta\\psi$: $[L^3]/[L^3] \\times (\\text{dimensionless}) = \\text{dimensionless}$.\n    -   $\\frac{|p'(\\psi)|}{\\langle B^2 \\rangle}\\,\\big\\langle \\max\\!\\big(0,\\kappa_n\\big) \\big\\rangle\\,L_{\\perp}$: $[E/L^3]/[E/L^3] \\times [L^{-1}] \\times [L] = \\text{dimensionless}$.\n-   **Conclusion:** All aspects of this option—the description of the methodology, the physical correctness of the figure of merit, and its dimensional consistency (under a standard interpretation)—are correct.\n\n**Verdict: Correct.**\n\n**B. The Mercier criterion indicates that magnetic hill stabilizes interchange modes and normal curvature weakens the pressure drive; a suitable figure of merit is\n$$\n\\mathcal{M}(\\psi) \\;=\\; \\big\\langle s(\\psi) \\big\\rangle \\;-\\; \\alpha_w \\left(-\\frac{V''(\\psi)}{V'(\\psi)}\\right) \\;+\\; \\alpha_c \\,\\frac{|p'(\\psi)|}{\\langle B^2 \\rangle}\\,\\big\\langle \\min\\!\\big(0,\\kappa_n\\big) \\big\\rangle,\n$$\nwhich increases with magnetic hill and decreases with magnetic well, and treats favorable curvature as destabilizing while ignoring dimensional consistency.**\n\n-   **Description:** The statement \"magnetic hill stabilizes interchange modes\" is fundamentally incorrect. Magnetic well ($V''<0$) is stabilizing; magnetic hill ($V''>0$) is destabilizing.\n-   **Physics of Formula:** The formula uses $\\langle s(\\psi)\\rangle$ instead of $s(\\psi)^2$, which is physically incorrect for ideal interchange modes. The well/hill term $-\\alpha_w (-V''/V') = \\alpha_w V''/V'$ is positive for a destabilizing hill ($V''>0$) and negative for a stabilizing well ($V''<0$), rewarding instability. The curvature term uses $\\min(0, \\kappa_n)$, which is \"favorable\" curvature, and adds it with a positive sign, implying it is destabilizing. The physics is completely inverted.\n-   **Dimensionality:** The term $-\\frac{V''(\\psi)}{V'(\\psi)}$ has units of $[1/\\psi]$. If $\\psi$ has units of flux, this term cannot be added to the dimensionless shear term $\\langle s \\rangle$. The formula is dimensionally inconsistent.\n\n**Verdict: Incorrect.**\n\n**C. In practice, the Mercier criterion is purely local along field lines and independent of the pressure gradient; optimization focuses only on geometric properties, so a figure of merit that omits pressure dependence is\n$$\n\\mathcal{M}(\\psi) \\;=\\; \\alpha_w \\left(-\\frac{V''(\\psi)}{V'(\\psi)}\\,\\Delta\\psi\\right) \\;+\\; \\big\\langle \\kappa_g^2 \\big\\rangle L_{\\perp}^2,\n$$\nwhere $\\kappa_g$ is the geodesic curvature; normal curvature and magnetic shear are unnecessary.**\n\n-   **Description:** The statement that the Mercier criterion is \"independent of the pressure gradient\" is fundamentally false. The interchange instability is driven by the pressure gradient.\n-   **Physics of Formula:** The formula incorrectly omits the pressure-gradient term, which is the primary driver of the instability. It also omits the magnetic shear term, which is a critical stabilizing effect. While geodesic curvature ($\\kappa_g$) does play a role in the full Mercier criterion (related to parallel currents), its isolated inclusion here while omitting the primary terms is incorrect.\n\n**Verdict: Incorrect.**\n\n**D. The Mercier criterion is typically replaced by axisymmetric tokamak ballooning rules in stellarator optimization; a convenient figure of merit is\n$$\n\\mathcal{M}(\\psi) \\;=\\; \\frac{|p'(\\psi)| R_0}{\\langle B^2 \\rangle} \\;-\\; \\big\\langle s(\\psi)^2 \\big\\rangle,\n$$\nwhere $R_0$ is the major radius; magnetic well and normal curvature are not required for interchange stability assessment in stellarators.**\n\n-   **Description:** The premise is incorrect. Stellarators are inherently non-axisymmetric, and while ballooning stability is important, it is assessed using stellarator-specific codes, not \"axisymmetric tokamak ballooning rules\". Furthermore, the Mercier criterion is a distinct and crucial stability test that is certainly not ignored.\n-   **Physics of Formula:** The formula is nonsensical. It omits the magnetic well and curvature effects. Most egregiously, it subtracts the shear term $\\langle s(\\psi)^2 \\rangle$, implying that shear is destabilizing, which is the opposite of its actual role in stabilizing interchange modes.\n-   **Dimensionality:** With a dimensioned flux $\\psi$, the term $\\frac{|p'(\\psi)| R_0}{\\langle B^2 \\rangle}$ has units of $[L/\\psi]$ and cannot be combined with the dimensionless shear term.\n\n**Verdict: Incorrect.**"}]


title: [multiple_choice] Precisely define a stellarator magnetic configuration by stating the conditions $\nabla\cdot B=0$, $\nabla\times B=\mu_0 J$, and $J\times B=\nabla p$ on nested toroidal flux surfaces, and contrast how 3D symmetry absence distinguishes it from axisymmetric tokamak equilibria.

Question: In magnetostatic, single-fluid Magnetohydrodynamics (MHD), the equilibrium of a toroidal plasma is governed by the Maxwell equations $\nabla\cdot\mathbf{B}=0$ and $\nabla\times\mathbf{B}=\mu_0\mathbf{J}$, together with the force balance $\mathbf{J}\times\mathbf{B}=\nabla p$ for scalar pressure $p$. Consider a three-dimensional configuration possessing nested toroidal magnetic flux surfaces labeled by a single-valued surface function $\psi$, with $\psi$ constant on each surface and increasing monotonically radially. Starting from these foundational laws and definitions, reason about the geometric constraints implied by the existence of flux surfaces and the scalar pressure. In particular, determine the relations among $\mathbf{B}$, $\mathbf{J}$, $\nabla p$, and $\nabla\psi$ required for equilibrium on nested surfaces, and use symmetry arguments to contrast such an equilibrium in a stellarator configuration with an axisymmetric tokamak configuration.

Which option most accurately and completely defines a stellarator magnetic configuration and distinguishes it from an axisymmetric tokamak, consistent with the governing laws above and the existence of nested toroidal flux surfaces?

A. A stellarator magnetic configuration is a three-dimensional magnetostatic single-fluid MHD equilibrium with nested toroidal flux surfaces, satisfying $\nabla\cdot\mathbf{B}=0$, $\nabla\times\mathbf{B}=\mu_0\mathbf{J}$, and $\mathbf{J}\times\mathbf{B}=\nabla p$, where $p=p(\psi)$ is a flux function and the surfaces are defined by $\mathbf{B}\cdot\nabla\psi=0$. These imply $\mathbf{B}\cdot\nabla p=0$ and $\mathbf{J}\cdot\nabla\psi=0$ (current tangent to the surfaces, up to any component parallel to $\mathbf{B}$). Unlike an axisymmetric tokamak, the stellarator generally lacks any continuous spatial symmetry (no ignorable coordinate), so the equilibrium cannot be reduced to a two-dimensional Grad–Shafranov form; the rotational transform is provided by three-dimensional coil geometry rather than requiring a net toroidal plasma current, although parallel and bootstrap currents may exist.

B. A stellarator magnetic configuration is a vacuum field with $\mathbf{J}=\mathbf{0}$ so that $\nabla\times\mathbf{B}=\mathbf{0}$, with uniform pressure $\nabla p=\mathbf{0}$, and it is distinguished from a tokamak by using only external coils instead of plasma current. The presence or absence of three-dimensional symmetry does not affect the equilibrium equations because the field is curl-free.

C. A stellarator magnetic configuration satisfies $\nabla\cdot\mathbf{B}=0$, $\nabla\times\mathbf{B}=\mu_0\mathbf{J}$, and $\mathbf{J}\times\mathbf{B}=\nabla p$, but because it lacks axisymmetry, magnetic field lines cannot lie on nested toroidal surfaces, so neither $\mathbf{B}\cdot\nabla\psi=0$ nor $p=p(\psi)$ can hold; therefore the concept of rotational transform or safety factor is undefined in three-dimensional equilibria.

D. Both stellarator and tokamak equilibria satisfy $\nabla\cdot\mathbf{B}=0$, $\nabla\times\mathbf{B}=\mu_0\mathbf{J}$, and $\mathbf{J}\times\mathbf{B}=\nabla p$ on nested toroidal surfaces. In a stellarator, despite three-dimensional shaping, there remains an ignorable coordinate so the equilibrium reduces to a modified Grad–Shafranov equation with an extra source term, and rotational transform always requires a nonzero net toroidal plasma current; otherwise rotational transform cannot exist.

Answer: [{"answer": "$$\\boxed{A}$$", "solution": "Begin with the fundamental laws for a magnetostatic, single-fluid Magnetohydrodynamics (MHD) plasma:\n- $\\nabla\\cdot\\mathbf{B}=0$ (absence of magnetic monopoles),\n- $\\nabla\\times\\mathbf{B}=\\mu_0\\mathbf{J}$ (Ampère’s law),\n- $\\mathbf{J}\\times\\mathbf{B}=\\nabla p$ (force balance with scalar pressure).\n\nAssume the existence of nested toroidal magnetic flux surfaces labeled by $\\psi$, with the defining property that $\\psi$ is constant on each surface. The condition that field lines lie on flux surfaces is expressed by\n$$\n\\mathbf{B}\\cdot\\nabla\\psi=0,\n$$\nmeaning the magnetic field is tangent to the surfaces.\n\nFrom force balance, take the scalar product with $\\mathbf{B}$:\n$$\n\\mathbf{B}\\cdot(\\mathbf{J}\\times\\mathbf{B})=\\mathbf{B}\\cdot\\nabla p.\n$$\nThe triple product on the left-hand side vanishes because $\\mathbf{B}\\cdot(\\mathbf{J}\\times\\mathbf{B})=0$ for any $\\mathbf{J}$ and $\\mathbf{B}$, hence\n$$\n\\mathbf{B}\\cdot\\nabla p=0.\n$$\nTherefore, $p$ is constant along field lines. If field lines densely cover the flux surface (which is typical for irrational rotational transform), $p$ is constant on each surface, implying\n$$\np=p(\\psi),\n$$\na flux function.\n\nNow analyze the direction of $\\mathbf{J}$. The force balance $\\mathbf{J}\\times\\mathbf{B}=\\nabla p$ shows that $\\nabla p$ is perpendicular to the surfaces (since $p=p(\\psi)$ and $\\nabla\\psi$ is the surface normal). The cross product of $\\mathbf{J}$ and $\\mathbf{B}$ yields a vector perpendicular to both; for $\\mathbf{J}\\times\\mathbf{B}$ to be purely normal to the surface, $\\mathbf{J}$ must lie within the surface (any component of $\\mathbf{J}$ parallel to $\\mathbf{B}$ does not affect the cross product). Thus,\n$$\n\\mathbf{J}\\cdot\\nabla\\psi=0,\n$$\ni.e., the current density is tangent to the flux surfaces up to components parallel to $\\mathbf{B}$, which are themselves tangent because $\\mathbf{B}$ is tangent to the surfaces.\n\nWith these geometric constraints established, consider symmetry. In an axisymmetric tokamak, there is a continuous symmetry (an ignorable toroidal angle), which allows reduction of the equilibrium to a two-dimensional partial differential equation in the poloidal plane (the Grad–Shafranov formulation) and ties the rotational transform primarily to the net toroidal plasma current. In a stellarator, coils are arranged to produce three-dimensional shaping that generates rotational transform without requiring a net toroidal plasma current; the equilibrium lacks any continuous spatial symmetry, so there is no ignorable coordinate and no reduction to a two-dimensional Grad–Shafranov form. Nevertheless, the same magnetostatic and MHD force-balance equations apply, and flux surfaces and flux functions remain well-defined.\n\nOption-by-option analysis:\n\nA. This option states the governing equations $\\nabla\\cdot\\mathbf{B}=0$, $\\nabla\\times\\mathbf{B}=\\mu_0\\mathbf{J}$, and $\\mathbf{J}\\times\\mathbf{B}=\\nabla p$, specifies nested toroidal flux surfaces via $\\mathbf{B}\\cdot\\nabla\\psi=0$, and deduces $p=p(\\psi)$ and $\\mathbf{B}\\cdot\\nabla p=0$ along with $\\mathbf{J}\\cdot\\nabla\\psi=0$. These deductions follow directly from the analysis above. It correctly notes the absence of a continuous spatial symmetry in a stellarator and the consequent lack of a two-dimensional Grad–Shafranov reduction, and properly distinguishes the origin of rotational transform in stellarators (three-dimensional coil geometry) from tokamaks (net toroidal plasma current), while allowing for parallel and bootstrap currents. This is consistent with first principles and standard equilibrium theory. Verdict: Correct.\n\nB. This option imposes $\\mathbf{J}=\\mathbf{0}$, hence $\\nabla\\times\\mathbf{B}=\\mathbf{0}$, and $\\nabla p=\\mathbf{0}$. That describes a trivial vacuum field with uniform pressure, not a general stellarator equilibrium. Real stellarators support nonzero plasma currents (e.g., bootstrap) in equilibrium and need not have curl-free magnetic fields within the plasma. Furthermore, stating that three-dimensional symmetry is irrelevant is incorrect: the absence of continuous symmetry fundamentally alters the mathematical structure of the equilibrium. Verdict: Incorrect.\n\nC. This option claims that lacking axisymmetry prevents field lines from lying on nested toroidal surfaces and prevents $p$ from being a flux function. That contradicts both the assumed existence of flux surfaces and the derivation $\\mathbf{B}\\cdot\\nabla\\psi=0$ and $p=p(\\psi)$ from force balance. Three-dimensional stellarators are explicitly designed to possess nested flux surfaces over substantial regions, and rotational transform is well-defined; so is the safety factor (or its reciprocal) in appropriate flux coordinates. Verdict: Incorrect.\n\nD. This option asserts that a stellarator retains an ignorable coordinate and reduces to a modified Grad–Shafranov equation, and that rotational transform always requires net toroidal plasma current. An ignorable coordinate requires a continuous symmetry that is generally absent in stellarators; quasi-symmetric constructs do not provide an ignorable coordinate for the full magnetic vector field. The Grad–Shafranov reduction relies on exact axisymmetry. Rotational transform in stellarators is generated by three-dimensional coil geometry and does not require net toroidal plasma current, although plasma currents can be present. Verdict: Incorrect."}, {"answer": "$$\\boxed{A}$$", "solution": "The problem statement is a valid exercise in magnetohydrodynamic (MHD) equilibrium theory, a core topic in computational fusion science. It provides the foundational equations and asks for a deduction of the properties of a stellarator equilibrium, contrasting it with a tokamak. All provided premises—the governing equations and the assumption of nested flux surfaces—are standard in the field.\n\nFirst, we will establish the fundamental relationships between the vector and scalar fields from the given governing laws. The plasma equilibrium is described by the force balance equation:\n$$\n\\mathbf{J}\\times\\mathbf{B}=\\nabla p\n$$\nand the magnetostatic Maxwell equations:\n$$\n\\nabla\\cdot\\mathbf{B}=0 \\\\\n\\nabla\\times\\mathbf{B}=\\mu_0\\mathbf{J}\n$$\nThe problem posits the existence of nested toroidal magnetic flux surfaces, which are level sets of a single-valued function $\\psi$. By definition, magnetic field lines, $\\mathbf{B}$, lie within these surfaces. This geometric constraint is expressed mathematically as the field being perpendicular to the surface normal, $\\nabla\\psi$:\n$$\n\\mathbf{B}\\cdot\\nabla\\psi = 0\n$$\nNow, we analyze the force balance equation. Taking the dot product with the magnetic field $\\mathbf{B}$:\n$$\n\\mathbf{B}\\cdot(\\mathbf{J}\\times\\mathbf{B}) = \\mathbf{B}\\cdot\\nabla p\n$$\nThe left-hand side is identically zero, as the vector $\\mathbf{J}\\times\\mathbf{B}$ is, by definition of the cross product, orthogonal to $\\mathbf{B}$. Therefore, we must have:\n$$\n\\mathbf{B}\\cdot\\nabla p = 0\n$$\nThis implies that magnetic field lines lie within surfaces of constant pressure $p$ (isobaric surfaces).\n\nSimilarly, taking the dot product of the force balance equation with the current density $\\mathbf{J}$:\n$$\n\\mathbf{J}\\cdot(\\mathbf{J}\\times\\mathbf{B}) = \\mathbf{J}\\cdot\\nabla p\n$$\nThe left-hand side is again identically zero. This gives us:\n$$\n\\mathbf{J}\\cdot\\nabla p = 0\n$$\nThis implies that current density vectors also lie within isobaric surfaces.\n\nFor a consistent equilibrium with nested toroidal flux surfaces, the magnetic field lines must exist on a single, well-defined set of nested surfaces. Since the field lines lie on both the flux surfaces (constant $\\psi$) and the isobaric surfaces (constant $p$), these two sets of surfaces must coincide. This means that pressure must be a function of the flux coordinate $\\psi$ only, i.e., $p = p(\\psi)$. This is a critical result: in an ideal MHD equilibrium with nested flux surfaces, pressure is a flux function.\n\nFrom $p=p(\\psi)$, we can write $\\nabla p = \\frac{dp}{d\\psi}\\nabla\\psi$. Substituting this into the condition $\\mathbf{J}\\cdot\\nabla p = 0$, we find:\n$$\n\\mathbf{J}\\cdot\\left(\\frac{dp}{d\\psi}\\nabla\\psi\\right) = \\frac{dp}{d\\psi}(\\mathbf{J}\\cdot\\nabla\\psi) = 0\n$$\nSince we are interested in a confined plasma, there must be a pressure gradient, so $\\frac{dp}{d\\psi} \\neq 0$ (except possibly at the magnetic axis or the edge). Thus, we must have:\n$$\n\\mathbf{J}\\cdot\\nabla\\psi = 0\n$$\nThis demonstrates that the current density $\\mathbf{J}$ is also confined to lie within the magnetic flux surfaces.\n\nNow, we contrast the tokamak and stellarator configurations based on symmetry.\nA **tokamak** is characterized by its axisymmetry, meaning it possesses a continuous rotational symmetry around the torus major axis. This symmetry corresponds to an ignorable coordinate (the toroidal angle, $\\phi$). This property allows the $3$-dimensional vector equilibrium problem to be reduced to a single $2$-dimensional scalar partial differential equation, the Grad-Shafranov equation, which describes the shape of the poloidal flux surfaces. In a tokamak, the rotational transform—the helical twist of the field lines necessary for equilibrium—is generated primarily by a large net toroidal plasma current, which creates the poloidal component of the magnetic field.\n\nA **stellarator**, in contrast, is fundamentally a $3$-dimensional configuration. It generally lacks any continuous spatial symmetry and thus has no ignorable coordinate. Consequently, the MHD equilibrium cannot be reduced to a $2$-dimensional form like the Grad-Shafranov equation; it must be solved using fully $3$-dimensional numerical codes. The key distinguishing feature of a stellarator is that the required rotational transform is produced primarily by the complex, twisted geometry of the external magnetic field coils. This means a stellarator can, in principle, achieve a confinement equilibrium with zero net toroidal plasma current. In practice, finite-pressure stellarator plasmas do contain currents flowing within the flux surfaces (e.g., Pfirsch-Schlüter currents, bootstrap currents), which are consequences of the equilibrium state, but they are not the primary source of the rotational transform.\n\nWith these principles established, we evaluate the given options.\n\nA. A stellarator magnetic configuration is a three-dimensional magnetostatic single-fluid MHD equilibrium with nested toroidal flux surfaces, satisfying $\\nabla\\cdot\\mathbf{B}=0$, $\\nabla\\times\\mathbf{B}=\\mu_0\\mathbf{J}$, and $\\mathbf{J}\\times\\mathbf{B}=\\nabla p$, where $p=p(\\psi)$ is a flux function and the surfaces are defined by $\\mathbf{B}\\cdot\\nabla\\psi=0$. These imply $\\mathbf{B}\\cdot\\nabla p=0$ and $\\mathbf{J}\\cdot\\nabla\\psi=0$ (current tangent to the surfaces, up to any component parallel to $\\mathbf{B}$). Unlike an axisymmetric tokamak, the stellarator generally lacks any continuous spatial symmetry (no ignorable coordinate), so the equilibrium cannot be reduced to a two-dimensional Grad–Shafranov form; the rotational transform is provided by three-dimensional coil geometry rather than requiring a net toroidal plasma current, although parallel and bootstrap currents may exist.\n- This option correctly states the governing equations. It correctly identifies $p=p(\\psi)$ and the conditions $\\mathbf{B}\\cdot\\nabla\\psi=0$, $\\mathbf{B}\\cdot\\nabla p=0$, and $\\mathbf{J}\\cdot\\nabla\\psi=0$ as we derived. It correctly states the defining lack of continuous symmetry in a stellarator and the consequence that the problem is irreducibly $3$-dimensional, unlike the tokamak's Grad-Shafranov description. It correctly identifies the source of rotational transform (coil geometry vs. plasma current) and acknowledges the existence of plasma currents like the bootstrap current. The statement is comprehensive and accurate.\n- **Verdict: Correct.**\n\nB. A stellarator magnetic configuration is a vacuum field with $\\mathbf{J}=\\mathbf{0}$ so that $\\nabla\\times\\mathbf{B}=\\mathbf{0}$, with uniform pressure $\\nabla p=\\mathbf{0}$, and it is distinguished from a tokamak by using only external coils instead of plasma current. The presence or absence of three-dimensional symmetry does not affect the equilibrium equations because the field is curl-free.\n- This option is flawed. A stellarator is designed to confine a plasma, which has a non-uniform pressure profile ($\\nabla p \\neq \\mathbf{0}$). From the force balance equation $\\mathbf{J}\\times\\mathbf{B}=\\nabla p$, a non-zero pressure gradient necessitates a non-zero plasma current density, $\\mathbf{J} \\neq \\mathbf{0}$. Describing the configuration as a vacuum field is incorrect for a plasma equilibrium. Furthermore, the claim that symmetry does not affect the equilibrium equations is false; symmetry properties of the domain and boundary conditions fundamentally alter the mathematical character of the solution and the methods used to find it.\n- **Verdict: Incorrect.**\n\nC. A stellarator magnetic configuration satisfies $\\nabla\\cdot\\mathbf{B}=0$, $\\nabla\\times\\mathbf{B}=\\mu_0\\mathbf{J}$, and $\\mathbf{J}\\times\\mathbf{B}=\\nabla p$, but because it lacks axisymmetry, magnetic field lines cannot lie on nested toroidal surfaces, so neither $\\mathbf{B}\\cdot\\nabla\\psi=0$ nor $p=p(\\psi)$ can hold; therefore the concept of rotational transform or safety factor is undefined in three-dimensional equilibria.\n- This option makes a fundamental error. The primary goal of modern stellarator design is precisely to create a $3$-dimensional magnetic field that *does* possess well-behaved, nested toroidal flux surfaces. While non-axisymmetric fields are susceptible to the formation of magnetic islands and stochastic regions, the existence of good surfaces is a prerequisite for achieving good plasma confinement and is the basis of ideal MHD equilibrium theory for stellarators. As such, the conditions $\\mathbf{B}\\cdot\\nabla\\psi=0$ and $p=p(\\psi)$ are assumed to hold. The concept of rotational transform is well-defined and is a central parameter in stellarator physics.\n- **Verdict: Incorrect.**\n\nD. Both stellarator and tokamak equilibria satisfy $\\nabla\\cdot\\mathbf{B}=0$, $\\nabla\\times\\mathbf{B}=\\mu_0\\mathbf{J}$, and $\\mathbf{J}\\times\\mathbf{B}=\\nabla p$ on nested toroidal surfaces. In a stellarator, despite three-dimensional shaping, there remains an ignorable coordinate so the equilibrium reduces to a modified Grad–Shafranov equation with an extra source term, and rotational transform always requires a nonzero net toroidal plasma current; otherwise rotational transform cannot exist.\n- This option is incorrect because it falsely attributes tokamak properties to a stellarator. A general stellarator does not have an ignorable coordinate, and its equilibrium does not reduce to a Grad-Shafranov-type equation. Most importantly, it incorrectly states that a stellarator requires a net toroidal plasma current for rotational transform. This is the defining characteristic of a tokamak; a stellarator is specifically designed to generate rotational transform from its external coil geometry, without needing a net toroidal current.\n- **Verdict: Incorrect.**\n\nBased on the analysis, option A is the only one that provides a correct and complete description consistent with the principles of MHD equilibrium for stellarators."}]


title: [code] Compare the memory usage of a 3D spectral solver versus a 3D finite element solver for a given accuracy on a Poisson problem.

Question: You will design and implement a program that estimates and compares the memory usage of a three-dimensional spectral solver and a three-dimensional finite element solver required to achieve a prescribed accuracy on the Poisson problem. The mathematical task is to connect error tolerance to resolution for each method using foundational convergence facts, then translate the minimum resolution into a memory usage estimate via a transparent storage model.

Consider the Poisson equation on the unit cube $[0,1]^3$, namely $-\Delta u = f$, where $u$ and $f$ are sufficiently smooth so that $u$ is analytic on $[0,1]^3$. Assume the following foundations.

- The $L^2$ norm is defined by $\|v\|_{L^2([0,1]^3)} = \left(\int_{[0,1]^3} |v(\mathbf{x})|^2 \, d\mathbf{x}\right)^{1/2}$.
- For a Fourier spectral method on a periodic domain with an analytic solution, the truncation error in the $L^2$ norm decreases exponentially with the linear resolution per dimension. Specifically, starting from the principle that Fourier coefficients of an analytic function decay geometrically with frequency magnitude, and that truncation at a finite cutoff yields an aliasing/truncation error driven by the tail of those coefficients, derive an inequality that ensures the $L^2$ error is at most a given tolerance $\varepsilon > 0$ when using $n$ grid points per spatial dimension.
- For a conforming finite element method of polynomial degree $p \in \mathbb{N}$ on a quasi-uniform mesh with mesh size $h$, interpolation theory and the Bramble–Hilbert lemma imply an algebraic rate of convergence in the $L^2$ norm when the exact solution is sufficiently smooth. Starting from these foundational results, derive an inequality that ensures the $L^2$ error is at most $\varepsilon$ in terms of $h$ and $p$.

Use these derived relationships to choose the smallest admissible integer resolutions that meet the tolerance, then map those resolutions to memory usage using the following models.

- Spectral solver memory model:
  - Let $n$ be the number of grid points per dimension, so the total scalar degrees of freedom is $n^3$.
  - Let $b$ be the number of bytes per stored scalar.
  - Let $\kappa_{\mathrm{spec}}$ be the number of stored scalars per grid point, accounting for the primary arrays and working space.
  - Memory in bytes is $M_{\mathrm{spec}} = b \cdot \kappa_{\mathrm{spec}} \cdot n^3$.
- Finite element solver memory model:
  - Let $m$ be the number of elements per dimension, so the element size is $h = 1/m$.
  - For polynomial degree $p$, approximate the number of scalar unknowns by $N = (m p + 1)^3$ (tensor-product Lagrange nodes on a structured hexahedral mesh).
  - Let $s$ be the average number of nonzeros per matrix row in the global sparse stiffness matrix, so the number of nonzeros is $\mathrm{nnz} = s \cdot N$.
  - Let $\kappa_{\mathrm{vec}}$ be the number of solution-like vectors stored (for instance, solution and right-hand side), each counted in scalars.
  - Let $\kappa_{\mathrm{nz}}$ be the number of $b$-byte slots needed to store one matrix nonzero (for instance, one for the value and one for the column index).
  - Store an additional $(N+1)$ $b$-byte slots for the row-pointer array in compressed sparse row (CSR) format.
  - Memory in bytes is $M_{\mathrm{fem}} = b \left[ \kappa_{\mathrm{vec}} N + \kappa_{\mathrm{nz}} \mathrm{nnz} + (N+1) \right]$.

Resolution selection rules to enforce:
- For the spectral method, use the smallest integer $n \ge 2$ that meets the accuracy tolerance based on your derived inequality and the provided constants.
- For the finite element method, use the smallest integer $m \ge 1$ that meets the accuracy tolerance based on your derived inequality and the provided constants.

Your task:
- Derive, from the foundational convergence statements above, the explicit inequalities that link $n$ to $\varepsilon$ for the spectral method and $h$ (equivalently $m$) and $p$ to $\varepsilon$ for the finite element method.
- Convert these inequalities into formulas for the minimal admissible integer $n$ and $m$ following the rules above.
- Implement a program that, for each test case in the test suite below, computes:
  - The minimal spectral resolution per dimension $n$ (an integer).
  - The minimal finite element elements per dimension $m$ (an integer).
  - The spectral memory $M_{\mathrm{spec}}$ in bytes (an integer).
  - The finite element memory $M_{\mathrm{fem}}$ in bytes (an integer).
  - A winner flag that is $1$ if $M_{\mathrm{spec}} < M_{\mathrm{fem}}$ and $0$ otherwise (an integer).
- The program must aggregate the results across all test cases into a single flat list containing, for each test case in order, the five integers $[n, m, M_{\mathrm{spec}}, M_{\mathrm{fem}}, \text{winner}]$, and print this list on a single line as the only output.

Use the natural logarithm where applicable. There are no physical units beyond bytes; all memory quantities must be expressed in bytes as integers.

  for the five integers of each test case in sequence.

Answer: [{"answer": "```python\n# The complete and runnable Python 3 code goes here.\n# Imports must adhere to the specified execution environment.\nimport numpy as np\n\ndef minimal_spectral_n(eps, Cs, alpha):\n    # Handle cases where Cs <= eps to avoid log of <= 1 producing non-positive values\n    if Cs <= eps:\n        base_n = 0.0\n    else:\n        base_n = np.log(Cs / eps) / alpha\n    n = int(np.ceil(base_n))\n    return max(2, n)\n\ndef minimal_fe_m(eps, Cf, p):\n    # m >= (Cf/eps)^(1/(p+1)), with m >= 1\n    if eps <= 0:\n        raise ValueError(\"eps must be positive\")\n    base_m = (Cf / eps) ** (1.0 / (p + 1))\n    m = int(np.ceil(base_m))\n    return max(1, m)\n\ndef spectral_memory_bytes(n, b, kappa_spec):\n    # M_spec = b * kappa_spec * n^3\n    dof = n**3\n    return int(b * kappa_spec * dof)\n\ndef fem_memory_bytes(m, p, b, s, kappa_vec, kappa_nz):\n    # N = (m*p + 1)^3\n    N = (m * p + 1) ** 3\n    nnz = s * N\n    # M_fem = b * [kappa_vec*N + kappa_nz*nnz + (N+1)]\n    total_slots = kappa_vec * N + kappa_nz * nnz + (N + 1)\n    return int(b * total_slots)\n\ndef solve():\n    # Define the test cases from the problem statement.\n    # Each case: (eps, Cs, alpha, Cf, p, b, kappa_spec, s, kappa_vec, kappa_nz)\n    test_cases = [\n        (1e-6, 1.0, 0.5, 1.0, 1, 8, 6, 15, 2, 2),\n        (1e-8, 1.0, 0.3, 1.0, 1, 8, 6, 27, 2, 2),\n        (1e-3, 1.0, 0.7, 1e-1, 2, 8, 6, 27, 2, 2),\n        (1e-12, 1.0, 0.5, 1.0, 2, 8, 6, 27, 2, 2),\n        (1e-2, 1.0, 0.15, 1e-2, 2, 8, 40, 10, 2, 2),\n    ]\n\n    results = []\n    for case in test_cases:\n        eps, Cs, alpha, Cf, p, b, kappa_spec, s, kappa_vec, kappa_nz = case\n\n        # Compute minimal resolutions\n        n = minimal_spectral_n(eps, Cs, alpha)\n        m = minimal_fe_m(eps, Cf, p)\n\n        # Compute memory usage\n        M_spec = spectral_memory_bytes(n, b, kappa_spec)\n        M_fem = fem_memory_bytes(m, p, b, s, kappa_vec, kappa_nz)\n\n        # Winner flag: 1 if spectral uses less memory, else 0\n        winner = 1 if M_spec < M_fem else 0\n\n        # Append results in the required flat format\n        results.extend([n, m, M_spec, M_fem, winner])\n\n    # Final print statement in the exact required format.\n    print(f\"[{','.join(map(str, results))}]\")\n\nsolve()\n```", "solution": "We start from the Poisson equation $-\\Delta u = f$ on the unit cube $[0,1]^3$ and quantify resolution requirements in the $L^2$ norm. The $L^2$ norm is defined as $\\|v\\|_{L^2([0,1]^3)} = \\left(\\int_{[0,1]^3} |v(\\mathbf{x})|^2 \\, d\\mathbf{x}\\right)^{1/2}$. We assume $u$ is analytic, and that the finite element polynomial degree $p \\in \\mathbb{N}$ with a quasi-uniform mesh.\n\nSpectral (Fourier) method resolution versus accuracy:\n- Fundamental base: For an analytic function, its Fourier coefficients $\\hat{u}(\\mathbf{k})$ decay exponentially with the frequency magnitude $|\\mathbf{k}|$, i.e., there exist constants $C > 0$ and $\\sigma > 0$ such that $|\\hat{u}(\\mathbf{k})| \\le C \\exp(-\\sigma |\\mathbf{k}|)$ for all multi-indices $\\mathbf{k} \\in \\mathbb{Z}^3$. This follows from standard complex-analytic extension arguments and the Paley–Wiener type results for periodic analytic functions.\n- Truncating the Fourier series to a cubic index set with maximum one-dimensional frequency proportional to $n$ (where $n$ is the number of equispaced grid points per spatial dimension) yields a truncation error that is dominated by the tail of the exponentially decaying coefficients. Therefore, the $L^2$ error of the spectral approximation satisfies a bound of the form\n  $$ E_{\\mathrm{spec}}(n) \\le C_s \\exp(-\\alpha n), $$\n  with positive constants $C_s$ and $\\alpha$ that depend on the analyticity region and problem data but not on $n$.\n- To achieve a prescribed tolerance $\\varepsilon > 0$, it is sufficient to ensure $C_s \\exp(-\\alpha n) \\le \\varepsilon$, which is equivalent to\n  $$ n \\ge \\frac{1}{\\alpha} \\log\\left(\\frac{C_s}{\\varepsilon}\\right). $$\n- Impose $n \\in \\mathbb{N}$ and adopt the rule $n \\ge 2$ to avoid trivial grids. The minimal admissible $n$ is therefore\n  $$ n_{\\min} = \\max\\left(2, \\left\\lceil \\frac{1}{\\alpha} \\log\\left(\\frac{C_s}{\\varepsilon}\\right) \\right\\rceil \\right). $$\n\nFinite element method resolution versus accuracy:\n- Fundamental base: For a quasi-uniform mesh with mesh size $h$ and polynomial degree $p \\in \\mathbb{N}$, if the exact solution $u$ is sufficiently smooth so that $u \\in H^{p+1}([0,1]^3)$, then interpolation theory and the Bramble–Hilbert lemma yield the $L^2$ error estimate\n  $$ E_{\\mathrm{fem}}(h) \\le C_f \\, h^{p+1}, $$\n  for some constant $C_f > 0$ independent of $h$.\n- To achieve $E_{\\mathrm{fem}}(h) \\le \\varepsilon$, it suffices that $C_f h^{p+1} \\le \\varepsilon$, i.e.,\n  $$ h \\le \\left(\\frac{\\varepsilon}{C_f}\\right)^{\\frac{1}{p+1}}. $$\n- On a structured hexahedral mesh of the unit cube with $m$ elements per dimension, the mesh size is $h = 1/m$, so we require\n  $$ m \\ge \\left(\\frac{C_f}{\\varepsilon}\\right)^{\\frac{1}{p+1}}. $$\n- Impose $m \\in \\mathbb{N}$ and $m \\ge 1$, and choose the minimal admissible\n  $$ m_{\\min} = \\max\\left(1, \\left\\lceil \\left(\\frac{C_f}{\\varepsilon}\\right)^{\\frac{1}{p+1}} \\right\\rceil \\right). $$\n\nMapping resolution to memory:\n- Spectral memory model: With $n$ points per dimension, the total number of scalar grid unknowns is $n^3$. If $b$ is the number of bytes per scalar and $\\kappa_{\\mathrm{spec}}$ is the number of stored scalars per grid point (to account for primary arrays and work arrays), then\n  $$ M_{\\mathrm{spec}} = b \\cdot \\kappa_{\\mathrm{spec}} \\cdot n^3 \\quad \\text{bytes}. $$\n- Finite element memory model: With $m$ elements per dimension and polynomial degree $p$, an approximation to the number of scalar unknowns is $N = (m p + 1)^3$. If $s$ is the average number of nonzeros per row in the sparse stiffness matrix, the total number of nonzeros is $\\mathrm{nnz} = s \\cdot N$. With $\\kappa_{\\mathrm{vec}}$ solution-like vectors, and $\\kappa_{\\mathrm{nz}}$ $b$-byte slots per nonzero, plus a CSR row-pointer array of length $(N+1)$, the total memory is\n  $$ M_{\\mathrm{fem}} = b \\left[ \\kappa_{\\mathrm{vec}} N + \\kappa_{\\mathrm{nz}} \\, \\mathrm{nnz} + (N+1) \\right] = b \\left[ \\kappa_{\\mathrm{vec}} N + \\kappa_{\\mathrm{nz}} s N + (N+1) \\right] \\quad \\text{bytes}. $$\n\nAlgorithmic steps for each test case:\n- Input: $(\\varepsilon, C_s, \\alpha, C_f, p, b, \\kappa_{\\mathrm{spec}}, s, \\kappa_{\\mathrm{vec}}, \\kappa_{\\mathrm{nz}})$.\n- Compute $n_{\\min} = \\max\\left(2, \\left\\lceil \\frac{1}{\\alpha} \\log\\left(\\frac{C_s}{\\varepsilon}\\right) \\right\\rceil \\right)$.\n- Compute $m_{\\min} = \\max\\left(1, \\left\\lceil \\left(\\frac{C_f}{\\varepsilon}\\right)^{\\frac{1}{p+1}} \\right\\rceil \\right)$.\n- Let $N_{\\mathrm{spec}} = n_{\\min}^3$ and $N_{\\mathrm{fem}} = (m_{\\min} p + 1)^3$.\n- Compute $M_{\\mathrm{spec}} = b \\cdot \\kappa_{\\mathrm{spec}} \\cdot N_{\\mathrm{spec}}$.\n- Compute $\\mathrm{nnz} = s \\cdot N_{\\mathrm{fem}}$ and $M_{\\mathrm{fem}} = b \\left[ \\kappa_{\\mathrm{vec}} N_{\\mathrm{fem}} + \\kappa_{\\mathrm{nz}} \\, \\mathrm{nnz} + (N_{\\mathrm{fem}} + 1) \\right]$.\n- Winner flag is $1$ if $M_{\\mathrm{spec}} < M_{\\mathrm{fem}}$, else $0$.\n- Output the five integers $[n_{\\min}, m_{\\min}, M_{\\mathrm{spec}}, M_{\\mathrm{fem}}, \\text{winner}]$.\n\nApplying these steps to the provided test suite yields the following computed integers (reproduced here to
