line."}, {"answer": "```python\n# The complete and runnable Python 3 code goes here.\n# Imports must adhere to the specified execution environment.\nimport numpy as np\n\ndef solve():\n    \"\"\"\n    Computes and compares memory usage for spectral and FEM solvers\n    for the 3D Poisson problem based on given convergence properties.\n    \"\"\"\n    # Define the test cases from the problem statement.\n    # Each tuple contains:\n    # (epsilon, C_s, alpha, C_f, p, b, k_spec, s, k_vec, k_nz)\n    test_cases = [\n        # Test 1\n        (1e-6, 1.0, 0.5, 1.0, 1, 8, 6, 15, 2, 2),\n        # Test 2\n        (1e-8, 1.0, 0.3, 1.0, 1, 8, 6, 27, 2, 2),\n        # Test 3\n        (1e-3, 1.0, 0.7, 0.1, 2, 8, 6, 27, 2, 2),\n        # Test 4\n        (1e-12, 1.0, 0.5, 1.0, 2, 8, 6, 27, 2, 2),\n        # Test 5\n        (1e-2, 1.0, 0.15, 1e-2, 2, 8, 40, 10, 2, 2),\n    ]\n\n    results = []\n    for case in test_cases:\n        epsilon, C_s, alpha, C_f, p, b, k_spec, s, k_vec, k_nz = case\n\n        # === Spectral Method Calculation ===\n\n        # Calculate minimal resolution n\n        # n >= (1/alpha) * ln(C_s / epsilon)\n        if C_s / epsilon <= 0:\n             # This case happens if epsilon is non-positive or C_s is non-positive\n             # The problem context implies these are positive.\n             # Or if log argument is <= 1, log is <=0, so n can be small.\n             n_float = 0\n        else:\n             n_float = (1 / alpha) * np.log(C_s / epsilon)\n        \n        # n must be an integer >= 2\n        n = max(2, int(np.ceil(n_float)))\n\n        # Calculate spectral memory M_spec = b * k_spec * n^3\n        # Python's integers have arbitrary precision, so overflow is not a concern.\n        M_spec = b * k_spec * (n**3)\n\n        # === Finite Element Method Calculation ===\n\n        # Calculate minimal resolution m\n        # m >= (C_f / epsilon)^(1 / (p + 1))\n        base = C_f / epsilon\n        # Handle case where base is negative (not expected) or power is complex\n        if base < 0:\n            # Physical problem implies this shouldn't happen for m >= 1\n            m_float = 0\n        else:\n            m_float = base**(1 / (p + 1))\n            \n        # m must be an integer >= 1\n        m = max(1, int(np.ceil(m_float)))\n\n        # Calculate FEM memory M_fem\n        # N = (m*p + 1)^3\n        N = (m * p + 1)**3\n        \n        # M_fem = b * [ (k_vec + s*k_nz + 1)*N + 1 ]\n        M_fem = b * ((k_vec + s * k_nz + 1) * N + 1)\n\n        # === Comparison ===\n        winner = 1 if M_spec < M_fem else 0\n\n        # Append results for this test case\n        results.extend([n, m, M_spec, M_fem, winner])\n\n    # Final print statement in the exact required format.\n    print(f\"[{','.join(map(str, results))}]\")\n\nsolve()\n```", "solution": "The problem requires a comparison of the memory usage of a Fourier spectral method and a finite element method (FEM) to solve the Poisson equation $-\\Delta u = f$ on the unit cube $[0,1]^3$ to a prescribed accuracy $\\varepsilon$. The solution comprises two principal steps: first, deriving the minimum required resolution for each method from foundational convergence theory, and second, calculating the memory footprint based on the derived resolution and provided memory models.\n\n### Step 1: Derivation of Resolution Requirements\n\nLet $\\| \\cdot \\|$ denote the $L^2([0,1]^3)$ norm. We are given the error tolerance $\\varepsilon$.\n\n#### Spectral Method Resolution\nThe problem states that for an analytic solution, the $L^2$ error of a Fourier spectral method decreases exponentially with the linear resolution, $n$, which is the number of grid points per dimension. We can model this relationship with the inequality:\n$$ \\|u - u_n\\|_{L^2} \\le C_s e^{-\\alpha n} $$\nwhere $u_n$ is the numerical solution, and $C_s > 0$ and $\\alpha > 0$ are given constants. To meet the error tolerance $\\varepsilon$, we must have:\n$$ C_s e^{-\\alpha n} \\le \\varepsilon $$\nSolving for $n$, we proceed as follows:\n$$ e^{-\\alpha n} \\le \\frac{\\varepsilon}{C_s} $$\nTaking the natural logarithm of both sides:\n$$ -\\alpha n \\le \\ln\\left(\\frac{\\varepsilon}{C_s}\\right) $$\nMultiplying by $-1$ reverses the inequality:\n$$ \\alpha n \\ge -\\ln\\left(\\frac{\\varepsilon}{C_s}\\right) = \\ln\\left(\\left(\\frac{\\varepsilon}{C_s}\\right)^{-1}\\right) = \\ln\\left(\\frac{C_s}{\\varepsilon}\\right) $$\nThis yields the minimum continuous resolution:\n$$ n \\ge \\frac{1}{\\alpha} \\ln\\left(\\frac{C_s}{\\varepsilon}\\right) $$\nThe problem specifies that $n$ must be an integer and $n \\ge 2$. Therefore, the minimal admissible resolution is the smallest integer satisfying these conditions:\n$$ n = \\max\\left(2, \\left\\lceil \\frac{1}{\\alpha} \\ln\\left(\\frac{C_s}{\\varepsilon}\\right) \\right\\rceil\\right) $$\nwhere $\\lceil \\cdot \\rceil$ denotes the ceiling function, which rounds a number up to the next integer.\n\n#### Finite Element Method Resolution\nFor a conforming finite element method of polynomial degree $p$ on a quasi-uniform mesh of size $h$, the standard a priori error estimate in the $L^2$ norm for a sufficiently smooth solution is:\n$$ \\|u - u_h\\|_{L^2} \\le C_f h^{p+1} $$\nwhere $C_f > 0$ is a constant independent of $h$. The mesh consists of $m$ elements per dimension on the unit cube, so the mesh size is $h = 1/m$. Substituting this into the inequality gives:\n$$ \\|u - u_h\\|_{L^2} \\le C_f \\left(\\frac{1}{m}\\right)^{p+1} $$\nTo meet the error tolerance $\\varepsilon$, we require:\n$$ C_f m^{-(p+1)} \\le \\varepsilon $$\nSolving for $m$:\n$$ m^{-(p+1)} \\le \\frac{\\varepsilon}{C_f} $$\nTaking the reciprocal of both sides reverses the inequality:\n$$ m^{p+1} \\ge \\frac{C_f}{\\varepsilon} $$\nTaking the $(p+1)$-th root of both sides gives:\n$$ m \\ge \\left(\\frac{C_f}{\\varepsilon}\\right)^{\\frac{1}{p+1}} $$\nThe problem requires the smallest integer $m \\ge 1$. Thus, the minimal number of elements per dimension is:\n$$ m = \\max\\left(1, \\left\\lceil \\left(\\frac{C_f}{\\varepsilon}\\right)^{\\frac{1}{p+1}} \\right\\rceil\\right) $$\n\n### Step 2: Memory Usage Calculation\n\nWith the minimal resolutions $n$ and $m$ determined, we apply the given memory models.\n\n#### Spectral Solver Memory\nThe memory in bytes, $M_{\\mathrm{spec}}$, is a function of the bytes per scalar $b$, the scalars per grid point $\\kappa_{\\mathrm{spec}}$, and the resolution $n$:\n$$ M_{\\mathrm{spec}} = b \\cdot \\kappa_{\\mathrm{spec}} \\cdot n^3 $$\n\n#### Finite Element Solver Memory\nThe memory in bytes, $M_{\\mathrm{fem}}$, is more complex. First, the total number of degrees of freedom $N$ is estimated using the number of elements per dimension $m$ and the polynomial degree $p$:\n$$ N = (mp + 1)^3 $$\nThe total memory is the sum of storage for vectors and the sparse matrix. The matrix is stored in Compressed Sparse Row (CSR) format.\n$$ M_{\\mathrm{fem}} = b \\left[ \\kappa_{\\mathrm{vec}} N + \\kappa_{\\mathrm{nz}} \\mathrm{nnz} + (N+1) \\right] $$\nwhere $\\mathrm{nnz} = s \\cdot N$ is the number of non-zero entries in the matrix. Substituting for $\\mathrm{nnz}$ and rearranging gives a more direct formula:\n$$ M_{\\mathrm{fem}} = b \\left[ \\kappa_{\\mathrm{vec}} N + \\kappa_{\\mathrm{nz}} (s N) + N+1 \\right] = b \\left[ (\\kappa_{\\mathrm{vec}} + s \\kappa_{\\mathrm{nz}} + 1)N + 1 \\right] $$\n\n### Step 3: Comparison\nFinally, for each test case, a winner flag is computed based on which method is more memory-efficient:\n$$ \\text{winner} = \\begin{cases} 1 & \\text{if } M_{\\mathrm{spec}} < M_{\\mathrm{fem}} \\\\ 0 & \\text{otherwise} \\end{cases} $$\nThese calculations are performed for each set of parameters in the test suite."}]


title: [multiple_choice] Analyze how quasi-symmetry breaking affects momentum confinement and error field correction effectiveness in optimized stellarator configurations.

Question: In an optimized stellarator targeting quasi-symmetry, consider the interplay between quasi-symmetry breaking, toroidal momentum confinement, and static error field penetration at a rational surface. Let the magnitude of symmetry-breaking Fourier content be quantified by a small parameter $\delta_B$, defined as the norm of the non-quasi-symmetric magnetic spectrum normalized to the dominant quasi-symmetric component. Assume operation in a collisionality regime where the neoclassical toroidal viscosity (NTV) torque is linear in the toroidal rotation and scales quadratically with symmetry breaking. Concretely, suppose the toroidal angular momentum dynamics can be modeled by a global balance between an externally applied torque $T_{\mathrm{ext}}$ and dissipative torques consisting of a baseline (collisional plus turbulent) drag proportional to the toroidal angular frequency $\omega$ with coefficient $\mu_0$ and an NTV drag proportional to $\omega$ with a coefficient that scales as $\Lambda_* \delta_B^2$, where $\Lambda_*$ is a device- and regime-dependent constant. The steady state is the long-time limit of this balance. The plasma faces a static intrinsic error field at a single helicity resonant surface, and error-field correction coils can supply a correction field to reduce the net resonant field. In linear, resistive magnetohydrodynamics (MHD), rotational shielding of a static perturbation arises from the Doppler shift of the plasma-frame frequency, and weaker rotation generally implies stronger resonant response and larger islands for the same applied field.

Use the following representative parameters for a single optimized configuration operated at two levels of quasi-symmetry breaking:
- External torque $T_{\mathrm{ext}} = 1$ (normalized torque units),
- Baseline drag coefficient $\mu_0 = 0.5$,
- NTV prefactor $\Lambda_* = 5000$ (normalized units),
- Case $\mathrm{QS1}$: $\delta_B = 0.01$,
- Case $\mathrm{QS2}$: $\delta_B = 0.03$.

From first principles, analyze how quasi-symmetry breaking modifies toroidal momentum confinement and the qualitative effectiveness of error-field correction. Which of the following statements are correct?

A. For fixed $T_{\mathrm{ext}}$, increasing $\delta_B$ from $0.01$ to $0.03$ reduces the steady toroidal rotation by a factor of approximately $5$, thereby degrading momentum confinement and rotational shielding of error fields.

B. In the limit of exact quasi-symmetry, intrinsic ambipolarity implies vanishing leading-order non-ambipolar radial current and approximate conservation of a helical canonical momentum, which supports long momentum confinement times and robust error-field screening at finite $T_{\mathrm{ext}}$.

C. Because the imposed error field is static in the laboratory frame, the linear resonant response and island width at the rational surface are independent of the plasma rotation, so quasi-symmetry breaking has no first-order effect on the penetration threshold; only the resistivity matters.

D. Even when quasi-symmetry is broken, the neoclassical toroidal viscosity can be strongly reduced by choosing a radial electric field $E_r$ that detunes bounce-precession resonances of trapped particles (avoiding the superbanana-plateau regime), thereby partially restoring rotational shielding and reducing the required correction-coil current.

E. Increasing quasi-symmetry breaking always improves the effectiveness of error-field correction coils because the larger neoclassical viscosity damps the electromagnetic torque from the error field.

Answer: [{"answer": "$$\\boxed{ABD}$$", "solution": "We begin from conservation of toroidal angular momentum and symmetry considerations. In a toroidal, magnetized plasma, the toroidal angular momentum balance can be written schematically as\n$$\n\\frac{\\mathrm{d}L_\\phi}{\\mathrm{d}t} = T_{\\mathrm{ext}} - T_{\\mathrm{diss}},\n$$\nwhere $L_\\phi$ is the total toroidal angular momentum and $T_{\\mathrm{ext}}$ is the applied external torque. The dissipative torque $T_{\\mathrm{diss}}$ includes a baseline collisional and turbulent drag that is approximately linear in the toroidal rotation (with coefficient $\\mu_0$), and the neoclassical toroidal viscosity (NTV) torque, which arises from non-axisymmetric magnetic fields causing trapped-particle non-ambipolar drifts and stresses. In the relevant collisionality regimes, the NTV torque is linear in $\\omega$ and scales as the square of a non-axisymmetry measure, here the quasi-symmetry breaking parameter $\\delta_B$. Thus,\n$$\nT_{\\mathrm{diss}} \\approx \\left(\\mu_0 + \\Lambda_* \\delta_B^2\\right)\\,\\omega,\n$$\nwhere $\\Lambda_*$ is a prefactor set by magnetic geometry, collisionality, and pressure. This expression follows from well-tested neoclassical theory: non-axisymmetric perturbations produce a toroidal viscous stress proportional to rotation, with a transport coefficient that scales as the square of the perturbation amplitude in the linear regime.\n\nIn steady state, $\\mathrm{d}L_\\phi/\\mathrm{d}t=0$, so torque balance gives\n$$\nT_{\\mathrm{ext}} = \\left(\\mu_0 + \\Lambda_* \\delta_B^2\\right)\\,\\omega,\n$$\nhence\n$$\n\\omega = \\frac{T_{\\mathrm{ext}}}{\\mu_0 + \\Lambda_* \\delta_B^2}.\n$$\nThis shows directly that increased quasi-symmetry breaking (larger $\\delta_B$) increases the NTV damping coefficient and reduces the steady rotation for fixed external torque, degrading momentum confinement and reducing rotational shielding of error fields.\n\nWe now analyze the error-field penetration physics. The generalized Ohm’s law contains the term $\\mathbf{E} + \\mathbf{v} \\times \\mathbf{B} = \\eta \\mathbf{j} + \\cdots$, and at a rational surface the plasma’s linear response to a static resonant field depends on the plasma-frame frequency of the perturbation. For a mode with helicity $(m,n)$, the plasma-frame frequency is $\\omega_{\\mathrm{pl}} = n \\Omega_\\phi + m \\Omega_\\theta$, where $\\Omega_\\phi$ and $\\Omega_\\theta$ are toroidal and poloidal angular frequencies associated with the flow. Larger $\\omega_{\\mathrm{pl}}$ produces stronger rotational shielding (the resonant layer is effectively detuned), thereby reducing the resonant response amplitude and shrinking magnetic island widths. Conversely, when $\\omega_{\\mathrm{pl}}$ is small due to strong damping of rotation, shielding weakens, the electromagnetic resonant torque more readily locks the plasma, and static error fields penetrate more easily. Therefore, for fixed uncorrected error field, the required correction-coil effort to achieve a target residual island width increases as $\\delta_B$ increases.\n\nWith these principles, evaluate each option:\n\nOption A: Compute the change in steady rotation between the two cases using the torque balance. For case $\\mathrm{QS1}$, with $\\delta_B = 0.01$, we have $\\delta_B^2 = 0.0001$ and\n$$\n\\Lambda_* \\delta_B^2 = 5000 \\times 0.0001 = 0.5,\n$$\nso\n$$\n\\mu_0 + \\Lambda_* \\delta_B^2 = 0.5 + 0.5 = 1.0,\n\\quad\n\\omega_{\\mathrm{QS1}} = \\frac{T_{\\mathrm{ext}}}{\\mu_0 + \\Lambda_* \\delta_B^2} = \\frac{1}{1.0} = 1.\n$$\nFor case $\\mathrm{QS2}$, with $\\delta_B = 0.03$, we have $\\delta_B^2 = 0.0009$ and\n$$\n\\Lambda_* \\delta_B^2 = 5000 \\times 0.0009 = 4.5,\n$$\nso\n$$\n\\mu_0 + \\Lambda_* \\delta_B^2 = 0.5 + 4.5 = 5.0,\n\\quad\n\\omega_{\\mathrm{QS2}} = \\frac{1}{5.0} = 0.2.\n$$\nThus the rotation decreases by a factor of $1/0.2 = 5$. Because rotational shielding decreases with reduced rotation, momentum confinement degrades and error-field penetration is facilitated. Verdict — Correct.\n\nOption B: In the limit of exact quasi-symmetry, there exists an approximate ignorable coordinate (for example, a helical angle), leading to a corresponding approximate conserved canonical momentum (Noether’s theorem). In quasi-symmetry, the guiding-center drifts parallel to the symmetry direction ensure intrinsic ambipolarity at leading order: the bounce-averaged radial particle fluxes balance without requiring a strong radial electric field $E_r$. This suppresses non-ambipolar radial current and reduces neoclassical momentum damping, lengthening the toroidal momentum confinement time. As a consequence, for finite $T_{\\mathrm{ext}}$, one expects higher $\\omega$ and thus stronger rotational shielding of static error fields. These statements are standard results of neoclassical theory for quasi-symmetric stellarators and do not contradict any fundamental principles. Verdict — Correct.\n\nOption C: The assertion that the linear resonant response and island width are independent of rotation is not consistent with linear resistive MHD. The resonant layer dynamics depend on the plasma-frame frequency $\\omega_{\\mathrm{pl}}$ through the Doppler shift in $\\mathbf{E} + \\mathbf{v}\\times \\mathbf{B}$, and the matching conditions at the rational surface include terms proportional to $\\omega_{\\mathrm{pl}}$ that reduce the real part of the response and can suppress island growth. Numerous analyses show that the amplitude of the resonant response to a static perturbation decreases with increasing flow shear and rotation, and locking thresholds are higher when the rotation is larger. Therefore, quasi-symmetry breaking, by reducing $\\omega$, has a first-order effect on penetration thresholds. Verdict — Incorrect.\n\nOption D: The neoclassical toroidal viscosity depends sensitively on the relationship between particle drift-precession frequencies and the spectrum of non-axisymmetric fields. In particular, in the superbanana-plateau regime, when the bounce-precession frequency of trapped ions aligns with harmonics of the field spectrum, the NTV torque can be large. By tailoring the radial electric field $E_r$ (which alters $\\mathbf{E}\\times\\mathbf{B}$ and precession frequencies), one can detune from these resonances, significantly reducing the NTV coefficient and thereby increasing $\\omega$ for the same $T_{\\mathrm{ext}}$. This improves rotational shielding and reduces the correction-coil current needed to achieve a target residual response. This strategy is supported by neoclassical theory and experiments in non-axisymmetric devices. Verdict — Correct.\n\nOption E: Larger neoclassical viscosity does not damp the electromagnetic torque from the error field; rather, it damps the plasma rotation. As rotation decreases, the electromagnetic resonant torque becomes more effective at phase-locking and penetrating because the plasma-frame frequency diminishes. Therefore, increasing quasi-symmetry breaking generally degrades, not improves, the effectiveness of error-field correction coils (more precise cancellation or higher coil current is required for the same performance). Verdict — Incorrect."}, {"answer": "$$\\boxed{ABD}$$", "solution": "The problem statement is scientifically grounded, well-posed, and internally consistent. It presents a simplified but standard model for toroidal momentum balance in a stellarator, linking quasi-symmetry breaking to neoclassical toroidal viscosity (NTV), and subsequently to plasma rotation and error field shielding. All necessary parameters and physical relationships are provided to analyze the system's behavior and evaluate the given options. Therefore, the problem is valid, and we may proceed with the solution.\n\nThe core of the problem is the global toroidal momentum balance equation, which in steady-state equates the external driving torque to the sum of all drag torques. According to the problem description, this is:\n$$T_{\\mathrm{ext}} = T_{\\mathrm{drag,baseline}} + T_{\\mathrm{NTV}}$$\nThe baseline drag is given as proportional to the toroidal angular frequency $\\omega$ with a coefficient $\\mu_0$, and the NTV drag is also proportional to $\\omega$ with a coefficient that scales as $\\Lambda_* \\delta_B^2$. Thus, the equation becomes:\n$$T_{\\mathrm{ext}} = \\mu_0 \\omega + (\\Lambda_* \\delta_B^2) \\omega$$\nThis can be rearranged to solve for the steady-state angular frequency $\\omega$:\n$$T_{\\mathrm{ext}} = (\\mu_0 + \\Lambda_* \\delta_B^2) \\omega$$\n$$\\omega = \\frac{T_{\\mathrm{ext}}}{\\mu_0 + \\Lambda_* \\delta_B^2}$$\nThe total drag coefficient is $\\mu_{\\mathrm{tot}} = \\mu_0 + \\Lambda_* \\delta_B^2$. The global momentum confinement time $\\tau_\\phi$ is inversely proportional to this drag coefficient.\n\nWe are given the following parameters:\n- External torque $T_{\\mathrm{ext}} = 1$\n- Baseline drag coefficient $\\mu_0 = 0.5$\n- NTV prefactor $\\Lambda_* = 5000$\n\nWe now evaluate the two cases of quasi-symmetry breaking.\n\nCase QS1: Good quasi-symmetry, $\\delta_B = 0.01$.\nThe NTV component of the drag coefficient is $\\Lambda_* \\delta_{B,1}^2 = 5000 \\times (0.01)^2 = 5000 \\times 10^{-4} = 0.5$.\nThe total drag coefficient is $\\mu_{\\mathrm{tot},1} = \\mu_0 + \\Lambda_* \\delta_{B,1}^2 = 0.5 + 0.5 = 1.0$.\nThe steady-state rotation is $\\omega_1 = \\frac{T_{\\mathrm{ext}}}{\\mu_{\\mathrm{tot},1}} = \\frac{1}{1.0} = 1.0$ (in normalized frequency units).\n\nCase QS2: Degraded quasi-symmetry, $\\delta_B = 0.03$.\nThe NTV component of the drag coefficient is $\\Lambda_* \\delta_{B,2}^2 = 5000 \\times (0.03)^2 = 5000 \\times 9 \\times 10^{-4} = 4.5$.\nThe total drag coefficient is $\\mu_{\\mathrm{tot},2} = \\mu_0 + \\Lambda_* \\delta_{B,2}^2 = 0.5 + 4.5 = 5.0$.\nThe steady-state rotation is $\\omega_2 = \\frac{T_{\\mathrm{ext}}}{\\mu_{\\mathrm{tot},2}} = \\frac{1}{5.0} = 0.2$ (in normalized frequency units).\n\nThe ratio of the rotation frequencies is $\\frac{\\omega_1}{\\omega_2} = \\frac{1.0}{0.2} = 5$. Thus, increasing $\\delta_B$ from $0.01$ to $0.03$ reduces the steady-state toroidal rotation by a factor of $5$. As stated in the problem, weaker rotation implies weaker rotational shielding of static error fields.\n\nNow we evaluate each option:\n\n**A. For fixed $T_{\\mathrm{ext}}$, increasing $\\delta_B$ from $0.01$ to $0.03$ reduces the steady toroidal rotation by a factor of approximately $5$, thereby degrading momentum confinement and rotational shielding of error fields.**\nOur calculation shows that the rotation decreases from $\\omega_1 = 1.0$ to $\\omega_2 = 0.2$, which is a reduction by a factor of exactly $5$. The total drag coefficient increases from $\\mu_{\\mathrm{tot},1} = 1.0$ to $\\mu_{\\mathrm{tot},2} = 5.0$. Since the momentum confinement time is inversely proportional to the drag coefficient, this represents a significant degradation of momentum confinement. The problem text explicitly states that weaker rotation degrades rotational shielding. Therefore, this statement is fully consistent with our derivation from the provided model.\n**Verdict: Correct.**\n\n**B. In the limit of exact quasi-symmetry, intrinsic ambipolarity implies vanishing leading-order non-ambipolar radial current and approximate conservation of a helical canonical momentum, which supports long momentum confinement times and robust error-field screening at finite $T_{\\mathrm{ext}}$.**\nThis statement describes the ideal limit of quasi-symmetry ($\\delta_B \\to 0$). In this limit, the magnetic field strength has a hidden symmetry, akin to toroidal symmetry in a tokamak. This symmetry implies the conservation of a canonical momentum (toroidal for quasi-axisymmetry, helical for quasi-helical symmetry). This conservation law leads to intrinsically ambipolar neoclassical particle fluxes, meaning the net radial current driven by neoclassical effects is zero at leading order. The NTV torque is a direct consequence of non-ambipolar radial currents, so in the limit of exact quasi-symmetry, the NTV torque vanishes. In our model, this corresponds to the term $\\Lambda_* \\delta_B^2$ going to zero. The total drag would then be minimal, equal only to the baseline drag $\\mu_0$. This minimal drag implies a long momentum confinement time. For a given torque $T_{\\mathrm{ext}}$, this leads to a high steady-state rotation $\\omega = T_{\\mathrm{ext}}/\\mu_0$. High rotation enables robust rotational screening of static error fields. This statement accurately describes the fundamental physics of quasi-symmetry.\n**Verdict: Correct.**\n\n**C. Because the imposed error field is static in the laboratory frame, the linear resonant response and island width at the rational surface are independent of the plasma rotation, so quasi-symmetry breaking has no first-order effect on the penetration threshold; only the resistivity matters.**\nThis statement is fundamentally incorrect. A static magnetic perturbation in the lab frame is experienced by the rotating plasma as an oscillating field. In the plasma frame, the frequency of the perturbation is Doppler-shifted by the plasma rotation, $\\omega' = \\omega_{lab} - k \\cdot v \\approx -m\\omega$, where $m$ is the poloidal mode number of the resonant harmonic and $\\omega$ is the plasma's toroidal rotation frequency. The plasma's response, governed by resistive MHD, is strongly dependent on this apparent frequency $\\omega'$. A high plasma-frame frequency (i.e., high rotation $\\omega$) leads to strong shielding currents that oppose the penetration of the field, thus reducing the resulting island width. Weaker rotation leads to a weaker shielding effect. Since quasi-symmetry breaking directly impacts rotation (as shown in A), it has a strong effect on the error field penetration threshold. The statement that the response is independent of rotation is a direct contradiction of the principle of rotational shielding, which was even mentioned in the problem description.\n**Verdict: Incorrect.**\n\n**D. Even when quasi-symmetry is broken, the neoclassical toroidal viscosity can be strongly reduced by choosing a radial electric field $E_r$ that detunes bounce-precession resonances of trapped particles (avoiding the superbanana-plateau regime), thereby partially restoring rotational shielding and reducing the required correction-coil current.**\nThis statement introduces a valid and important physical mechanism not explicitly included in the simple model, but completely consistent with the underlying physics. The NTV torque is not a simple constant for a given magnetic geometry ($\\delta_B$) but depends sensitively on the radial electric field, $E_r$, and the plasma collisionality. The torque arises from resonant interactions between the particle drift orbits and the non-symmetric magnetic field components. The $E_r \\times B$ drift modifies particle precession frequencies. By tuning $E_r$, one can shift the system away from these strong resonances (e.g., the superbanana-plateau regime where NTV is very large) to a regime with much lower NTV. This would lower the total drag, increasing the plasma rotation $\\omega$ for a given $T_{\\mathrm{ext}}$ and $\\delta_B$. The restored rotation would improve rotational shielding, meaning the plasma can screen the intrinsic error field more effectively on its own. Consequently, a smaller correction field from external coils would be needed to suppress magnetic islands to the desired level.\n**Verdict: Correct.**\n\n**E. Increasing quasi-symmetry breaking always improves the effectiveness of error-field correction coils because the larger neoclassical viscosity damps the electromagnetic torque from the error field.**\nThis statement's conclusion is contrary to the established physics. As demonstrated in the analysis of option A, increasing quasi-symmetry breaking (increasing $\\delta_B$) increases NTV, which in turn reduces the steady-state plasma rotation. Reduced rotation leads to weaker rotational shielding. This makes the plasma more vulnerable to both intrinsic and externally applied static error fields. To prevent the formation of a locked mode island, the error field at the rational surface must be minimized. Since the plasma's self-shielding capability is diminished, a larger correction field must be applied by the external coils to cancel the intrinsic error. This means the correction coils must work harder, which can be interpreted as a reduction in their \"effectiveness\" or an increase in the operational requirement. The claim that higher viscosity \"damps the electromagnetic torque\" is misleading in this context. While a higher viscosity allows the plasma to sustain a larger braking torque from a locked island before stopping completely, the goal of error field correction is to prevent the island from forming and locking in the first place, a task made harder by the poor rotational shielding.\n**Verdict: Incorrect.**"}]


title: [multiple_choice] Evaluate how mismatched quadrature between bulk and interface elements can lead to loss of equilibrium consistency and propose an order matching rule.

Question: A planar small-strain elasticity patch consists of $2$ conforming isoparametric quadrilateral bulk elements of polynomial order $p$ that share a common edge. Along that common edge, a zero-thickness interface element is inserted to transmit tractions; the interface interpolates the displacement jump with polynomial order $r$ along the edge, and the geometric mapping of the edge has order $g$ (so the edge Jacobian is a polynomial of degree $g-1$). The interface follows a linear traction–separation relation $t = K \llbracket u \rrbracket$ with constant, symmetric, positive semidefinite matrix $K$, where $\llbracket u \rrbracket$ denotes the displacement jump.

In the finite element method (FEM), the semi-discrete internal force contributions at the interface for the two adjacent bulk elements are obtained by numerically integrating the edge term in the weak form, schematically of the form $\int_{\Gamma} N^{\top} t \, \mathrm{d}s$, where $N$ are the traces of bulk shape functions on the edge, and $t$ is the interface traction evaluated consistently with the interface kinematics. Suppose Gauss–Legendre quadrature is used on the edge in two places: one quadrature rule attached to the interface element itself (with $n_{\Gamma}$ points), and an independent quadrature rule attached to each bulk element face (with $n_{b}$ points), with potentially different point locations and weights after mapping to the physical edge.

You are asked to assess equilibrium consistency in this setting and to select an order matching rule that prevents spurious unbalanced nodal forces across the interface under polynomial patch tests (e.g., constant-stress states) and during linearization.

Which statement most correctly explains how mismatched quadrature between the bulk faces and the interface can lead to loss of discrete equilibrium consistency, and prescribes a sufficient order matching rule that eliminates this pathology for isoparametric elements of orders $p$, $r$, and $g$?

A. Discrete loss of equilibrium occurs when the two bulk faces and the interface element evaluate and weight the traction at different edge points, so that the assembled nodal forces on the two sides are not guaranteed to be equal and opposite. To prevent this, use one and the same mapped Gauss–Legendre rule on the shared edge for both bulk faces and for the interface element (identical points and weights), with a number of points $n$ chosen so that the rule is exact for the highest-degree edge integrand that appears in the residual and tangent, namely all polynomials up to degree $\max\{2p,\, p+r\} + g - 1$. For Gauss–Legendre, this is guaranteed if $2n - 1 \ge \max\{2p,\, p+r\} + g - 1$.

B. Discrete equilibrium is preserved as long as each participant (both bulk faces and the interface) uses any Gauss–Legendre rule of order at least $p$ on the edge; it is not necessary that the quadrature points or weights coincide between the bulk faces and the interface.

C. Because the traction–separation law is linear, a single Gauss point on the interface edge suffices for all $p$, while each bulk face may independently use reduced integration with $n_{b} = p$ points; the resulting scheme preserves equilibrium by symmetry.

D. Matching the quadrature on the edge is unnecessary. It is sufficient to choose the interface edge quadrature order equal to the volumetric quadrature order used in the interiors of the bulk elements; any standard choice of edge points on the bulk faces will lead to balanced interface forces by virtue of the variational formulation.

Answer: [{"answer": "$$\\boxed{A}$$", "solution": "We begin from the principle of virtual work for small-strain elasticity with an internal interface, which adds an edge contribution. For virtual displacement field $\\delta u$, the interface term has the form\n$$\n\\delta W_{\\Gamma} = \\int_{\\Gamma} \\delta \\llbracket u \\rrbracket^{\\top} \\, t \\, \\mathrm{d}s,\n$$\nwith linear traction–separation relation\n$$\nt = K \\, \\llbracket u \\rrbracket.\n$$\nIn a standard finite element discretization, the displacement field on each bulk side is approximated using shape functions of order $p$, and the displacement jump $\\llbracket u \\rrbracket$ is either represented implicitly from the two bulk traces or via an interface interpolation of order $r$ along the edge. The geometric mapping of the isoparametric edge has order $g$, so the edge Jacobian $J_{\\Gamma}$ is a polynomial of degree $g-1$ in the edge parameter.\n\nFor a bulk node $a$ on one side of the interface, the interface contribution to its residual force is\n$$\nf^{\\Gamma}_a = \\int_{\\Gamma} N_a^{\\top} \\, t \\, \\mathrm{d}s,\n$$\nwhere $N_a$ is the trace of the bulk shape function associated with node $a$ on the edge. In a cohesive formulation with $t = K \\llbracket u \\rrbracket$, the traction depends on the jump, and hence on the interface interpolation (order $r$) or on the traces of the bulk interpolations (order $p$). The discrete residual (and its linearization) are computed by numerical quadrature along $\\Gamma$.\n\nIf the two adjacent bulk faces and the interface element evaluate the integral $\\int_{\\Gamma} N^{\\top} t \\, \\mathrm{d}s$ using different quadrature point sets and weights (after mapping to the same physical edge), then in general the two discrete nodal force vectors assembled on opposite sides of the interface are not guaranteed to be equal and opposite. The reason is that the discrete action on one side computes a weighted sum\n$$\nf^{\\Gamma, +}_a \\approx \\sum_{i=1}^{n_b} w^{(b)}_i \\, N_a^{+}(\\xi^{(b)}_i) \\, t(\\xi^{(b)}_i),\n$$\nwhile the discrete reaction on the other side computes\n$$\nf^{\\Gamma, -}_a \\approx - \\sum_{j=1}^{n_b} w^{(b)}_j \\, N_a^{-}(\\xi^{(b)}_j) \\, t(\\xi^{(b)}_j),\n$$\nor, if built from the interface element,\n$$\nf^{\\Gamma, \\text{int}}_a \\approx \\sum_{k=1}^{n_{\\Gamma}} w^{(\\Gamma)}_k \\, \\widehat{N}_a(\\xi^{(\\Gamma)}_k) \\, t(\\xi^{(\\Gamma)}_k),\n$$\nwith generally different sets $\\{\\xi^{(b)}_i, w^{(b)}_i\\}$ and $\\{\\xi^{(\\Gamma)}_k, w^{(\\Gamma)}_k\\}$. Unless these sets coincide and $t$ is evaluated consistently at the same points, there is no algebraic guarantee that the assembled forces on opposite sides will cancel exactly. This destroys discrete equilibrium consistency, even in polynomial patch tests where the exact continuous interface balance is satisfied. In particular, when the exact traction along the edge is a polynomial of degree not fully captured by the chosen quadrature rules, the mismatch of evaluation points prevents the two sides from producing equal and opposite discrete moments of the traction distribution.\n\nTo remove this pathology, two requirements suffice:\n\n$1.$ Use a single, common Gauss–Legendre quadrature rule on the shared edge for both bulk faces and the interface element, i.e., identical mapped quadrature points and weights so that the same evaluations of $t$ and the same weights enter both sides. This enforces the discrete action–reaction pair at the algebraic level.\n\n$2.$ Choose the common number of Gauss points $n$ large enough to integrate exactly the polynomial integrands arising in both the residual and the tangent matrix along the edge. We now bound the polynomial degree of the edge integrands.\n\nDegree counting on the edge:\n\n- For the residual, $f^{\\Gamma}_a = \\int_{\\Gamma} N_a^{\\top} \\, t \\, \\mathrm{d}s$. If $t$ is computed from a jump interpolated with order $r$ along the edge, then along the edge parameter the traction $t$ is a polynomial of degree at most $r$ (times constants from $K$). If the interface instead derives the jump purely from the traces of bulk fields of order $p$, then $t$ has degree at most $p$. In a general cohesive discretization that couples bulk and interface fields, the worst-case degree for the residual integrand is $p + r$ (product of order $p$ and order $r$), before accounting for geometry.\n\n- For the consistent tangent, the integrand contains products of shape functions, e.g., terms of the form $\\int_{\\Gamma} N_a^{\\top} K N_b \\, \\mathrm{d}s$ and, when the jump is expressed via bulk traces, mixed terms of degree up to $2p$. Thus, a safe bound for the tangent integrand degree is $\\max\\{2p,\\, p + r\\}$.\n\n- The isoparametric edge mapping contributes the Jacobian factor $J_{\\Gamma}$ of degree $g-1$, increasing the overall integrand degree by $g-1$.\n\nGauss–Legendre quadrature with $n$ points is exact for all polynomials up to degree $2n - 1$. Therefore, a sufficient exactness condition for both residual and tangent on the edge is\n$$\n2n - 1 \\;\\ge\\; \\max\\{2p,\\, p + r\\} + g - 1,\n$$\nor equivalently\n$$\nn \\;\\ge\\; \\left\\lceil \\dfrac{\\max\\{2p,\\, p + r\\} + g}{2} \\right\\rceil.\n$$\nIn the common case of straight edges ($g = 1$) and interface jump built from bulk traces ($r = p$), this reduces to $2n - 1 \\ge 2p \\implies n \\ge p + 1$, the standard face rule. Using the same mapped points and weights on both bulk faces and the interface guarantees that the discrete action and reaction are equal and opposite at the algebraic level, ensuring equilibrium consistency and passing polynomial patch tests.\n\nWe now assess the options:\n\nOption A: This states that mismatched quadrature points and weights on the shared edge can lead to non-canceling discrete forces and prescribes two remedies: use a single common quadrature on the edge for all participants and choose the number of points $n$ so that Gauss–Legendre exactness covers the highest-degree integrands in residual and tangent, namely all polynomials up to degree $\\max\\{2p,\\, p + r\\} + g - 1$. This matches the derivation above. Verdict — Correct.\n\nOption B: It allows independent edge quadrature rules as long as each has order at least $p$, and does not require coincident points. This fails for two reasons. First, independent point sets generally break the algebraic action–reaction pairing, so equal and opposite forces are not guaranteed. Second, order $p$ is insufficient to exactly integrate tangent terms of degree up to $2p$ (and residual terms of degree up to $p + r$), especially when $g > 1$. Verdict — Incorrect.\n\nOption C: It proposes one-point integration on the interface for all $p$ and reduced integration $n_b = p$ on the bulk faces, claiming symmetry preserves equilibrium. One-point rules are exact only up to degree $1$; they will not integrate higher-degree edge polynomials arising for $p \\ge 1$ and $g \\ge 1$, leading to residual and tangent errors. Moreover, symmetry does not compensate for different point sets; discrete action–reaction still fails in general. Verdict — Incorrect.\n\nOption D: It suggests matching the interface edge quadrature order to the volumetric quadrature used in the bulk interiors and claims edge matching is unnecessary. Volumetric and edge exactness requirements are unrelated; interior rules do not control edge polynomial exactness, and without identical edge quadrature points and weights the algebraic cancellation of interface forces is not guaranteed. Verdict — Incorrect."}, {"answer": "$$\\boxed{A}$$", "solution": "The user wants me to analyze a problem in computational solid mechanics concerning numerical integration at an interface between two finite elements.\n\n### Step 1: Extract Givens\n\n-   **System**: A planar small-strain elasticity patch.\n-   **Bulk Elements**: $2$ conforming isoparametric quadrilateral bulk elements of polynomial order $p$. They share a common edge, $\\Gamma$.\n-   **Interface Element**: A zero-thickness element is inserted on the common edge $\\Gamma$.\n-   **Interface Kinematics**: The interface interpolates the displacement jump, $\\llbracket u \\rrbracket$, with polynomial order $r$.\n-   **Geometric Mapping**: The shared edge $\\Gamma$ has a geometric mapping of order $g$. The Jacobian of this mapping is a polynomial of degree $g-1$.\n-   **Interface Constitutive Law**: A linear traction–separation relation, $t = K \\llbracket u \\rrbracket$, where $K$ is a constant, symmetric, positive semidefinite matrix.\n-   **Weak Form Contribution**: The internal force contribution from the interface is of the form $\\int_{\\Gamma} N^{\\top} t \\, \\mathrm{d}s$. Here, $N$ are the traces of the bulk element shape functions (polynomials of order $p$), and $t$ is the interface traction.\n-   **Numerical Integration**: Gauss-Legendre quadrature is used. The interface element has its own rule with $n_{\\Gamma}$ points. Each adjacent bulk element face has its own independent rule with $n_{b}$ points. Concepts such as isoparametric elements, polynomial shape functions ($p$), interface elements (cohesive zones) with independent kinematics ($r$), geometric mapping order ($g$), weak forms, and numerical quadrature are all fundamental and well-established in this field. The formulation is scientifically and mathematically sound.\n-   **Well-Posedness**: The question asks for a condition (a quadrature matching rule) to ensure a desirable numerical property (discrete equilibrium). This is a well-defined problem in numerical analysis for FEM, and a specific, stable, and meaningful solution can be derived from first principles.\n-   **Objectivity**: The problem is stated in precise, technical language common to computational mechanics. All terms have unambiguous definitions within the context of FEM. There is no subjective or opinion-based content.\n-   **Completeness and Consistency**: The problem provides all necessary parameters ($p, r, g$) and describes the numerical setup sufficiently to analyze the sources of error and derive a corrective rule.
