Tokamaks are real devices, and magnetic diagnostics are indeed a standard diagnostic technique in fusion research.\n\n- **Well-Posed**: The problem is structured to have a unique answer based on established scientific knowledge about Tokamak diagnostics.\n\n- **Objective**: The problem uses clear, precise, technical language appropriate for the field of nuclear fusion engineering.\n\n- **Self-contained and Consistent**: The problem provides sufficient context about Tokamaks and magnetic diagnostics to evaluate the options.\n\n- **Scientific Verifiability**: The answer can be verified against established literature and practices in fusion physics and engineering.\n\nThe problem does not exhibit any of the invalidating flaws listed in the checklist. It is scientifically sound, well-posed, objective, and verifiable.\n\n### Step 3: Verdict and Action\n\n**Verdict: VALID**\n\nThe problem is valid and I will proceed to the solution phase.\n\n---\n\n## Principle-Based Derivation\n\n**Fundamental Principles of Magnetic Diagnostics in Tokamaks:**\n\nIn a Tokamak, the plasma is confined by strong magnetic fields, typically composed of:\n- A toroidal field (in the toroidal direction)\n- A poloidal field (generated by plasma current and external coils)\n\nThe plasma position, shape, and stability are directly related to the magnetic field configuration. Any changes in plasma position, current distribution, or instabilities (such as disruptions or edge-localized modes) will alter the magnetic field distribution around the plasma.\n\n**Magnetic diagnostics** work on Faraday's law of electromagnetic induction:\n\n$$\\mathcal{E} = -\\frac{d\\Phi_B}{dt}$$\n\nwhere $\\mathcal{E}$ is the induced electromotive force and $\\Phi_B$ is the magnetic flux.\n\nThe key diagnostic tools include:\n\n1. **Magnetic probes (Mirnov coils)**: Small coils that measure local magnetic field fluctuations, particularly useful for detecting MHD instabilities.\n\n2. **Flux loops**: Coils that encircle the plasma (partially or fully) to measure the poloidal magnetic flux, which is directly related to the plasma current and position.\n\n3. **Rogowski coils**: Measure the total plasma current.\n\nThese measurements provide information about:\n- Plasma position (horizontal and vertical)\n- Plasma shape\n- Plasma current\n- MHD instabilities\n- Plasma equilibrium\n\n---\n\n## Option-by-Option Analysis\n\n**Option A: \"Magnetic diagnostics measure the plasma's optical emission to determine its position and stability.\"**\n\nThis option confuses magnetic diagnostics with optical/spectroscopic diagnostics. While optical emission spectroscopy is indeed used in Tokamaks for various measurements (temperature, impurities, etc.), it is **not** what magnetic diagnostics do. Magnetic diagnostics specifically measure magnetic fields, not optical emissions.\n\n**Verdict: Incorrect**\n\n---\n\n**Option B: \"Magnetic diagnostics use magnetic probes and flux loops to measure changes in magnetic fields around the plasma, which are indicative of its position and stability.\"**\n\nThis option accurately describes the principle and application of magnetic diagnostics:\n\n1. It correctly identifies the instruments used: **magnetic probes** and **flux loops**.\n2. It correctly states that these instruments **measure changes in magnetic fields** around the plasma.\n3. It correctly links these measurements to determining **plasma position and stability**.\n\nThis is consistent with established practices in Tokamak diagnostics. Magnetic probes (Mirnov coils) detect local magnetic field fluctuations, while flux loops measure poloidal flux changes. These measurements are processed through equilibrium reconstruction codes to determine plasma position, shape, and stability characteristics.\n\n**Verdict: Correct**\n\n---\n\n**Option C: \"Magnetic diagnostics involve the use of high-frequency electromagnetic waves to directly probe the interior of the plasma.\"**\n\nThis option describes **microwave diagnostics** or **reflectometry**, not magnetic diagnostics. Techniques like electron cyclotron emission (ECE), interferometry, and reflectometry use electromagnetic waves to probe plasma properties. However, these are distinct from magnetic diagnostics, which rely on measuring magnetic fields through inductive pickup coils.\n\n**Verdict: Incorrect**\n\n---\n\n**Option D: \"Magnetic diagnostics rely on measuring the plasma's thermal radiation to infer its magnetic field configuration.\"**\n\nThis option describes **radiation-based diagnostics** (such as bolometry or infrared imaging), not magnetic diagnostics. While thermal radiation measurements provide valuable information about plasma temperature and power balance, they do not directly measure magnetic fields and are not classified as magnetic diagnostics.\n\n**Verdict: Incorrect**\n\n---\n\n**Option E: \"Magnetic diagnostics use electric field sensors to measure the electric potential around the plasma, which correlates directly with its stability.\"**\n\nThis option incorrectly describes magnetic diagnostics as measuring **electric fields** rather than **magnetic fields**. While electric field measurements can be relevant in plasma physics (e.g., for studying plasma rotation or electric potential profiles), magnetic diagnostics specifically focus on magnetic field measurements through inductive coils, not electric field sensors.\n\n**Verdict: Incorrect**\n\n---\n\n## Conclusion\n\nBased on the principle-based analysis, **Option B** is the only scientifically accurate description of magnetic diagnostics in a Tokamak. It correctly identifies the instruments (magnetic probes and flux loops), the physical quantity measured (changes in magnetic fields), and the application (determining plasma position and stability)."}]


title: [calculation] Explain how real-time equilibrium reconstruction errors translate into ECCD alignment uncertainties and estimate the stabilization margin needed to ensure robustness.

Question: In a tokamak plasma, a rotating $m/n = 2/1$ magnetic island of saturated width $w$ requires targeted Electron Cyclotron Current Drive (ECCD) to stabilize. Consider the modified Rutherford equation (MRE) for magnetic island evolution, in which the ECCD enters as a resonant helical current term whose effectiveness depends on alignment to the island $O$-point. Real-time Grad–Shafranov equilibrium reconstruction provides the rational surface location and mapping needed to align ECCD, but reconstruction errors lead to alignment uncertainties. In a control-oriented reduced model, represent the ECCD effectiveness by an alignment factor $S(\Delta r, \Delta \phi)$ that multiplies the commanded ECCD resonant current, where $\Delta r$ is the radial misalignment at the rational surface and $\Delta \phi$ is the helical phase misalignment (in radians) relative to the island $O$-point.

Starting from the modified Rutherford equation as the fundamental base and the definition that, for stabilization, the effective ECCD resonant current must exceed the resonant bootstrap current deficit, derive how real-time equilibrium reconstruction errors in the magnetic axis position and safety factor profile translate into $\Delta r$ and $\Delta \phi$ uncertainties. Model the ECCD alignment factor as the product of a radial Gaussian deposition overlap and a helical phase projection, $S(\Delta r, \Delta \phi) = \exp\!\big(-\Delta r^{2}/(2\delta^{2})\big)\,\cos(\Delta \phi)$, where $\delta$ is the standard deviation of the ECCD Gaussian radial deposition at the rational surface. Let the net geometric coupling efficiency from ray-tracing and quasilinear current-drive modeling be $\eta_{\text{geo}}$.

Assume the following scientifically consistent parameters:
- Major radius $R_{0} = 3.0\,\text{m}$, minor radius $a = 1.0\,\text{m}$, rational surface radius $r_{s} = 0.5\,\text{m}$, and saturated island width $w = 0.06\,\text{m}$.
- At $r_{s}$, the safety factor is $q(r_{s}) = 2.0$ and its radial gradient is $\left.\frac{dq}{dr}\right|_{r_{s}} = 0.8\,\text{m}^{-1}$.
- Real-time equilibrium reconstruction standard deviations: magnetic axis position $\sigma_{\text{ax}} = 4\,\text{mm}$ and fractional safety factor error $\sigma_{q,\text{rel}} = 0.004$.
- Independent helical phase estimation standard deviations: magnetic probe phase $\sigma_{\text{probe}} = 0.12\,\text{rad}$ and equilibrium mapping phase $\sigma_{\text{map}} = 0.10\,\text{rad}$.
- ECCD radial deposition standard deviation $\delta = 0.02\,\text{m}$ and net geometric coupling efficiency $\eta_{\text{geo}} = 0.85$.

Treat all error sources as independent and Gaussian. Use first-order error propagation to obtain the standard deviations $\sigma_{r}$ and $\sigma_{\phi}$ for the radial and phase alignment errors. Define a robustness requirement such that stabilization must be guaranteed in the worst case within $2\sigma$ bounds simultaneously for both $\Delta r$ and $\Delta \phi$. Under this worst-case criterion, the stabilization margin $M_{\min}$ is the minimum ratio of commanded ECCD resonant current to the critical resonant current required with perfect alignment.

Derive an analytic expression for $M_{\min}$ in terms of $\eta_{\text{geo}}$, $\delta$, $\sigma_{r}$, and $\sigma_{\phi}$, evaluate it numerically using the parameters above, and round your final margin to three significant figures. Express the final answer as a dimensionless number. Angles must be treated in radians throughout.

Answer: [{"answer": "$$\\boxed{2.21}$$", "solution": "The modified Rutherford equation (MRE) provides the evolution of the magnetic island width $w$ under the influence of resistive tearing drive, bootstrap current drive, and externally driven resonant current, such as Electron Cyclotron Current Drive (ECCD). In a standard reduced form, the stabilizing action of ECCD enters the MRE through a term proportional to the resonant helical current component delivered near the rational surface, and its effectiveness is modulated by how well the deposition is aligned to the island $O$-point. For stabilization at fixed $w$, the criterion is that the effective ECCD resonant current exceed the resonant bootstrap current deficit that drives the island. Denoting the commanded ECCD resonant current by $I_{\\text{cmd}}$, the effective resonant current is\n$$\nI_{\\text{eff}} = \\eta_{\\text{geo}}\\,S(\\Delta r, \\Delta \\phi)\\,I_{\\text{cmd}},\n$$\nwhere $\\eta_{\\text{geo}}$ is a net geometric/current-drive efficiency and $S(\\Delta r, \\Delta \\phi)$ captures misalignment between deposition and the island structure. The stabilization margin is defined as\n$$\nM \\equiv \\frac{I_{\\text{cmd}}}{I_{\\text{crit}}},\n$$\nwhere $I_{\\text{crit}}$ is the critical resonant current required under perfect alignment ($S=1$) to cancel the resonant bootstrap drive. The robust stabilization requirement under misalignment is\n$$\nI_{\\text{eff}} \\ge I_{\\text{crit}} \\quad \\Rightarrow \\quad \\eta_{\\text{geo}}\\,S(\\Delta r, \\Delta \\phi)\\,I_{\\text{cmd}} \\ge I_{\\text{crit}} \\quad \\Rightarrow \\quad M \\ge \\frac{1}{\\eta_{\\text{geo}}\\,S(\\Delta r, \\Delta \\phi)}.\n$$\nThus the minimum margin ensuring robustness over a specified misalignment set is\n$$\nM_{\\min} = \\frac{1}{\\eta_{\\text{geo}}\\,\\min S(\\Delta r, \\Delta \\phi)}.\n$$\n\nTo connect real-time equilibrium reconstruction errors to ECCD alignment uncertainties, we propagate errors from the reconstructed axis and safety factor profile to the rational surface location and helical phase mapping:\n- The radial location $r_{s}$ of the $q=m/n$ rational surface is determined by the condition $q(r_{s})=m/n$. A small error $\\Delta q$ in the reconstructed $q$ at $r_{s}$ induces a radial error\n$$\n\\Delta r_{q} \\approx \\frac{\\Delta q}{\\left.\\frac{dq}{dr}\\right|_{r_{s}}}.\n$$\nTreating the magnetic axis position error $\\sigma_{\\text{ax}}$ as an independent radial shift and the fractional safety factor error as $\\sigma_{q} = q(r_{s})\\,\\sigma_{q,\\text{rel}}$, the radial standard deviation is\n$$\n\\sigma_{r} = \\sqrt{\\sigma_{\\text{ax}}^{2} + \\left(\\frac{\\sigma_{q}}{\\left.\\frac{dq}{dr}\\right|_{r_{s}}}\\right)^{2}}.\n$$\n- The helical phase alignment $\\phi$ combines the measured mode phase from magnetic probes with the equilibrium mapping that translates measurement coordinates to the ECCD launch geometry. With independent Gaussian uncertainties, the phase standard deviation is\n$$\n\\sigma_{\\phi} = \\sqrt{\\sigma_{\\text{probe}}^{2} + \\sigma_{\\text{map}}^{2}}.\n$$\n\nUnder the adopted control-oriented deposition model, the alignment factor is the product\n$$\nS(\\Delta r, \\Delta \\phi) = \\exp\\!\\left(-\\frac{\\Delta r^{2}}{2\\delta^{2}}\\right)\\,\\cos(\\Delta \\phi),\n$$\nwhere the Gaussian overlap captures the radial sensitivity of the on-axis peak to a misalignment $\\Delta r$, and the cosine captures the projection of the ECCD resonant current onto the $O$-point helical harmonic when the phase is offset by $\\Delta \\phi$.\n\nThe robustness requirement calls for guaranteed stabilization within the rectangle defined by $\\Delta r = \\pm 2\\sigma_{r}$ and $\\Delta \\phi = \\pm 2\\sigma_{\\phi}$ simultaneously. The minimum of $S$ over this rectangle occurs at the extremes $\\Delta r = 2\\sigma_{r}$ and $\\Delta \\phi = 2\\sigma_{\\phi}$, yielding\n$$\nS_{\\text{worst}} = \\exp\\!\\left(-\\frac{(2\\sigma_{r})^{2}}{2\\delta^{2}}\\right)\\,\\cos(2\\sigma_{\\phi}) = \\exp\\!\\left(-\\frac{2\\sigma_{r}^{2}}{\\delta^{2}}\\right)\\,\\cos(2\\sigma_{\\phi}).\n$$\nTherefore,\n$$\nM_{\\min} = \\frac{1}{\\eta_{\\text{geo}}\\,\\exp\\!\\left(-\\frac{2\\sigma_{r}^{2}}{\\delta^{2}}\\right)\\,\\cos(2\\sigma_{\\phi})}.\n$$\n\nWe now evaluate the uncertainties from the given parameters.\n\nFirst, the safety factor standard deviation at $r_{s}$ is\n$$\n\\sigma_{q} = q(r_{s})\\,\\sigma_{q,\\text{rel}} = 2.0 \\times 0.004 = 0.008.\n$$\nThe radial standard deviation from $q$ error is\n$$\n\\left(\\frac{\\sigma_{q}}{\\left.\\frac{dq}{dr}\\right|_{r_{s}}}\\right) = \\frac{0.008}{0.8\\,\\text{m}^{-1}} = 0.01\\,\\text{m}.\n$$\nCombining with the axis position error,\n$$\n\\sigma_{r} = \\sqrt{(0.004\\,\\text{m})^{2} + (0.01\\,\\text{m})^{2}} = \\sqrt{0.000016 + 0.0001}\\,\\text{m} = \\sqrt{0.000116}\\,\\text{m} \\approx 0.010770\\,\\text{m}.\n$$\nNext, the phase standard deviation is\n$$\n\\sigma_{\\phi} = \\sqrt{(0.12\\,\\text{rad})^{2} + (0.10\\,\\text{rad})^{2}} = \\sqrt{0.0144 + 0.01}\\,\\text{rad} = \\sqrt{0.0244}\\,\\text{rad} \\approx 0.156201\\,\\text{rad}.\n$$\nThe worst-case offsets are $\\Delta r = 2\\sigma_{r} \\approx 0.021540\\,\\text{m}$ and $\\Delta \\phi = 2\\sigma_{\\phi} \\approx 0.312402\\,\\text{rad}$.\n\nCompute the Gaussian radial factor:\n$$\n\\exp\\!\\left(-\\frac{(2\\sigma_{r})^{2}}{2\\delta^{2}}\\right) = \\exp\\!\\left(-\\frac{(0.021540\\,\\text{m})^{2}}{2(0.02\\,\\text{m})^{2}}\\right) = \\exp\\!\\left(-\\frac{0.0004639716}{0.0008}\\right) = \\exp(-0.5799645) \\approx 0.56043.\n$$\nCompute the phase projection:\n$$\n\\cos(2\\sigma_{\\phi}) = \\cos(0.312402) \\approx 0.95158.\n$$\nThus,\n$$\nS_{\\text{worst}} \\approx 0.56043 \\times 0.95158 \\approx 0.53300.\n$$\nFinally, with $\\eta_{\\text{geo}} = 0.85$,\n$$\nM_{\\min} = \\frac{1}{0.85 \\times 0.53300} = \\frac{1}{0.45305} \\approx 2.207.\n$$\n\nRounded to three significant figures as required, the robust stabilization margin is $2.21$, which is dimensionless. This margin ensures that, even with simultaneous $2\\sigma$ radial and phase misalignments induced by real-time equilibrium reconstruction errors, the effective ECCD resonant current meets or exceeds the critical value for stabilization."}, {"answer": "$$\\boxed{2.21}$$", "solution": "The problem requires the derivation and numerical evaluation of the minimum stabilization margin, $M_{\\min}$, for an Electron Cyclotron Current Drive (ECCD) system used to control a $m/n=2/1$ magnetic island in a tokamak. The derivation proceeds in four main steps: 1) calculating the standard deviation of the radial alignment error, $\\sigma_r$; 2) calculating the standard deviation of the helical phase alignment error, $\\sigma_\\phi$; 3) applying the specified worst-case robustness criterion to find the minimum alignment factor, $S_{\\min}$; and 4) deriving the expression for $M_{\\min}$ and computing its value.\n\nAll sources of error are stated to be independent and Gaussian, so we will use the standard formula for propagation of uncertainty, where variances of independent contributions add.\n\n**Step 1: Radial Alignment Uncertainty, $\\sigma_r$**\n\nThe radial misalignment, $\\Delta r$, is the error in the diagnosed position of the rational surface, $r_s$, where the safety factor $q(r_s) = 2$. Two independent sources contribute to this error:\n1.  The uncertainty in the magnetic axis position, which acts as a global offset. The standard deviation is given as $\\sigma_{\\text{ax}}$.\n2.  The uncertainty in the reconstructed safety factor ($q$) profile itself.\n\nTo quantify the effect of the $q$-profile uncertainty, we consider the relationship between a small change in radius, $\\delta r$, and the corresponding change in safety factor, $\\delta q$, around the rational surface $r_s$. Using a first-order Taylor expansion:\n$$ \\delta q \\approx \\left.\\frac{dq}{dr}\\right|_{r_s} \\delta r $$\nThis implies that an error in the local value of $q$, which we denote $\\Delta q$, leads to an error in the inferred radial position, $\\Delta r_q$:\n$$ \\Delta r_q \\approx \\frac{\\Delta q}{\\left.\\frac{dq}{dr}\\right|_{r_s}} $$\nThe problem provides the fractional safety factor error standard deviation, $\\sigma_{q,\\text{rel}}$. The standard deviation of the absolute error in $q$ at the rational surface, $\\sigma_{\\Delta q}$, is therefore:\n$$ \\sigma_{\\Delta q} = q(r_s) \\cdot \\sigma_{q,\\text{rel}} $$\nThe variance of the radial error component due to the $q$-profile uncertainty, $\\sigma_{r,q}^2$, is then:\n$$ \\sigma_{r,q}^2 = \\left( \\frac{1}{\\left.\\frac{dq}{dr}\\right|_{r_s}} \\right)^2 \\sigma_{\\Delta q}^2 = \\left( \\frac{q(r_s) \\sigma_{q,\\text{rel}}}{\\left.\\frac{dq}{dr}\\right|_{r_s}} \\right)^2 $$\nSince the error from the magnetic axis position and the $q$-profile reconstruction are independent, their variances add to give the total variance of the radial alignment error, $\\sigma_r^2$:\n$$ \\sigma_r^2 = \\sigma_{\\text{ax}}^2 + \\sigma_{r,q}^2 = \\sigma_{\\text{ax}}^2 + \\left( \\frac{q(r_s) \\sigma_{q,\\text{rel}}}{\\left.\\frac{dq}{dr}\\right|_{r_s}} \\right)^2 $$\n\n**Step 2: Phase Alignment Uncertainty, $\\sigma_\\phi$**\n\nThe helical phase misalignment, $\\Delta \\phi$, arises from errors in estimating the phase of the island's O-point. The problem identifies two independent sources:\n1.  The error from the magnetic probe phase measurement, with standard deviation $\\sigma_{\\text{probe}}$.\n2.  The error from mapping the probe measurement location to the rational surface in the equilibrium reconstruction, with standard deviation $\\sigma_{\\text{map}}$.\n\nAs these error sources are independent, the total variance of the phase alignment error, $\\sigma_\\phi^2$, is the sum of the individual variances:\n$$ \\sigma_\\phi^2 = \\sigma_{\\text{probe}}^2 + \\sigma_{\\text{map}}^2 $$\n\n**Step 3: Worst-Case Alignment Factor, $S_{\\min}$**\n\nThe ECCD effectiveness is governed by the alignment factor $S(\\Delta r, \\Delta \\phi) = \\exp(-\\Delta r^{2}/(2\\delta^{2}))\\,\\cos(\\Delta \\phi)$, where $\\delta$ is the ECCD deposition width. The robustness requirement states that stabilization must be guaranteed for the worst case within $2\\sigma$ bounds for both $\\Delta r$ and $\\Delta \\phi$ simultaneously. To find the minimum effectiveness, we must find the values of $\\Delta r$ and $\\Delta \\phi$ within their $2\\sigma$ intervals, $[ -2\\sigma_r, 2\\sigma_r ]$ and $[ -2\\sigma_\\phi, 2\\sigma_\\phi ]$, that minimize $S$.\n\nThe exponential term is minimized when $|\\Delta r|$ is maximized, i.e., $|\\Delta r| = 2\\sigma_r$. The cosine term is minimized when $|\\Delta \\phi|$ is maximized, i.e., $|\\Delta \\phi| = 2\\sigma_\\phi$ (assuming $2\\sigma_\\phi < \\pi/2$, which is expected for a functional control system).\nTherefore, the minimum alignment factor, $S_{\\min}$, under this criterion is:\n$$ S_{\\min} = S(2\\sigma_r, 2\\sigma_\\phi) = \\exp\\left(-\\frac{(2\\sigma_r)^2}{2\\delta^2}\\right) \\cos(2\\sigma_\\phi) = \\exp\\left(-\\frac{2\\sigma_r^2}{\\delta^2}\\right) \\cos(2\\sigma_\\phi) $$\n\n**Step 4: Stabilization Margin, $M_{\\min}$**\n\nThe condition for stabilization is that the effective ECCD resonant current, $I_{\\text{ECCD,eff}}$, must exceed the critical current required, $I_{\\text{crit}}$ (equal to the bootstrap current deficit). The effective current is the commanded current, $I_{\\text{ECCD,cmd}}$, reduced by the net geometric coupling efficiency, $\\eta_{\\text{geo}}$, and the alignment factor, $S(\\Delta r, \\Delta \\phi)$:\n$$ I_{\\text{ECCD,eff}} = I_{\\text{ECCD,cmd}} \\cdot \\eta_{\\text{geo}} \\cdot S(\\Delta r, \\Delta \\phi) $$\nThe stabilization condition is $I_{\\text{ECCD,eff}} \\ge I_{\\text{crit}}$, which means:\n$$ I_{\\text{ECCD,cmd}} \\cdot \\eta_{\\text{geo}} \\cdot S(\\Delta r, \\Delta \\phi) \\ge I_{\\text{crit}} $$\nThe stabilization margin $M$ is defined as the ratio of the commanded current to the critical current, $M = I_{\\text{ECCD,cmd}} / I_{\\text{crit}}$. Rearranging the inequality gives:\n$$ M = \\frac{I_{\\text{ECCD,cmd}}}{I_{\\text{crit}}} \\ge \\frac{1}{\\eta_{\\text{geo}} \\cdot S(\\Delta r, \\Delta \\phi)} $$\nTo guarantee stabilization under the worst-case $2\\sigma$ scenario, the commanded current must be large enough to satisfy the condition even when the alignment factor is at its minimum, $S_{\\min}$. The minimum margin, $M_{\\min}$, is therefore:\n$$ M_{\\min} = \\frac{1}{\\eta_{\\text{geo}} \\cdot S_{\\min}} = \\frac{1}{\\eta_{\\text{geo}} \\exp\\left(-\\frac{2\\sigma_r^2}{\\delta^2}\\right) \\cos(2\\sigma_\\phi)} $$\nThis can be written as the final analytical expression:\n$$ M_{\\min} = \\frac{\\exp\\left(\\frac{2\\sigma_r^2}{\\delta^2}\\right)}{\\eta_{\\text{geo}} \\cos(2\\sigma_\\phi)} $$\n\n**Numerical Evaluation**\n\nWe now substitute the given parameters, ensuring all units are in the SI system (meters and radians).\n- $q(r_s) = 2.0$\n- $\\left.\\frac{dq}{dr}\\right|_{r_{s}} = 0.8\\,\\text{m}^{-1}$\n- $\\sigma_{\\text{ax}} = 4\\,\\text{mm} = 0.004\\,\\text{m}$\n- $\\sigma_{q,\\text{rel}} = 0.004$\n- $\\sigma_{\\text{probe}} = 0.12\\,\\text{rad}$\n- $\\sigma_{\\text{map}} = 0.10\\,\\text{rad}$\n- $\\delta = 0.02\\,\\text{m}$\n- $\\eta_{\\text{geo}} = 0.85$\n\nFirst, calculate the variances $\\sigma_r^2$ and $\\sigma_\\phi^2$:\n$$ \\sigma_r^2 = (0.004\\,\\text{m})^2 + \\left( \\frac{2.0 \\times 0.004}{0.8\\,\\text{m}^{-1}} \\right)^2 = (0.004)^2 + (0.01)^2 = 0.000016 + 0.0001 = 0.000116\\,\\text{m}^2 $$\n$$ \\sigma_\\phi^2 = (0.12\\,\\text{rad})^2 + (0.10\\,\\text{rad})^2 = 0.0144 + 0.0100 = 0.0244\\,\\text{rad}^2 $$\n\nNext, calculate the arguments for the exponential and cosine functions in the $M_{\\min}$ expression:\n$$ \\frac{2\\sigma_r^2}{\\delta^2} = \\frac{2 \\times 0.000116\\,\\text{m}^2}{(0.02\\,\\text{m})^2} = \\frac{0.000232}{0.0004} = 0.58 $$\nThe argument for the cosine is $2\\sigma_\\phi$. It is computationally more precise to use $\\sigma_\\phi^2$:\n$$ 2\\sigma_\\phi = 2\\sqrt{0.0244\\,\\text{rad}^2} \\approx 0.31241\\,\\text{rad} $$\nThis value is well below $\\pi/2 \\approx 1.57$, confirming that ECCD remains stabilizing.\n\nFinally, compute $M_{\\min}$:\n$$ M_{\\min} = \\frac{\\exp(0.58)}{0.85 \\times \\cos(2\\sqrt{0.0244})} \\approx \\frac{1.786038}{0.85 \\times \\cos(0.31241)} \\approx \\frac{1.786038}{0.85 \\times 0.95154} \\approx \\frac{1.786038}{0.808809} \\approx 2.20827 $$\nRounding the result to three significant figures, as requested, gives $2.21$."}]


title: [multiple_choice] Explain the role of actively shielded gradient coils in suppressing eddy currents in surrounding conductive structures, and outline how shield windings are designed to cancel external fields while preserving internal gradient linearity.

Question: A Magnetic Resonance Imaging (MRI) system uses a rapidly switched $x$-gradient, denoted $G_x(t)$, to achieve spatial encoding. The magnet bore contains surrounding conductive structures (for example, Radio Frequency (RF) shields and cryostat elements) that experience induced currents when exposed to time-varying magnetic fields. Consider an actively shielded gradient assembly composed of a primary gradient winding at radius $R_p$ and a shield winding at radius $R_s$ with $R_s > R_p$. The conductive surroundings can be idealized as closed loops that intercept magnetic flux. Starting from Faraday’s law of induction, $\nabla \times \mathbf{E} = -\partial \mathbf{B}/\partial t$, and the constitutive relation $\mathbf{J} = \sigma \mathbf{E}$ in the conductive structures, reason about how time-varying fields induce eddy currents and how an actively shielded design mitigates them. Also, recall that in current-free regions the magnetic field obeys $\nabla \cdot \mathbf{B} = 0$ and can be derived from a scalar potential that satisfies Laplace’s equation, implying a multipole expansion in space and superposition of contributions from different windings. 

Which option best describes the mechanism by which actively shielded gradient coils suppress eddy currents in surrounding conductive structures and outlines how shield windings are designed to cancel external fields while preserving internal gradient linearity?

A. The shield winding carries current with opposite sign to the primary during switching, so the superposed time-varying fields outside the gradient set nearly cancel, reducing $\partial \Phi/\partial t$ through surrounding loops and thus minimizing induced electromotive force and eddy currents. The winding pattern is chosen so that the lowest-order external multipole moments (for example, the dipole and next orders) produced by the primary are canceled by the shield, while the internal field retains a linear term $B_z \approx G_x(t)\,x$ within the imaging volume. This approach slightly reduces gradient efficiency and increases inductance, but preserves linearity to within a design tolerance.

B. The shield winding is driven with the same current direction as the primary to reinforce the gradient and thereby confine the magnetic field, while eddy currents are suppressed primarily by increasing the resistivity $\sigma$ of the cryostat materials; the external field need not be canceled if higher $G_x(t)$ is achieved.

C. The shield winding is placed at a smaller radius than the primary and is driven in the same direction, which increases the near-field strength and therefore reduces the time derivative of the magnetic flux at larger radii; the internal gradient linearity is preserved because stronger fields are inherently more uniform.

D. A static offset field from the shield winding is tuned to cancel the external field produced by the primary at the bore wall, removing the need to consider $\partial \mathbf{B}/\partial t$ during switching; the internal gradient remains linear because only a static bias is added.

Answer: [{"answer": "$$\\boxed{A}$$", "solution": "### Problem Validation\n\n**Step 1: Extract Givens**\n-   A rapidly switched $x$-gradient in an MRI system is denoted $G_x(t)$.\n-   The magnet bore contains surrounding conductive structures that experience induced currents.\n-   An actively shielded gradient assembly consists of a primary gradient winding at radius $R_p$ and a shield winding at radius $R_s$.\n-   The radii are related by $R_s > R_p$.\n-   Conductive surroundings are idealized as closed loops intercepting magnetic flux.\n-   Faraday’s law of induction: $\\nabla \\times \\mathbf{E} = -\\partial \\mathbf{B}/\\partial t$.\n-   Constitutive relation in conductors: $\\mathbf{J} = \\sigma \\mathbf{E}$.\n-   In current-free regions, the magnetic field satisfies $\\nabla \\cdot \\mathbf{B} = 0$ and can be derived from a scalar potential satisfying Laplace’s equation.\n-   This implies a multipole expansion in space and superposition of contributions.\n-   The question asks for the mechanism of eddy current suppression and the design method for preserving internal linearity while canceling external fields.\n\n**Step 2: Validate Using Extracted Givens**\n-   **Scientifically Grounded**: The problem is based entirely on the fundamental principles of classical electromagnetism (Faraday's Law, Ampere's Law, properties of fields satisfying Laplace's equation) as applied to the established and well-understood technology of Magnetic Resonance Imaging. All stated principles are correct.\n-   **Well-Posed**: The problem asks for a qualitative but physically rigorous explanation of a specific engineering design (active shielding). The provided physical laws and setup are sufficient to deduce the correct mechanism. A unique, correct description exists.\n-   **Objective**: The language is technical and unambiguous. It uses standard terminology from physics and MRI engineering.\n\n**Flaw Checklist:**\n1.  **Scientific or Factual Unsoundness**: None. The physical setup and governing equations are correct.\n2.  **Non-Formalizable or Irrelevant**: None. The problem is directly relevant to the topic of spatial encoding in MRI.\n3.  **Incomplete or Contradictory Setup**: None. The problem provides all necessary conceptual foundations. The condition $R_s > R_p$ is consistent with an external shield design.\n4.  **Unrealistic or Infeasible**: None. Actively shielded gradients are a standard, crucial component of modern MRI scanners.\n5.  **Ill-Posed or Poorly Structured**: None. The question is clear and leads to a definite physical explanation.\n6.  **Pseudo-Profound, Trivial, or Tautological**: None. The problem requires a non-trivial synthesis of several electromagnetic concepts to explain a sophisticated engineering solution.\n7.  **Outside Scientific Verifiability**: None. The principles and their application in MRI are experimentally verified and well-documented.\n\n**Step 3: Verdict and Action**\nThe problem statement is **valid**. The solution will now be derived.\n\n### Principle-Based Derivation and Solution\n\nThe core issue is the generation of eddy currents in conductive structures surrounding the gradient coils. According to Faraday's law of induction, a time-varying magnetic field $\\mathbf{B}$ induces a curling electric field $\\mathbf{E}$ given by $\\nabla \\times \\mathbf{E} = -\\partial \\mathbf{B}/\\partial t$. In a material with conductivity $\\sigma$, this electric field drives a current density $\\mathbf{J} = \\sigma \\mathbf{E}$. These are the eddy currents.\n\nThe primary gradient coil (at radius $R_p$) is driven by a time-varying current to produce the desired gradient field, $G_x(t)$. This creates a time-varying magnetic field, $\\mathbf{B}_p(t)$, which extends into the space surrounding the coil set. This time-varying field is the source of the unwanted eddy currents in structures like the cryostat.\n\nThe principle of active shielding is to introduce a second coil, the shield winding (at radius $R_s > R_p$), which is driven by a current that is also time-varying. The goal is to design the system such that the total magnetic field, which is the superposition of the primary field and the shield field ($\\mathbf{B}_{total} = \\mathbf{B}_p + \\mathbf{B}_s$), has specific properties in two distinct regions:\n1.  **External Region ($r > R_s$)**: The field must be as close to zero as possible. If $\\mathbf{B}_{total} \\approx 0$ in this region, then its time derivative $\\partial \\mathbf{B}_{total}/\\partial t$ will also be nearly zero. By Faraday's law, this means a negligible electric field is induced in the surrounding conductive structures, and thus the eddy currents are suppressed. To achieve this cancellation, the shield winding must carry a current that flows in the opposite direction to the primary winding current. This creates a magnetic field that opposes the primary field in the external region.\n2.  **Internal Region (imaging volume, $r \\ll R_p$)**: The field must produce a highly linear gradient, e.g., $B_z \\approx G_x(t) x$, with minimal contamination from higher-order spatial terms.\n\nThe design of the wire patterns for the primary and shield windings is key. As stated, the field in current-free regions can be described by a scalar potential that satisfies Laplace's equation, allowing for a multipole expansion. The design process consists of choosing the winding geometries such that:\n-   For the region inside the primary coil, the superposition of the fields from both windings results in the desired linear gradient term dominating, while other terms are minimized.\n-   For the region outside the shield coil, the lowest-order multipole moments (which have the largest spatial extent) of the primary coil's field are canceled by the corresponding moments of the shield coil's field. This effectively \"confines\" the time-varying field to the volume between the primary and shield windings.\n\nA necessary consequence of this design is that the shield coil's magnetic field opposes the primary coil's field within the imaging volume as well. Therefore, to achieve a target gradient strength, a larger current is required in the primary coil compared to an unshielded design. This reduces the gradient efficiency (gradient strength per unit current). Furthermore, the magnetic coupling between the two coils affects the total inductance of the system, which must be handled by the gradient amplifier. These are accepted engineering trade-offs for the crucial benefit of fast, clean gradient switching.\n\n### Option-by-Option Analysis\n\n**A. The shield winding carries current with opposite sign to the primary during switching, so the superposed time-varying fields outside the gradient set nearly cancel, reducing $\\partial \\Phi/\\partial t$ through surrounding loops and thus minimizing induced electromotive force and eddy currents. The winding pattern is chosen so that the lowest-order external multipole moments (for example, the dipole and next orders) produced by the primary are canceled by the shield, while the internal field retains a linear term $B_z \\approx G_x(t)\\,x$ within the imaging volume. This approach slightly reduces gradient efficiency and increases inductance, but preserves linearity to within a design tolerance.**\nThis option correctly identifies that the shield current is opposite to the primary current. It correctly links the cancellation of the external field to a reduction in the time-rate-of-change of magnetic flux ($\\Phi$), which is the source of induced EMF and eddy currents, per Faraday's law. It accurately describes the design methodology using cancellation of external multipole moments while preserving the internal linear gradient. Lastly, it correctly states the primary engineering trade-offs: reduced efficiency and modified inductance. This description aligns perfectly with the derived principles.\n**Verdict: Correct.**\n\n**B. The shield winding is driven with the same current direction as the primary to reinforce the gradient and thereby confine the magnetic field, while eddy currents are suppressed primarily by increasing the resistivity $\\sigma$ of the cryostat materials; the external field need not be canceled if higher $G_x(t)$ is achieved.**\nThis option is incorrect on multiple grounds. Driving the shield current in the same direction as the primary would *add* to the external field, exacerbating eddy currents. Suppressing eddy currents by increasing resistivity is a *passive* shielding technique, not the *active* shielding described in the problem. The final statement that the external field need not be canceled is the opposite of the goal of shielding.\n**Verdict: Incorrect.**\n\n**C. The shield winding is placed at a smaller radius than the primary and is driven in the same direction, which increases the near-field strength and therefore reduces the time derivative of the magnetic flux at larger radii; the internal gradient linearity is preserved because stronger fields are inherently more uniform.**\nThis option is factually wrong. First, it contradicts the problem statement that $R_s > R_p$. For an external shield, it must be located outside the primary. Second, it incorrectly states the current direction is the same. Third, the reasoning that increasing near-field strength reduces the time derivative of flux at a distance is physically nonsensical. Fourth, the claim that stronger fields are inherently more uniform is false; uniformity and linearity are determined by coil geometry, not field strength.\n**Verdict: Incorrect.**\n\n**D. A static offset field from the shield winding is tuned to cancel the external field produced by the primary at the bore wall, removing the need to consider $\\partial \\mathbf{B}/\\partial t$ during switching; the internal gradient remains linear because only a static bias is added.**\nThis option fundamentally misunderstands the problem. Eddy currents are induced by a *time-varying* magnetic field ($\\partial \\mathbf{B}/\\partial t$). A *static* (DC) field from the shield coil cannot cancel a time-varying (AC) field from the primary coil. The superposition of a static field and a time-varying field would still result in a time-varying field, and thus eddy currents would still be induced. The problem is dynamic and requires a dynamic solution.\n**Verdict: Incorrect.**"}, {"answer": "$$\\boxed{A}$$", "solution": "The conductive surroundings can be idealized as loops that intercept magnetic flux, with induced electromotive force given by $\\mathcal{E} = -\\dfrac{d\\Phi}{dt}$, where $\\Phi$ is the magnetic flux through a loop. From Faraday’s law, $\\nabla \\times \\mathbf{E} = -\\partial \\mathbf{B}/\\partial t$, any time-varying magnetic field in the vicinity of a conductive structure induces a curl in the electric field, which drives a current density $\\mathbf{J} = \\sigma \\mathbf{E}$ according to the material’s conductivity $\\sigma$. These induced currents, termed eddy currents, generate secondary magnetic fields that distort the intended gradient and can persist after switching due to inductive time constants in the surrounding structures. Therefore, to suppress eddy currents one must minimize the time variation of the magnetic flux $\\Phi$ threading any conductive loop in the environment, equivalently minimize $\\partial \\mathbf{B}/\\partial t$ in the surroundings during gradient switching.\n\nIn current-free regions, $\\nabla \\cdot \\mathbf{B} = 0$ and the magnetic field can be derived from a scalar potential that satisfies Laplace’s equation. This implies the field in the bore can be expressed as a superposition of multipole terms. A primary gradient winding designed to produce a linear internal field $B_z \\approx G_x(t)\\,x$ also produces external multipole components that, in the far field, are dominated by low-order terms (for example, an effective magnetic dipole moment). By superposition, one can add a second winding (the shield) such that its external multipoles cancel those of the primary, while inside the imaging region the sum of their fields retains the desired linear term. To achieve cancellation outside while preserving the internal gradient, the shield winding must be driven with current of opposite sign relative to the primary during switching. In simplified terms, if the primary’s far-field is characterized by an effective magnetic dipole moment $m_p \\propto N_p I_p A_p$ (with $N_p$ the number of turns, $I_p$ the current, and $A_p$ an effective area), the shield is designed so that $m_s \\propto N_s I_s A_s$ satisfies $m_p + m_s \\approx 0$. Because $R_s > R_p$, the shield can achieve the required cancellation with appropriately chosen $N_s$ and $I_s$ of opposite sign. Simultaneously, the winding geometry (often found via stream-function optimization or other inverse methods) is constrained so that the internal first-order gradient term $G_x(t)$ remains linear and higher-order terms are minimized within a target region. Practically, adding the shield increases inductance and reduces gradient efficiency, but preserves linearity within specified tolerances while strongly suppressing external time-varying fields that would induce eddy currents.\n\nOption-by-option analysis:\n\nA. This option correctly ties the suppression mechanism to Faraday’s law: by making the net external time-varying field small, the time derivative of flux $\\partial \\Phi/\\partial t$ through conductive loops is minimized, reducing $\\mathcal{E}$ and thus eddy currents. It correctly states that the shield current is of opposite sign during switching, so external multipoles (for example, the effective dipole) cancel, while the internal linear term $B_z \\approx G_x(t)\\,x$ is preserved through appropriate winding design. Finally, it acknowledges realistic trade-offs: increased inductance and slightly reduced efficiency. Verdict — Correct.\n\nB. Driving the shield with the same current direction as the primary would increase, not cancel, the external field due to superposition, thereby increasing $\\partial \\mathbf{B}/\\partial t$ in the surroundings. Increasing $\\sigma$ of the cryostat is not a viable gradient-coil design mechanism; moreover, higher $\\sigma$ increases eddy current density for a given $\\mathbf{E}$. The claim that external field cancellation is unnecessary if $G_x(t)$ is larger is incorrect with respect to suppressing eddy currents. Verdict — Incorrect.\n\nC. Placing the shield at a smaller radius and driving it in the same direction does not confine the time-varying field in a way that reduces $\\partial \\mathbf{B}/\\partial t$ at larger radii; instead, superposition with same-sign currents tends to increase external field amplitudes. Stronger fields are not inherently more uniform, and increasing near-field strength does not guarantee preserved internal linearity without appropriate geometry. Verdict — Incorrect.\n\nD. A static offset field cannot cancel a time-varying field’s time derivative; eddy currents are driven by $\\partial \\mathbf{B}/\\partial t$, so a static bias does not suppress the induced $\\mathcal{E}$ during switching. Additionally, adding a static bias without proper design can degrade internal gradient linearity. Verdict — Incorrect."}]


title: [calculation] Compute the error incurred by neglecting displacement current in a high-frequency coil transient by comparing the exact Ampère–Maxwell law with its magnetoquasistatic limit for a given $\epsilon$, $\sigma$, and $\omega$.

Question: A toroidal field coil near a stainless-steel vacuum vessel in a magnetic confinement fusion device is driven with a short-duration, nearly monochromatic burst at angular frequency $\omega$. In computational eddy current modeling, it is common to invoke the magnetoquasistatic (MQS) approximation, which neglects displacement current. To assess the validity of this approximation at high frequency, consider a small control volume within the homogeneous, isotropic, nonmagnetic ($\mu \approx \mu_0$) vessel wall where the induced electric field can be treated locally as uniform and time-harmonic. The material has electrical conductivity $\sigma$ and permittivity $\epsilon$, and the electric field is represented by a phasor $\tilde{\mathbf{E}}$ associated with the real field $\mathbf{E}(\mathbf{r},t) = \Re\{\tilde{\mathbf{E}}(\mathbf{r}) \exp(i \omega t)\}$.

Starting only from the Ampère–Maxwell law $\nabla \times \mathbf{H} = \mathbf{J} + \partial \mathbf{D} / \partial t$, the linear constitutive relations $\mathbf{J} = \sigma \mathbf{E}$ and $\mathbf{D} = \epsilon \mathbf{E}$, and the phasor representation for time-harmonic fields, derive an expression for the fractional magnitude error $\delta$ in the source term $\nabla \times \mathbf{H}$ that is incurred when the displacement current $\partial \mathbf{D} / \partial t$ is neglected (i.e., when using the MQS approximation). Then, evaluate $\delta$ for the following parameters representative of a $316\mathrm{L}$ stainless-steel vacuum vessel subjected to a high-frequency coil transient:
- $\epsilon = 8.854\,187\,817 \times 10^{-12}\,\mathrm{F/m}$,
- $\sigma = 1.40 \times 10^{6}\,\mathrm{S/m}$,
- $\omega = 2\pi \times 50 \times 10^{6}\,\mathrm{rad/s}$.

Express the final error as a dimensionless decimal, and round your answer to four significant figures.

Answer: [{"answer": "$$\\boxed{1.987 \\times 10^{-9}}$$", "solution": "The goal is to quantify the error caused by neglecting displacement current in the Ampère–Maxwell law for time-harmonic fields in a homogeneous conductor. The relevant fundamental starting point is the Ampère–Maxwell law,\n$$\n\\nabla \\times \\mathbf{H} = \\mathbf{J} + \\frac{\\partial \\mathbf{D}}{\\partial t},\n$$\ntogether with the linear constitutive relations for an isotropic medium,\n$$\n\\mathbf{J} = \\sigma \\mathbf{E}, \\quad \\mathbf{D} = \\epsilon \\mathbf{E}.\n$$\n\nFor a time-harmonic excitation at angular frequency $\\omega$, write the electric field in phasor form as $\\mathbf{E}(\\mathbf{r},t) = \\Re\\{\\tilde{\\mathbf{E}}(\\mathbf{r}) \\exp(i \\omega t)\\}$. Under this representation, time differentiation corresponds to multiplication by $i \\omega$ in the phasor domain. Therefore, the Ampère–Maxwell law becomes, in phasor form,\n$$\n\\nabla \\times \\tilde{\\mathbf{H}} = \\tilde{\\mathbf{J}} + i \\omega \\tilde{\\mathbf{D}}.\n$$\nUsing the constitutive relations in phasor form, $\\tilde{\\mathbf{J}} = \\sigma \\tilde{\\mathbf{E}}$ and $\\tilde{\\mathbf{D}} = \\epsilon \\tilde{\\mathbf{E}}$, we obtain\n$$\n\\nabla \\times \\tilde{\\mathbf{H}} = \\sigma \\tilde{\\mathbf{E}} + i \\omega \\epsilon \\tilde{\\mathbf{E}} = \\left(\\sigma + i \\omega \\epsilon\\right) \\tilde{\\mathbf{E}}.\n$$\n\nUnder the magnetoquasistatic (MQS) approximation, the displacement current term is neglected, yielding\n$$\n\\left(\\nabla \\times \\tilde{\\mathbf{H}}\\right)_{\\mathrm{MQS}} = \\sigma \\tilde{\\mathbf{E}}.\n$$\n\nWe define the fractional magnitude error $\\delta$ in the source term $\\nabla \\times \\tilde{\\mathbf{H}}$ due to neglecting displacement current as the relative difference between the exact and MQS source magnitudes:\n$$\n\\delta \\equiv \\frac{\\left\\|\\nabla \\times \\tilde{\\mathbf{H}} - \\left(\\nabla \\times \\tilde{\\mathbf{H}}\\right)_{\\mathrm{MQS}}\\right\\|}{\\left\\|\\nabla \\times \\tilde{\\mathbf{H}}\\right\\|}.\n$$\nSubstituting the expressions derived above,\n$$\n\\nabla \\times \\tilde{\\mathbf{H}} - \\left(\\nabla \\times \\tilde{\\mathbf{H}}\\right)_{\\mathrm{MQS}} = \\left(\\sigma + i \\omega \\epsilon\\right)\\tilde{\\mathbf{E}} - \\sigma \\tilde{\\mathbf{E}} = i \\omega \\epsilon \\tilde{\\mathbf{E}}.\n$$\nThus,\n$$\n\\delta = \\frac{\\left\\|i \\omega \\epsilon \\tilde{\\mathbf{E}}\\right\\|}{\\left\\|\\left(\\sigma + i \\omega \\epsilon\\right)\\tilde{\\mathbf{E}}\\right\\|}.\n$$\nSince the magnitude of a complex scalar $a + i b$ is $\\sqrt{a^2 + b^2}$, and factoring out $\\|\\tilde{\\mathbf{E}}\\|$ from numerator and denominator, we find\n$$\n\\delta = \\frac{\\omega \\epsilon}{\\sqrt{\\sigma^2 + \\left(\\omega \\epsilon\\right)^2}}.\n$$\n\nThis is the desired expression, derived from first principles. It depends only on the material parameters $\\sigma$, $\\epsilon$ and the angular frequency $\\omega$, and is independent of the local field amplitude $\\|\\tilde{\\mathbf{E}}\\|$.\n\nWe now evaluate $\\delta$ for the given numerical values:\n- $\\epsilon = 8.854\\,187\\,817 \\times 10^{-12}\\,\\mathrm{F/m}$,\n- $\\sigma = 1.40 \\times 10^{6}\\,\\mathrm{S/m}$,\n- $\\omega = 2\\pi \\times 50 \\times 10^{6}\\,\\mathrm{rad/s}$.\n\nFirst compute $\\omega$:\n$$\n\\omega = 2\\pi \\times 50 \\times 10^{6} = 100\\pi \\times 10^{6} = \\pi \\times 10^{8}\\,\\mathrm{rad/s}.\n$$\nThen compute $\\omega \\epsilon$:\n$$\n\\omega \\epsilon = \\left(\\pi \\times 10^{8}\\right)\\left(8.854\\,187\\,817 \\times 10^{-12}\\right) \\,\\mathrm{S/m} \\approx 2.781\\,625 \\times 10^{-3}\\,\\mathrm{S/m}.\n$$\nCompute the denominator:\n$$\n\\sqrt{\\sigma^2 + \\left(\\omega \\epsilon\\right)^2} = \\sqrt{\\left(1.40 \\times 10^{6}\\right)^2 + \\left(2.781\\,625 \\times 10^{-3}\\right)^2}\\,\\mathrm{S/m}.\n$$\nSince $\\left(2.781\\,625 \\times 10^{-3}\\right)^2 \\approx 7.741 \\times 10^{-6}$ is negligible compared to $\\left(1.40 \\times 10^{6}\\right)^2 = 1.96 \\times 10^{12}$, we have\n$$\n\\sqrt{\\sigma^2 + \\left(\\omega \\epsilon\\right)^2} \\approx 1.40 \\times 10^{6}\\,\\mathrm{S/m}.\n$$\nTherefore,\n$$\n\\delta \\approx \\frac{2.781\\,625 \\times 10^{-3}}{1.40 \\times 10^{6}} = 1.986\\,875 \\times 10^{-9}.\n$$\n\nRounded to four significant figures, the dimensionless fractional error is\n$$\n\\delta \\approx 1.987 \\times 10^{-9}.\n$$"}, {"answer": "$$\n\\boxed{1.987 \\times 10^{-9}}\n$$", "solution": "The problem requires the derivation of the fractional magnitude error, $\\delta$, in the source term of the Ampère–Maxwell law when the magnetoquasistatic (MQS) approximation is applied. This approximation involves neglecting the displacement current. The analysis will be performed in the frequency domain using phasor representation for time-harmonic fields.\n\nThe Ampère–Maxwell law in the time domain is given by:\n$$\n\\nabla \\times \\mathbf{H} = \\mathbf{J} + \\frac{\\partial \\mathbf{D}}{\\partial t}\n$$\nwhere $\\mathbf{H}$ is the magnetic field intensity, $\\mathbf{J}$ is the conduction current density, and $\\mathbf{D}$ is the electric displacement field.\n\nFor a time-harmonic field with angular frequency $\\omega$, any field quantity $\\mathbf{F}(\\mathbf{r}, t)$ can be represented by its complex phasor $\\tilde{\\mathbf{F}}(\\mathbf{r})$ such that $\\mathbf{F}(\\mathbf{r}, t) = \\Re\\{\\tilde{\\mathbf{F}}(\\mathbf{r}) \\exp(i \\omega t)\\}$. In the phasor domain, the time derivative operator $\\partial/\\partial t$ corresponds to multiplication by $i\\omega$. Applying this transformation to the Ampère–Maxwell law yields its phasor form:\n$$\n\\nabla \\times \\tilde{\\mathbf{H}} = \\tilde{\\mathbf{J}} + i\\omega\\tilde{\\mathbf{D}}\n$$\n\nThe problem provides the linear constitutive relations for a homogeneous, isotropic material:\n$$\n\\mathbf{J} = \\sigma \\mathbf{E}\n$$\n$$\n\\mathbf{D} = \\epsilon \\mathbf{E}\n$$\nwhere $\\sigma$ is the electrical conductivity and $\\epsilon$ is the permittivity. In the phasor domain, these relations are:\n$$\n\\tilde{\\mathbf{J}} = \\sigma \\tilde{\\mathbf{E}}\n$$\n$$\n\\tilde{\\mathbf{D}} = \\epsilon \\tilde{\\mathbf{E}}\n$$\n\nSubstituting the phasor constitutive relations into the phasor form of the Ampère–Maxwell law gives:\n$$\n\\nabla \\times \\tilde{\\mathbf{H}} = \\sigma \\tilde{\\mathbf{E}} + i\\omega(\\epsilon \\tilde{\\mathbf{E}})\n$$\nFactoring out the electric field phasor $\\tilde{\\mathbf{E}}$ yields:\n$$\n\\nabla \\times \\tilde{\\mathbf{H}} = (\\sigma + i\\omega\\epsilon) \\tilde{\\mathbf{E}}\n$$\nThe term on the right-hand side is the phasor of the total current density, $\\tilde{\\mathbf{J}}_{\\text{total}}$. It is the sum of the conduction current density phasor, $\\tilde{\\mathbf{J}}_{c} = \\sigma \\tilde{\\mathbf{E}}$, and the displacement current density phasor, $\\tilde{\\mathbf{J}}_{d} = i\\omega\\epsilon \\tilde{\\mathbf{E}}$.\n\nThe MQS approximation consists of neglecting the displacement current, $\\partial \\mathbf{D} / \\partial t$, which is equivalent to neglecting its phasor, $\\tilde{\\mathbf{J}}_{d}$.\nLet $\\tilde{\\mathbf{S}}_{\\text{true}}$ be the true source term phasor and $\\tilde{\\mathbf{S}}_{\\text{MQS}}$ be the approximated source term phasor.\n$$\n\\tilde{\\mathbf{S}}_{\\text{true}} = \\nabla \\times \\tilde{\\mathbf{H}} = (\\sigma + i\\omega\\epsilon) \\tilde{\\mathbf{E}}\n$$\n$$\n\\tilde{\\mathbf{S}}_{\\text{MQS}} = \\sigma \\tilde{\\mathbf{E}}\n$$\nThe error incurred by this approximation is the difference between the true and approximated source terms, which is precisely the neglected displacement current density phasor:\n$$\n\\tilde{\\mathbf{S}}_{\\text{err}} = \\tilde{\\mathbf{S}}_{\\text{true}} - \\tilde{\\mathbf{S}}_{\\text{MQS}} = i\\omega\\epsilon \\tilde{\\mathbf{E}}\n$$\nThe fractional magnitude error, $\\delta$, is defined as the ratio of the magnitude of the error phasor to the magnitude of the true source term phasor:\n$$\n\\delta = \\frac{|\\tilde{\\mathbf{S}}_{\\text{err}}|}{|\\tilde{\\mathbf{S}}_{\\text{true}}|}\n$$\nThe magnitudes of the phasors are calculated as follows:\n$$\n|\\tilde{\\mathbf{S}}_{\\text{err}}| = |i\\omega\\epsilon \\tilde{\\mathbf{E}}| = |i||\\omega||\\epsilon||\\tilde{\\mathbf{E}}| = \\omega\\epsilon|\\tilde{\\mathbf{E}}|\n$$\nsince $\\omega$ and $\\epsilon$ are positive real constants.\n$$\n|\\tilde{\\mathbf{S}}_{\\text{true}}| = |(\\sigma + i\\omega\\epsilon)\\tilde{\\mathbf{E}}| = |\\sigma + i\\omega\\epsilon||\\tilde{\\mathbf{E}}|\n$$\nThe magnitude of the complex number $\\sigma + i\\omega\\epsilon$ is $\\sqrt{\\sigma^2 + (\\omega\\epsilon)^2}$. Therefore:\n$$\n|\\tilde{\\mathbf{S}}_{\\text{true}}| = \\sqrt{\\sigma^2 + (\\omega\\epsilon)^2}|\\tilde{\\mathbf{E}}|\n$$\nSubstituting these magnitudes into the expression for $\\delta$:\n$$\n\\delta = \\frac{\\omega\\epsilon|\\tilde{\\mathbf{E}}|}{\\sqrt{\\sigma^2 + (\\omega\\epsilon)^2}|\\tilde{\\mathbf{E}}|}\n$$\nThe term $|\\tilde{\\mathbf{E}}|$ cancels, yielding the final expression for the fractional magnitude error:\n$$\n\\delta = \\frac{\\omega\\epsilon}{\\sqrt{\\sigma^2 + (\\omega\\epsilon)^2}}\n$$\nNow, we evaluate $\\delta$ using the given parameters:\n- $\\epsilon = 8.854\\,187\\,817 \\times 10^{-12}\\,\\mathrm{F/m}$\n- $\\sigma = 1.40 \\times 10^{6}\\,\\mathrm{S/m}$\n- $\\omega = 2\\pi \\times 50 \\times 10^{6}\\,\\mathrm{rad/s} = 10^8\\pi\\,\\mathrm{rad/s}$\n\nFirst, we compute the product $\\omega\\epsilon$:\n$$\n\\omega\\epsilon = (10^8\\pi\\,\\mathrm{rad/s}) \\times (8.854\\,187\\,817 \\times 10^{-12}\\,\\mathrm{F/m}) \\approx 2.78159 \\times 10^{-3}\\,\\mathrm{S/m}\n$$\nNext, we compare the magnitudes of $\\sigma$ and $\\omega\\epsilon$:\n$$\n\\sigma = 1.40 \\times 10^{6}\\,\\mathrm{S/m}\n$$\n$$\n\\omega\\epsilon \\approx 2.78159 \\times 10^{-3}\\,\\mathrm{S/m}\n$$\nIt is clear that $\\omega\\epsilon \\ll \\sigma$. This implies that the term $(\\omega\\epsilon)^2$ in the denominator of the expression for $\\delta$ will be negligible compared to $\\sigma^2$:\n$$\n(\\omega\\epsilon)^2 \\approx (2.78159 \\times 10^{-3})^2 \\approx 7.737 \\times 10^{-6}\\,(\\mathrm{S/m})^2\n$$\n$$\n\\sigma^2 = (1.40 \\times 10^{6})^2 = 1.96 \\times 10^{12}\\,(\\mathrm{S/m})^2\n$$\nThus, $\\sqrt{\\sigma^2 + (\\omega\\epsilon)^2} \\approx \\sqrt{\\sigma^2} = \\sigma$.\nThe expression for $\\delta$ can be accurately approximated by:\n$$\n\\delta \\approx \\frac{\\omega\\epsilon}{\\sigma}\n$$\nSubstituting the values:\n$$\n\\delta \\approx \\frac{2.78159 \\times 10^{-3}\\,\\mathrm{S/m}}{1.40 \\times 10^{6}\\,\\mathrm{S/m}} \\approx 1.98685 \\times 10^{-9}\n$$\nThe problem requires rounding the final answer to four significant figures.\n$$\n\\delta \\approx 1.987 \\times 10^{-9}\n$$\nThis extremely small value for $\\delta$ confirms that the MQS approximation is highly valid for the given material and frequency, as the magnitude of the neglected displacement current is many orders of magnitude smaller than the magnitude of the conduction current, and consequently also much smaller than the magnitude of the total current."}]</wiki_content3.4>
